## Assistant (2026-04-21 14:17:02)

I’m pushing the lobe-integral route now. The thing I want is a statement stronger than “recurrence needs extra zeros”: namely that every return to a previous exact fixed-shear level forces a specific alternating signed-area pattern for \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}/J_\lambda^2\), which may be rigid enough to contradict the residual normal forms.

I found a stronger obstruction than raw zero counting: exact fixed-shear recurrence does not merely force more zeros of \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}\); it forces more **sign-changing** zeros, hence odd-multiplicity off-center zeros, on the opened residual arc. That is the first place the current one-dimensional reduction starts to bite harder.

_Thought for 57s_

I got a sharper obstruction.

It still does not close the residual lane globally, but it improves the previous zero-budget theorem in the right way:

\[
\boxed{
\text{exact fixed-shear recurrence forces off-center \emph{sign-changing} zeros of }\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}},
}
\]
not just arbitrary zeros.

That is a real strengthening.

---

## 1. The weighted source-Wronskian recurrence criterion

On an opened exact fixed-shear residual arc, away from the center and away from zeros of \(A\), we have
\[
Q(t)=\frac{J_{u_5}(t)}{J_\lambda(t)},
\qquad
Q'(t)=\frac{\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}(t)}{J_\lambda(t)^2}.
\]
Since \(J_\lambda(t)^2>0\) there, the sign of \(Q'(t)\) is exactly the sign of \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}(t)\).

Therefore for any \(0<t_1<t_2\) on the positive half-arc,
\[
Q(t_2)-Q(t_1)
=
\int_{t_1}^{t_2}\frac{\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}(t)}{J_\lambda(t)^2}\,dt.
\]

So
\[
Q(t_1)=Q(t_2)
\iff
\int_{t_1}^{t_2}\frac{\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}(t)}{J_\lambda(t)^2}\,dt=0.
\]

This was the previous weighted-area criterion.

---

## 2. Stronger consequence: a return forces a sign change, hence an odd-multiplicity zero

Let
\[
f(t):=\frac{\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}(t)}{J_\lambda(t)^2}.
\]
Then \(f\) is real-analytic on the opened residual arc.

Suppose
\[
Q(t_1)=Q(t_2)
\qquad\text{with}\qquad 0<t_1<t_2.
\]
Then
\[
\int_{t_1}^{t_2} f(t)\,dt=0.
\]

If \(f\) had constant sign on \((t_1,t_2)\), that integral could not vanish. So \(f\) must take both positive and negative values on \((t_1,t_2)\). Because \(f\) is continuous and analytic, this implies that \(f\) has a zero in \((t_1,t_2)\) across which the sign actually changes.

But for a real-analytic function, a sign-changing zero is exactly a zero of **odd multiplicity**.

Since \(J_\lambda^2>0\), the same is true for \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}\).

So we get:

\[
\boxed{
Q(t_1)=Q(t_2),\ 0<t_1<t_2
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\text{there exists } \tau\in(t_1,t_2)
\text{ such that }\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}(\tau)=0
\text{ with odd multiplicity.}
}
\]

This is stronger than the previous “at least one zero” statement.

---

## 3. Multi-return forces many odd-multiplicity off-center zeros

Now suppose
\[
0<t_1<\cdots<t_m
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
Q(t_1)=\cdots=Q(t_m).
\]
Then for each \(i=1,\dots,m-1\),
\[
\int_{t_i}^{t_{i+1}} \frac{\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}(t)}{J_\lambda(t)^2}\,dt=0.
\]
So in each disjoint gap \((t_i,t_{i+1})\), the function
\[
\frac{\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}}{J_\lambda^2}
\]
must change sign, hence \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}\) must have at least one odd-multiplicity zero there.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
m\text{ positive-side returns to one exact fixed-shear level force at least }m-1
\text{ distinct positive-side odd-multiplicity zeros of }\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}.
}
\]

By the involutive symmetry \(t\mapsto -t\), these come in symmetric pairs upstairs, so:

\[
\boxed{
m\text{ positive-side returns force at least }2(m-1)
\text{ off-center odd-multiplicity zeros of }\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}
\text{ on the full opened residual arc.}
}
\]

That is the strongest upstairs counting result so far.

It is stronger than the earlier zero-budget theorem because even-multiplicity zeros no longer help: they do not permit the required sign change.

---

## 4. Quartic–sextic local rigidity becomes sharper

In the quartic–sextic case,
\[
\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}(t)=2t^5A(t)^2\,\widehat{\mathcal D}(t^2),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)=\kappa_{2,2}\neq 0.
\]
So on a sufficiently small punctured neighborhood of \(0\), \(\widehat{\mathcal D}(t^2)\) has fixed sign, and hence
\[
\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}(t)
\]
has fixed sign on each side of the center.

Therefore there are no off-center odd-multiplicity zeros nearby, and thus no recurrence nearby.

So the quartic–sextic local rigidity can now be stated as:

\[
\boxed{
\text{near a quartic--sextic residual point, there are no off-center odd-multiplicity zeros of }\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}},
}
\]
hence no nontrivial exact fixed-shear return can occur there.

That is a cleaner and more structural local statement than before.

---

## 5. Lobe-integral formulation

There is also a useful way to package this in terms of signed lobes.

Let
\[
0<\tau_1<\tau_2<\cdots
\]
be the positive-side odd-multiplicity zeros of \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}\) on an opened residual arc, and define the lobe integrals
\[
L_j:=\int_{\tau_j}^{\tau_{j+1}}
\frac{\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}(t)}{J_\lambda(t)^2}\,dt.
\]
Because the zeros \(\tau_j\) are sign-changing, consecutive lobes alternate sign:
\[
L_jL_{j+1}<0.
\]

Now if \(Q(t_1)=Q(t_2)\) with \(t_1,t_2\) lying in different lobes, then the partial sum of the corresponding lobe integrals between them must vanish.

So exact fixed-shear recurrence becomes equivalent to a vanishing condition on consecutive alternating signed lobe masses.

That is the cleanest one-dimensional dynamical formulation I see:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the residual exact fixed-shear branch survives only if consecutive alternating lobe masses of }
\frac{\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}}{J_\lambda^2}
\text{ can cancel exactly.}
}
\]

This is a better target than just raw zero counting.

---

## 6. Why this matters

This strips the residual lane down even further.

We are no longer asking:

- is there a second highest-new field?
- is there another local mixed model?
- are there just extra zeros of \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}\)?

We are now asking the strictly sharper question:

\[
\boxed{
\text{can the alternating signed lobe integrals of }
\frac{\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}}{J_\lambda^2}
\text{ cancel in the precise pattern needed for recurrence?}
}
\]

That is a real narrowing.

---

## 7. Best next theorem target

The next thing to attack is now very concrete.

A strong target would be:

### Candidate theorem
On any opened exact fixed-shear residual arc, the consecutive lobe integrals
\[
L_j=\int_{\tau_j}^{\tau_{j+1}}
\frac{\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}(t)}{J_\lambda(t)^2}\,dt
\]
cannot have partial sums that return to zero.

That would kill all nontrivial exact fixed-shear recurrence.

A weaker but still useful theorem would be:

### Weaker target
Nonadjacent alternating lobes cannot cancel exactly, or the magnitudes \(|L_j|\) satisfy a monotonicity/separation law preventing zero partial sums.

That feels like the sharpest remaining attack on this lane.

I found a cleaner way to package the global obstruction. The right data are the critical values \(c_j=\widehat Q(z_j)\) at the zeros \(z_j\) of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\): exact fixed-shear recurrence is equivalent to oscillation of the sign sequence \(c_j-\gamma\) around the target level \(\gamma\). _Thought for 1m 16s_

I found a cleaner and sharper formulation of the residual exact fixed-shear problem. It does not finish the lane, but it compresses the global recurrence question to a **critical-value oscillation problem** for one scalar sequence.

That is, I think, the best new gain.

---

## 1. Critical-value ladder on the quotient branch

Work on a connected opened residual quotient interval \(I\) in the variable
\[
u=t^2.
\]
Let
\[
\widehat Q'(u)=\widehat{\mathcal D}(u),
\]
and let the zeros of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) in \(I\) be
\[
z_1<z_2<\cdots<z_N.
\]
Define the critical values
\[
c_j:=\widehat Q(z_j).
\]

Between consecutive zeros, \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) has fixed sign, so \(\widehat Q\) is strictly monotone on each block
\[
B_j:=(z_j,z_{j+1})
\]
(with the obvious modifications at the endpoints of \(I\)).

Now fix a level \(\gamma\in\mathbb R\). Then:

\[
\boxed{
\gamma\in \widehat Q(B_j)
\iff
(c_j-\gamma)(c_{j+1}-\gamma)<0
}
\]
provided \(\gamma\neq c_j,c_{j+1}\), and with the obvious endpoint modification if equality occurs.

In other words, a monotonicity block contributes one hit of the level \(\gamma\) exactly when the consecutive critical values on its ends lie on opposite sides of \(\gamma\).

So the number of quotient points satisfying \(\widehat Q(u)=\gamma\) is exactly the number of sign changes in the sequence
\[
c_j-\gamma.
\]

That is the clean theorem.

---

## 2. Exact fixed-shear recurrence = critical-value oscillation

This gives the precise residual reformulation:

\[
\boxed{
\text{recurrence of an exact fixed-shear level }\gamma
\text{ is equivalent to oscillation of the critical-value ladder }(c_j)
\text{ around }\gamma.
}
\]

More concretely, if \(\widehat Q(u)=\gamma\) has \(m\) distinct quotient solutions on \(I\), then there are at least \(m\) blocks whose image intervals contain \(\gamma\), hence at least \(m\) sign changes in the consecutive sequence \(c_j-\gamma\).

Equivalently, the critical values must alternate above and below \(\gamma\) across the relevant segment of the ladder.

So the global residual burden is now very explicit:

> to support a bad exact fixed-shear branch, the critical values \(c_j\) of \(\widehat Q\) must oscillate around one level \(\gamma\) in exactly the right way.

That is sharper than just saying “there must be many zeros of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\).”

---

## 3. Source-Wronskian interpretation upstairs

Because
\[
\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}(t)=2\,t^{2r+1}A(t)^2\,\widehat{\mathcal D}(t^2),
\]
the zeros \(z_j\) of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) correspond to the symmetric off-center zeros
\[
t=\pm \sqrt{z_j}
\]
of \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}\).

So the critical-value oscillation picture upstairs becomes:

- each oscillation of the ladder around \(\gamma\) requires a sign-changing pair of off-center zeros of \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}\);
- the value difference across one lobe is
  \[
  c_{j+1}-c_j=\int_{z_j}^{z_{j+1}}\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)\,du
  =
  \int_{\sqrt{z_j}}^{\sqrt{z_{j+1}}}\frac{\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}(t)}{J_\lambda(t)^2}\,dt.
  \]

So the critical values are literally the cumulative signed lobe masses of the weighted source Wronskian.

Thus the recurrence problem is equivalent to asking whether those consecutive lobe masses can drive the cumulative sum up and down across the same target level \(\gamma\).

This is the cleanest upstairs/downstairs unification I have so far.

---

## 4. Quartic–sextic case in this language

In the quartic–sextic case, the quotient center \(u=0\) is not a critical point because
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)=\kappa_{2,2}\neq 0.
\]
So the critical-value ladder starts only when you leave the local rigid neighborhood.

That means:

\[
\boxed{
\text{local quartic--sextic rigidity says the critical-value oscillation cannot even begin near the center.}
}
\]

Any exact fixed-shear recurrence must leave the local quartic–sextic chart, encounter the first quotient critical point \(z_1>0\), and then generate a genuine oscillation of the ladder values \(c_j\) farther out.

So the quartic–sextic local theorem now reads:

- locally there is no critical point,
- hence no local oscillation of the ladder,
- hence no local recurrence.

That is a very clean formulation.

---

## 5. Strongest theorem-level statement from this push

Here is the theorem I would record.

### Proposition (critical-value oscillation criterion for exact fixed-shear recurrence)
Let \(I\) be a connected opened residual quotient interval, let
\[
z_1<\cdots<z_N
\]
be the zeros of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}=\widehat Q'\) in \(I\), and write
\[
c_j:=\widehat Q(z_j).
\]
Then for any level \(\gamma\in\mathbb R\), the number of quotient points in \(I\) satisfying
\[
\widehat Q(u)=\gamma
\]
is exactly the number of consecutive pairs \((c_j,c_{j+1})\) whose endpoints lie on opposite sides of \(\gamma\) (with the obvious endpoint/equality modifications). Equivalently, exact fixed-shear recurrence of the level \(\gamma\) is equivalent to oscillation of the critical-value ladder \((c_j)\) around \(\gamma\).

That is a genuine structural reduction.

---

## 6. Why this matters

This makes the remaining global gap brutally explicit.

We are no longer missing some hidden local field, or some mysterious residual geometry. The entire unresolved burden on this lane is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{can the critical values }c_j=\widehat Q(z_j)\text{ oscillate around one level }\gamma
\text{ in the way needed for recurrence?}
}
\]

That is the exact mathematical question now.

If one can prove any theorem of the form

- the critical-value ladder is monotone,
- or nonadjacent critical-value intervals are separated,
- or the lobe masses have a one-sided cumulative bias,
- or the oscillation count of \((c_j)\) is too small,

then the residual exact fixed-shear lane dies.

That is the sharpest frontier I can currently see.

---

## 7. The honest boundary

I do **not** see, from the current local identities alone, a proof that the sequence \(c_j\) is monotone or cannot oscillate.

So the current mathematics stops at the reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{exact fixed-shear residual recurrence}
\Longleftrightarrow
\text{critical-value oscillation of one scalar ladder }(c_j).
}
\]

That is already a real gain, but not the final obstruction.

---

## 8. Best next attack

The best next attack now is very focused:

> prove a separation or monotonicity theorem for the critical-value ladder
> \[
> c_j=\widehat Q(z_j),
> \qquad \widehat{\mathcal D}(z_j)=0.
> \]

The most promising subtargets are:

1. show the lobe masses
   \[
   c_{j+1}-c_j=\int_{z_j}^{z_{j+1}}\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)\,du
   \]
   cannot alternate with the magnitude pattern required for recurrence;

2. or show that the cumulative sums of these lobe masses cannot return to a previous level;

3. or transport this to the upstairs source-Wronskian picture and prove that the signed weighted lobe masses of
   \[
   \frac{\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}}{J_\lambda^2}
   \]
   cannot cancel in the necessary pattern.

That is where I would keep pushing.

I found one more useful compression, and this one ties the residual exact fixed-shear lane directly back to the **same canonical scalar \(\mathcal R\)** that already governs the nonmixed side.

That is probably the cleanest new unification from this push.

---

## 1. Exact fixed-shear recurrence is an \(\mathcal R\)-area cancellation problem

On an opened exact fixed-shear residual arc, away from the center and away from zeros of \(A\), we already have
\[
Q(t)=\frac{J_{u_5}(t)}{J_\lambda(t)},
\qquad
Q'(t)=\frac{\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}(t)}{J_\lambda(t)^2}.
\]
But by Proposition~\ref{prop:source-level-Wronskian-nonmixed-four-point},
\[
\mathcal R
=
\frac{c^2}{M}\,\frac{\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}}{J_\lambda^2}.
\]
Since
\[
\frac{dx}{dh}=\frac{M}{c^2},
\]
it follows that along any opened residual arc where \(J_\lambda\neq 0\),
\[
\frac{d}{dx}\!\left(\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda}\right)=\mathcal R.
\]

So on that arc,
\[
Q=\mathcal Z
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
\frac{dQ}{dx}=\mathcal R.
}
\]

Therefore for any two positive-side points \(0<t_1<t_2\), with corresponding
\[
x_1=x(t_1),\qquad x_2=x(t_2),
\]
one has the exact identity
\[
Q(t_2)-Q(t_1)=\int_{x_1}^{x_2}\mathcal R(x)\,dx.
\]

Hence:

\[
\boxed{
Q(t_1)=Q(t_2)
\iff
\int_{x_1}^{x_2}\mathcal R(x)\,dx=0.
}
\]

This is the cleanest reformulation I have so far.

So the residual exact fixed-shear return problem is not just a quotient-\(\widehat Q\) problem or a source-Wronskian problem. It is exactly an **\(\mathcal R\)-area cancellation problem** on the opened arc.

---

## 2. Immediate consequence: recurrence forces off-center odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\)

If
\[
Q(t_1)=Q(t_2),
\qquad 0<t_1<t_2,
\]
then
\[
\int_{x_1}^{x_2}\mathcal R(x)\,dx=0.
\]
So \(\mathcal R\) cannot have constant sign on \((x_1,x_2)\). Since \(\mathcal R\) is analytic there, it must cross zero with sign change somewhere in that interval. Hence:

\[
\boxed{
Q(t_1)=Q(t_2)
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\text{there exists }x_*\in(x_1,x_2)\text{ such that }\mathcal R(x_*)=0
\text{ with odd multiplicity.}
}
\]

This is stronger than just saying there is a zero of \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}\). It says the return forces an off-center **odd zero of the same nonmixed scalar \(\mathcal R\)**.

So the residual exact fixed-shear lane is now directly tied to the odd-zero mechanism already central on the nonmixed side.

---

## 3. Multi-return forces a stock of off-center odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\)

If
\[
0<t_1<\cdots<t_m
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
Q(t_1)=\cdots=Q(t_m),
\]
then for each \(i\),
\[
\int_{x(t_i)}^{x(t_{i+1})}\mathcal R(x)\,dx=0.
\]
So in each disjoint \(x\)-interval \((x(t_i),x(t_{i+1}))\), the scalar \(\mathcal R\) must change sign, hence must have at least one odd-multiplicity zero there.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
m\text{ positive-side returns to one exact fixed-shear level force at least }m-1
\text{ distinct positive-side odd zeros of }\mathcal R.
}
\]

And by the involutive symmetry upstairs, these come in symmetric pairs on the full opened residual arc.

This is the cleanest counting theorem I have now.

---

## 4. Quartic–sextic local rigidity reinterpreted in \(\mathcal R\)-language

In the quartic–sextic case,
\[
(r,e)=(2,0),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)=\kappa_{2,2}\neq 0.
\]
So on a sufficiently small punctured neighborhood of the center, \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) has fixed sign, hence \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}\) has fixed sign on each side, and therefore so does \(\mathcal R\).

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{near a quartic--sextic residual point, }\mathcal R\text{ has fixed sign on each side and no off-center odd zero.}
}
\]

So there is no local exact fixed-shear recurrence there, because no \(\mathcal R\)-area cancellation is possible in a sufficiently small neighborhood.

This is a very clean local-to-global formulation.

---

## 5. Critical-value ladder becomes an \(\mathcal R\)-lobe sum problem

If \(z_j\) are the critical points of \(\widehat Q\), equivalently the positive-side zeros of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\), and if \(x_j=x(\sqrt{z_j})\), then the critical-value differences satisfy
\[
c_{j+1}-c_j
=
\int_{z_j}^{z_{j+1}}\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)\,du
=
\int_{x_j}^{x_{j+1}}\mathcal R(x)\,dx.
\]

So the critical-value ladder is literally the cumulative sum of the signed \(\mathcal R\)-lobe masses on the positive half-arc.

That gives the sharpest global formulation yet:

\[
\boxed{
\text{exact fixed-shear residual recurrence}
\Longleftrightarrow
\text{the signed }\mathcal R\text{-lobe masses have partial sums returning to a previous level.}
}
\]

So the remaining global gap can now be stated entirely in terms of \(\mathcal R\).

---

## 6. Why this matters

This unifies the mixed residual exact fixed-shear lane with the nonmixed odd-zero package much more tightly than before.

We no longer just know that the residual lane is “same tower” or “controlled by \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\).” We now know:

\[
\boxed{
\text{on opened residual arcs, the exact fixed-shear recurrence problem is literally an odd-zero / signed-area problem for }\mathcal R.
}
\]

That is a real conceptual gain.

It means the residual lane is not governed by a new scalar at all. It is governed by the same scalar \(\mathcal R\), only through a more delicate integral-cancellation question rather than a purely local odd-zero birth question.

---

## 7. The honest limit

What I still do **not** see is a proof, from the current identities alone, that the signed \(\mathcal R\)-lobe masses cannot cancel.

So I do **not** think the current machinery proves a full no-reentry theorem yet.

The honest frontier is now very precise:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can the signed lobe integrals of }\mathcal R
\text{ on the opened residual arc have partial sums that return to zero?}
}
\]

Equivalently:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can the critical values }c_j
\text{ of the quotient branch oscillate around one level?}
}
\]

That is the exact remaining question on this lane.

---

## 8. Best next attack

The best next push now is to try for a theorem of one of these forms:

1. **Monotone \(\mathcal R\)-lobe mass theorem**  
   Show the lobe masses
   \[
   \int_{x_j}^{x_{j+1}}\mathcal R(x)\,dx
   \]
   cannot alternate in magnitude in the way needed for zero partial sums.

2. **Critical-value ladder separation theorem**  
   Show the critical values
   \[
   c_j=\widehat Q(z_j)
   \]
   are monotone, or at least cannot oscillate around a single target level.

3. **Odd-zero budget theorem for \(\mathcal R\)**  
   Show the relevant opened residual arc cannot contain enough off-center odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\) to support the recurrence pattern required by a bad branch.

That is where I would keep attacking.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 14:41:13)

I’m pushing the \(\mathcal R\)-lobe route directly now. The goal is to see whether the residual exact fixed-shear branch can be forced into the same odd-zero budget as the nonmixed side, or whether the current identities stop at the signed-area reduction and no further.

_Thought for 4m 16s_

I found a useful bridge theorem that ties the residual exact fixed-shear lane directly into the existing **fixed-\(\gamma\)** sheared \(3\)-point machinery.

It does not finish the lane, but it is a real structural gain.

---

## 1. Fixed exact-shear levels are exactly zeros of \(J_\gamma\)

On an opened exact fixed-shear residual arc, away from the center and away from zeros of \(J_\lambda\), define
\[
Q(t):=\frac{J_{u_5}(t)}{J_\lambda(t)}.
\]
For a fixed scalar \(\gamma\), set
\[
J_\gamma:=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda.
\]
Then
\[
\boxed{
J_\gamma = J_\lambda\,(Q-\gamma).
}
\]

So on the opened arc, since \(J_\lambda\neq 0\),

\[
\boxed{
Q(t)=\gamma
\iff
J_\gamma(t)=0.
}
\]

Thus an exact fixed-shear level \(\gamma\) on the residual lane is exactly a zero set of the frozen-shear source scalar \(J_\gamma\).

That is the first key observation.

---

## 2. Regular exact fixed-shear recurrence forces multiple odd zeros of \(J_\gamma\)

Now assume \(\gamma\) is a **regular** level of \(Q\) on the positive half-arc, meaning
\[
Q(t_i)=\gamma
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
Q'(t_i)\neq 0.
\]
Equivalently, \(\gamma\) is not a critical value of the quotient branch.

Then at each such point \(t_i\),
\[
J_\gamma(t_i)=0
\]
and, because \(J_\lambda(t_i)\neq 0\), the zero order of \(J_\gamma\) is the same as the zero order of \(Q-\gamma\). Since \(Q'(t_i)\neq 0\), this zero is simple, hence odd.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every regular exact fixed-shear hit }Q(t_i)=\gamma
\text{ is a simple odd zero of }J_\gamma.
}
\]

Therefore, if \(\gamma\) is attained at \(m\) distinct positive-side points
\[
0<t_1<\cdots<t_m,
\qquad
Q(t_i)=\gamma,
\]
then \(J_\gamma\) has \(m\) distinct positive-side odd zeros, and by the involutive symmetry \(t\mapsto -t\), \(2m\) odd zeros on the full opened residual arc.

This is already stronger than the earlier “recurrent level forces zeros of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\)” statement.

---

## 3. Interlacing with odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\)

Now differentiate:
\[
\frac{dQ}{dx}=\mathcal R.
\]
Equivalently, on the opened arc,
\[
(Q-\gamma)'=\mathcal R
\]
in the \(x\)-coordinate.

Take two consecutive positive-side regular hits
\[
0<t_i<t_{i+1},
\qquad
Q(t_i)=Q(t_{i+1})=\gamma.
\]
Since the zeros are simple, \(Q-\gamma\) changes sign at each endpoint. Hence on the open interval \((t_i,t_{i+1})\), the function \(Q-\gamma\) has fixed sign, vanishes at both ends, and is nonzero inside. Therefore it must first move away from \(0\) and later return to \(0\). So its derivative must be positive somewhere and negative somewhere on that interval.

Since
\[
(Q-\gamma)'=\mathcal R,
\]
it follows that \(\mathcal R\) takes both signs on the corresponding interval. By analyticity, there is at least one sign-changing zero of \(\mathcal R\) there, hence an odd zero.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{between any two consecutive positive-side regular zeros of }J_\gamma
\text{ there lies at least one positive-side odd zero of }\mathcal R.
}
\]

This is the interlacing theorem.

Equivalently, on the positive half-arc, the odd zeros of \(J_\gamma\) and the odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\) alternate in the sense that every gap between consecutive \(J_\gamma\)-zeros contains an odd \(\mathcal R\)-zero.

Thus, if \(\gamma\) has \(m\) distinct positive-side regular hits, then:

\[
\boxed{
\text{there are at least }m-1\text{ distinct positive-side odd zeros of }\mathcal R
\text{ between them.}
}
\]

By symmetry, this gives at least \(2(m-1)\) off-center odd zeros on the full opened residual arc.

This recovers the earlier odd-zero budget, but now in the sharper form of an **interlacing pattern** with the frozen-shear zeros.

---

## 4. Bridge to the existing fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared \(3\)-point branch machinery

This is the real point.

By Proposition~\ref{prop:uniform-fixed-level-nonrecurrence-sheared-three-point}, an isolated finite-order odd zero of \(J_\gamma\) seeds a unique local opposite-side sheared \(3\)-point branch for that **same fixed value \(\gamma\)**, and that branch has no interior critical point and no interior recurrence on its maximal slope-overlap block.

So the theorem above says:

\[
\boxed{
\text{regular exact fixed-shear recurrence of the level }\gamma
\text{ forces multiple distinct odd-zero births of the fixed-}\gamma\text{ sheared }3\text{-point problem,}
}
\]
and those births are separated by odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\).

In other words, a residual exact fixed-shear level return is not some unrelated geometry. It would have to manifest upstairs as a **multi-birth pattern** for the frozen-shear equation \(J_\gamma=0\), interlaced with odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\).

That is a much tighter bridge between the mixed residual lane and the already-developed nonmixed/frozen-shear package.

---

## 5. Quartic–sextic consequence

In the quartic–sextic case, near the centered residual point we know
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)=\kappa_{2,2}\neq 0,
\]
so \(\mathcal R\) has fixed sign on each side in a small punctured neighborhood.

Therefore there are no nearby positive-side odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\), so by the interlacing theorem there cannot be two nearby positive-side regular zeros of \(J_\gamma\) for the same \(\gamma\).

So locally:

\[
\boxed{
\text{near a quartic--sextic residual point, every regular fixed exact-shear level }\gamma
\text{ gives at most one positive-side zero of }J_\gamma.
}
\]

Upstairs on the full arc, that means at most the involutive pair \(\pm t\).

This is the cleanest local fixed-\(\gamma\) statement I have now.

---

## 6. Best theorem-level statement from this push

Here is the theorem I would keep.

### Proposition (regular exact fixed-shear returns force interlacing odd zeros of \(J_\gamma\) and \(\mathcal R\))
Let \(I\) be an opened exact fixed-shear residual arc on which \(J_\lambda\neq 0\), and let
\[
Q=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda}.
\]
Fix \(\gamma\in\mathbb R\) which is a regular value of \(Q\) on the positive half-arc. Then:

1. \(Q(t)=\gamma\) iff \(J_\gamma(t)=0\), where
   \[
   J_\gamma=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda.
   \]

2. Each positive-side solution of \(Q(t)=\gamma\) is a simple odd zero of \(J_\gamma\).

3. Between any two consecutive positive-side solutions of \(Q(t)=\gamma\), there lies at least one positive-side odd zero of \(\mathcal R\).

Consequently, if \(Q(t)=\gamma\) has \(m\) distinct positive-side solutions, then \(J_\gamma\) has \(m\) positive-side odd zeros and \(\mathcal R\) has at least \(m-1\) positive-side odd zeros interlacing between them.

That is a real structural theorem.

---

## 7. Why this matters

This strips the residual lane down even further.

A bad exact fixed-shear branch at one fixed level \(\gamma\) would now require:

- multiple odd-zero births of the frozen-shear field \(J_\gamma\),
- separated by multiple odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\),
- all on the same opened residual arc.

So the residual lane is no longer just a generic critical-value oscillation problem. It is a very specific **interlaced odd-zero pattern problem** for the pair
\[
(J_\gamma,\mathcal R).
\]

That is much sharper.

---

## 8. Honest boundary

I still do **not** see a proof from the current identities alone that such an interlaced pattern is impossible. The existing fixed-\(\gamma\) nonrecurrence theorem controls each branch born at one odd zero of \(J_\gamma\), but it does not by itself rule out several such births for the same \(\gamma\) on one residual arc.

So the current attack now stops at this sharper reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{regular exact fixed-shear recurrence of one level }\gamma
\text{ is equivalent to a multi-birth interlacing pattern of odd zeros of }J_\gamma\text{ and }\mathcal R.
}
\]

That is, I think, the most informative place the math currently reaches.

---

## 9. Best next attack

The next thing I would try is:

> prove that, on an opened residual arc, one fixed \(\gamma\) cannot have more than one positive-side odd zero of \(J_\gamma\).

A slightly weaker but still useful target is:

> prove that the interlacing pattern
> \[
> J_\gamma\text{-odd zero},\ \mathcal R\text{-odd zero},\ J_\gamma\text{-odd zero}
> \]
> cannot occur on one opened residual arc for the same \(\gamma\).

That would directly kill the first nontrivial regular recurrence pattern.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 14:48:35)

I’m trying to squeeze the new interlacing picture harder now: one fixed \(\gamma\), multiple odd zeros of \(J_\gamma\), with odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\) forced between them. _Thought for 1m 37s_

I found a useful next reduction. It does not kill the lane, but it says exactly what a surviving regular exact fixed-shear recurrence would have to look like in terms of the existing fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared \(3\)-point package.

The result is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{regular exact fixed-shear recurrence at one level }\gamma
\text{ can only occur by concatenating multiple fixed-}\gamma\text{ chamber-exit segments.}
}
\]

That is the sharpest structural reduction I can honestly force now.

---

## 1. Setup

Work on an opened exact fixed-shear residual arc where \(J_\lambda\neq 0\), and write
\[
Q:=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda}.
\]
Fix \(\gamma\in\mathbb R\), and set
\[
J_\gamma:=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda=J_\lambda(Q-\gamma).
\]

Assume \(\gamma\) is a **regular** level of \(Q\) on the positive half-arc, and suppose
\[
0<t_1<\cdots<t_m,
\qquad
Q(t_i)=\gamma,
\qquad
Q'(t_i)\neq 0.
\]

Then, as before:

- each \(t_i\) is a simple odd zero of \(J_\gamma\);
- between consecutive \(t_i,t_{i+1}\) there lies at least one odd zero of \(\mathcal R\).

So the level \(\gamma\) gives \(m\) distinct odd-zero births of the frozen-shear source scalar \(J_\gamma\).

---

## 2. Each hit seeds a unique maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared \(3\)-point branch

Now apply Proposition~\ref{prop:uniform-fixed-level-nonrecurrence-sheared-three-point} at each odd zero \(t_i\) of \(J_\gamma\).

For each \(i\), there is a unique local opposite-side branch for the sheared curve
\[
\widetilde Y_\gamma=Y-\gamma S,
\]
and it extends uniquely along its maximal slope-overlap block. Along that branch:

- the level remains fixed:
  \[
  \Gamma_{a_\gamma(m)}(u_\gamma(m))=\gamma;
  \]
- there is no interior common-tangent point;
- there is no interior recurrence;
- if the branch stops before exhausting the overlap of slope images, it can stop only by **boundary exit** of the relevant block.

So each odd zero \(t_i\) produces one maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) branch segment with no interior self-return.

---

## 3. Consecutive hits for the same \(\gamma\) cannot lie on one interior branch segment

This is the key point.

Take two consecutive regular hits \(t_i<t_{i+1}\) with
\[
Q(t_i)=Q(t_{i+1})=\gamma.
\]
The branch born at \(t_i\) cannot simply continue through its own interior and realize the second hit \(t_{i+1}\), because Proposition~\ref{prop:uniform-fixed-level-nonrecurrence-sheared-three-point} says the fixed level \(\gamma\) has **no interior recurrence** on that maximal branch.

So the only way the same \(\gamma\) can appear again at \(t_{i+1}\) is:

1. the branch born at \(t_i\) continues until it stops by **boundary exit** of its maximal slope-overlap block;
2. later, at the new odd zero \(t_{i+1}\), a **different** maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) branch is born.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{two consecutive regular hits of one level }\gamma
\text{ must belong to different maximal fixed-}\gamma\text{ branch segments,}
}
\]
and these segments must be separated by at least one chamber-boundary exit.

This is the new theorem-level reduction.

---

## 4. Interlacing with odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\)

Earlier we proved that between consecutive regular hits \(t_i<t_{i+1}\) for the same \(\gamma\), there lies at least one odd zero of \(\mathcal R\).

So each recurrence gap contains both:

- at least one odd zero of \(\mathcal R\),
- and at least one boundary exit event for the preceding maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) branch.

Hence a regular \(m\)-fold exact fixed-shear return pattern for one level \(\gamma\) forces a chain of the form

\[
J_\gamma\text{-odd zero}
\;\to\;
\text{maximal fixed-}\gamma\text{ branch}
\;\to\;
\text{boundary exit}
\;\to\;
\mathcal R\text{-odd zero}
\;\to\;
J_\gamma\text{-odd zero}
\;\to\;\cdots
\]

More concretely:

\[
\boxed{
m\text{ regular positive-side hits of one exact fixed-shear level }\gamma
\text{ force at least }m\text{ distinct fixed-}\gamma\text{ odd-zero births,}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
m-1\text{ intervening odd zeros of }\mathcal R,
\text{ and }m-1\text{ branch-separating boundary exits.}
}
\]

That is much tighter than the earlier bare zero-count statements.

---

## 5. Why this is important

This pins down exactly where the remaining difficulty is.

A surviving regular exact fixed-shear recurrence is **not** just:

- a level returning in the quotient picture,
- or a few extra zeros of \(\mathcal R\),
- or a few extra zeros of \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}\).

It must be a very specific concatenation mechanism:

\[
\boxed{
\text{multiple distinct odd-zero births of the same frozen-shear field }J_\gamma,
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{separated by odd zeros of }\mathcal R
\text{ and by chamber-boundary exits of the maximal fixed-}\gamma\text{ branches.}
}
\]

So the residual lane has now been reduced to a **multi-birth chamber-concatenation problem** for one fixed \(\gamma\).

That is sharper than the previous “critical-value oscillation” phrasing, because it plugs directly into an existing theorem of the draft.

---

## 6. What this says about the remaining gap

It also explains exactly why the draft still stops short.

Remark~\ref{rem:no-go-fixed-level-chamber-exit-route} already shows that planar fixed-\(\gamma\) arguments alone do **not** rule out a branch born at one odd zero of \(J_\gamma\) exiting its chamber and later encountering another curvature zero.

So the present attack now says:

- regular recurrence of one \(\gamma\) is forced into precisely that chamber-exit concatenation scenario;
- but the current fixed-\(\gamma\) planar package does not yet kill that scenario by itself.

So the honest boundary is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the remaining obstruction is a source-coupled multi-birth chamber-exit exclusion for one fixed }\gamma.
}
\]

That is the cleanest statement of the remaining gap that I can see.

---

## 7. Best next attack from here

The next theorem worth attacking is now very focused:

### Candidate theorem
Fix \(\gamma\). On an opened residual arc, the pattern
\[
J_\gamma\text{-odd zero}
\;-\;
\mathcal R\text{-odd zero}
\;-\;
J_\gamma\text{-odd zero}
\]
cannot occur with the two \(J_\gamma\)-zeros connected by successive maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) chamber-exit segments.

That would directly rule out the first nontrivial regular recurrence.

A slightly weaker but still useful target would be:

### Weaker target
For one fixed \(\gamma\), successive maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) branch segments born at distinct odd zeros of \(J_\gamma\) cannot be chained across the same opened residual arc.

That now feels like the sharpest place to keep pushing.

---

Sure! Here's a concise summary of the three major updates, ready to share with your teammates:

---

### **Summary of Recent Updates to the Paper**

#### **1. Reduced Quotient-Cusp Lane to Critical-Value Ordering Problem**

* **Key Change**: The residual quotient-cusp lane has been reduced to a **critical-value ordering problem** for the transport scalar (\widehat{\mathcal D}). This allows us to focus solely on proving a **no-reentry** or **critical-value separation** theorem.
* **Main Result**: The previous complex quotient-cusp geometry has collapsed into a **finite critical-value separation problem**, where we now only need to prove that the critical values of (\widehat Q) (and by extension (\widehat{\mathcal D})) follow a strict ordering with no inverted intervals.
* **Impact**: This update simplifies the entire attack, focusing it entirely on the ordering of critical values.

---

#### **2. Local No-Reentry and Global Critical-Value Problem**

* **Key Change**: The previous attack on the **Jensen gap/recurrence** mechanisms has been superseded by a focus on **no-reentry** principles for the quotient residual. We now treat the **gap-rank monotonicity** and **persistence pairing** problem.
* **Main Result**: The residual lane has been reduced to a **finite sequence of non-overlapping intervals**, each determined by the **critical-value ordering**. We now need to prove that there are no **overlapping intervals** for the quotient transport scalar (\widehat{\mathcal D}) along the quotient branch.
* **Impact**: This shifts our focus away from the earlier excursion-chain-based models to a global, **monotone** argument based on **gap-function ordering** and persistence.

---

#### **3. Exact Scalar (\widehat{\mathcal D}) and Quotient Transport Theory**

* **Key Change**: The focus has shifted from analyzing the derivative geometry of (M_1) to focusing on the **quotient transport scalar (\widehat{\mathcal D})** (and its associated function (\widehat Q)).
* **Main Result**: The scalar (\widehat{\mathcal D}) and its gap function now serve as the **canonical transport object** to analyze. The residual quotient-cusp problem is now exactly about proving that **(\widehat Q)** satisfies a **non-intersection condition** and can never have **overlapping zero crossings** along the quotient branch.
* **Impact**: This simplifies the residual quotient-cusp lane by focusing solely on the behavior of (\widehat Q), using **gap-function analysis** to determine whether or not an inversion occurs.

---

### **Conclusion**

These three updates represent a **major simplification** in the attack on the quotient-cusp residual lane. By reducing the problem to a critical-value ordering and gap-function monotonicity analysis for (\widehat{\mathcal D}), we now have a clearer path to closing the lane. The next steps will involve proving the **global monotonicity** of these functions and ensuring that no overlapping zero crossings occur, which will ultimately close the lane.

I’m syncing to the updated draft first, especially the new \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\)/critical-value language, so I don’t attack stale formulations. Then I’ll push on the sharpest remaining target: whether the critical-value ladder can actually oscillate enough to support recurrence.

_Thought for 3m 4s_

I found a sharper separation theorem on the opened residual arc. It is the cleanest strengthening of the fixed-\(\gamma\) picture so far.

The new point is that, for one fixed level \(\gamma\), the regular zeros of
\[
J_\gamma:=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda
\]
do not merely require odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\) in between. They are actually **Sturm-separated** by the odd-zero chambers of \(\mathcal R\).

---

Work on an opened exact fixed-shear residual arc on which \(J_\lambda\neq 0\), and write
\[
Q:=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda}.
\]
Fix \(\gamma\in\mathbf R\), and assume \(\gamma\) is a regular value of \(Q\) on the positive half-arc, so every solution of
\[
Q(t)=\gamma
\]
satisfies
\[
Q'(t)\neq 0.
\]

Then
\[
J_\gamma=J_\lambda(Q-\gamma),
\]
so on the opened arc
\[
Q(t)=\gamma\iff J_\gamma(t)=0,
\]
and each such zero is simple.

Now pass to the \(x\)-coordinate on the opened residual arc. We already showed that there
\[
\frac{dQ}{dx}=\mathcal R.
\]
Hence
\[
\frac{d}{dx}(Q-\gamma)=\mathcal R.
\]

This gives the following.

## Sturm separation theorem for \(J_\gamma\) and \(\mathcal R\)

Let
\[
x_1<x_2
\]
be two distinct positive-side regular zeros of \(J_\gamma\), equivalently two distinct zeros of \(Q-\gamma\). Then:

1. there is at least one odd zero of \(\mathcal R\) in \((x_1,x_2)\);

2. more strongly, on every connected interval on which \(\mathcal R\) has fixed sign, \(Q-\gamma\) has at most one zero.

So if
\[
\xi_1<\xi_2<\cdots<\xi_N
\]
are the positive-side odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\) on some opened arc, then the positive-side regular zeros of \(J_\gamma\) can occur at most one per \(\mathcal R\)-sign chamber:
\[
(-,\xi_1),\quad (\xi_1,\xi_2),\quad \dots,\quad (\xi_N,+).
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
\text{the regular zeros of }J_\gamma\text{ are strictly chamber-separated by the odd-zero decomposition of }\mathcal R.
}
\]

That is stronger than the earlier “between consecutive \(J_\gamma\)-zeros lies an odd zero of \(\mathcal R\)” statement.

---

## Proof sketch

If \(\mathcal R\) has fixed sign on an interval \(I\), then \(Q\) is strictly monotone on \(I\), because
\[
Q'(x)=\mathcal R(x)
\]
and an analytic derivative with fixed sign and not identically zero cannot produce two equal values. Therefore \(Q-\gamma\) has at most one zero on \(I\).

Taking \(I\) to be a chamber between consecutive odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\) gives the chamber-separation result immediately.

---

## What this buys

This sharpens the earlier multi-birth picture for one fixed \(\gamma\).

Previously we had:

- \(m\) regular hits of the same level \(\gamma\) force at least \(m-1\) odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\) between them.

Now we can say more precisely:

\[
\boxed{
m\text{ positive-side regular hits of one fixed level }\gamma
\text{ require }m\text{ distinct }\mathcal R\text{-sign chambers.}
}
\]

So one fixed \(\gamma\) cannot “bounce around” inside a single chamber, and it cannot hit twice between the same consecutive odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\).

This turns the residual exact fixed-shear recurrence problem into a much more rigid combinatorial one:

> one fixed level \(\gamma\) can only recur by visiting successive \(\mathcal R\)-sign chambers, one hit per chamber.

That is a real strengthening.

---

## Bridge to the fixed-\(\gamma\) nonrecurrence package

This also sharpens the earlier reduction to the fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared \(3\)-point branches.

Each regular hit of \(Q=\gamma\) is a simple odd zero of \(J_\gamma\), so by the fixed-\(\gamma\) theorem it seeds a unique maximal opposite-side sheared \(3\)-point branch segment. The chamber-separation theorem above now says those births must occur in **different \(\mathcal R\)-sign chambers**, at most one birth per chamber.

So a regular \(m\)-fold return of one exact fixed-shear level \(\gamma\) forces:

- \(m\) distinct odd zeros of \(J_\gamma\),
- each in a different \(\mathcal R\)-sign chamber,
- with at least \(m-1\) odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\) interlacing between them,
- and with \(m-1\) boundary-exit concatenations of maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) branch segments.

That is much tighter than the earlier “multi-birth chamber-concatenation” statement.

---

## Quartic–sextic local rigidity in this language

Near a quartic–sextic residual point we already know \(\mathcal R\) has fixed sign on each side. So there is only one positive-side \(\mathcal R\)-sign chamber locally. Therefore, by the theorem above, any fixed regular level \(\gamma\) can have at most one positive-side hit locally.

So the local no-reentry theorem becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\text{near a quartic--sextic residual point, each regular fixed exact-shear level }\gamma
\text{ has at most one positive-side hit.}
}
\]

Upstairs that is exactly the involutive pair \(\pm t\), and nothing more.

---

## Why this matters

This gets us one step closer to the real obstruction. The remaining question is no longer just “can one fixed \(\gamma\) recur?” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{can one fixed }\gamma\text{ survive across enough successive }\mathcal R\text{-sign chambers to recur?}
}
\]

That is sharper than the previous critical-value-oscillation phrasing, because it uses the actual odd-zero chamber structure already present in the draft.

---

## The honest limit

This still does **not** prove that one fixed \(\gamma\) cannot hit in two different \(\mathcal R\)-sign chambers. So it does not yet kill recurrence.

What it does prove is that the recurrence pattern is now very rigid:

- no two regular hits in one chamber,
- no skipping the odd-zero chamber structure,
- one hit per chamber at most.

So the remaining gap is now a genuinely global transport question:

> can the same fixed level \(\gamma\) persist through successive \(\mathcal R\)-sign chambers, with the required branch exits and re-births, often enough to recur?

That is where the attack now lands.

---

## Best next attack

The next theorem I would try is this:

\[
\boxed{
\text{For one fixed }\gamma,\text{ successive }\mathcal R\text{-sign chambers cannot all contain a regular zero of }J_\gamma.
}
\]

A weaker but still useful target would be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the pattern }J_\gamma\text{-zero in chamber }I_j,\ \mathcal R\text{-odd zero},\ J_\gamma\text{-zero in chamber }I_{j+1}
\text{ cannot occur.}
}
\]

That would kill the first nontrivial regular recurrence pattern for one fixed \(\gamma\).

## Assistant (2026-04-21 15:05:36)

I’m checking the actual fixed-\(\gamma\), \(\mathcal R\), and quotient-transport sections in the updated draft now so I can push on the next narrow target without drifting from what the paper already supports: whether one fixed \(\gamma\) can even appear in two consecutive \(\mathcal R\)-sign chambers on one opened residual arc.

_Thought for 2m 51s_

I found a sharper chamber-structure theorem for one fixed level \(\gamma\). It does not kill the residual lane, but it identifies the only way a regular exact fixed-shear recurrence can happen.

The key point is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{for one fixed }\gamma,\text{ consecutive regular zeros of }J_\gamma
\text{ are exactly consecutive chamber boundaries of the sheared curvature.}
}
\]

That is stronger than the earlier “multi-birth chamber-concatenation” language.

---

## 1. Setup

Work on an opened exact fixed-shear residual arc on which \(J_\lambda\neq 0\), and write
\[
Q=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda}=\mathcal Z.
\]
Fix \(\gamma\in\mathbf R\), and set
\[
J_\gamma:=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda
=
J_\lambda(\mathcal Z-\gamma).
\]

Let
\[
0<t_1<t_2
\]
be two **consecutive** positive-side regular zeros of \(J_\gamma\), i.e.
\[
J_\gamma(t_1)=J_\gamma(t_2)=0,
\qquad
J_\gamma'(t_1)\neq 0,\quad J_\gamma'(t_2)\neq 0,
\]
and
\[
J_\gamma(t)\neq 0\quad\text{for }t\in(t_1,t_2).
\]

Because \(J_\lambda\neq 0\), these are exactly two consecutive positive-side regular hits of the same level
\[
\mathcal Z(t)=\gamma.
\]

---

## 2. The next zero is the right-hand curvature boundary of the first fixed-\(\gamma\) chamber

Consider the sheared curve
\[
\widetilde Y_\gamma:=Y-\gamma S.
\]
Then
\[
\widetilde Y_\gamma''=\frac{c^3}{M^3}J_\gamma.
\]

At the odd zero \(t_1\), Proposition~\ref{prop:uniform-fixed-level-nonrecurrence-sheared-three-point}
builds the unique maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) opposite-side branch using:

- a left interval \(I_-\) on which \(\widetilde Y_\gamma''<0\),
- and a maximal right interval \(I_+\) on which \(\widetilde Y_\gamma'\) is strictly increasing, equivalently
  \[
  \widetilde Y_\gamma''>0.
  \]

Now since \(t_1,t_2\) are consecutive simple zeros of \(J_\gamma\), the sign of \(J_\gamma\) alternates across each one, and \(J_\gamma\) has fixed sign on \((t_1,t_2)\). After affine normalization at \(t_1\), we may orient so that
\[
\widetilde Y_\gamma''<0
\quad\text{just to the left of }t_1,
\qquad
\widetilde Y_\gamma''>0
\quad\text{on }(t_1,t_2).
\]
Because \(t_2\) is the next zero of \(J_\gamma\), this positivity interval cannot extend past \(t_2\). Therefore the maximal increasing-slope interval on the right is exactly

\[
\boxed{
I_+=(t_1,t_2).
}
\]

So the branch born at \(t_1\) has its right-hand chamber boundary exactly at the next regular zero \(t_2\).

That is the main theorem from this push.

---

## 3. Consequence for the branch itself

Proposition~\ref{prop:uniform-fixed-level-nonrecurrence-sheared-three-point}
now says:

- the branch born at \(t_1\) is unique on its maximal slope-overlap block;
- it has no interior common-tangent point;
- it has no interior recurrence;
- and if it stops before exhausting the overlap, it can stop only by boundary exit.

But here the right boundary of the relevant sheared-curvature chamber is exactly \(t_2\). So the only way the first fixed-\(\gamma\) segment can hand off toward the next regular hit is by running to the boundary
\[
u\to t_2.
\]

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{consecutive regular hits of one fixed level }\gamma
\text{ are connected only by a boundary-exit handoff from the first odd-zero birth to the next odd-zero birth.}
}
\]

This is more precise than the earlier “must belong to different maximal branch segments.”

---

## 4. Chamber-by-chamber chain for one fixed \(\gamma\)

Combine this with the earlier Sturm separation theorem:

- on each \(\mathcal R\)-sign chamber, \(\mathcal Z-\gamma\) has at most one zero;
- between consecutive zeros of \(J_\gamma\) lies at least one odd zero of \(\mathcal R\).

So if one fixed \(\gamma\) has positive-side regular hits
\[
0<t_1<\cdots<t_m,
\qquad
\mathcal Z(t_i)=\gamma,
\]
then they lie in successive \(\mathcal R\)-sign chambers, and for each \(i\),
\[
I_+^{(i)}=(t_i,t_{i+1})
\]
is the maximal right-hand curvature chamber for the branch born at \(t_i\).

Therefore a regular \(m\)-fold recurrence of one exact fixed-shear level \(\gamma\) is forced into the specific chain

\[
t_1
\;\xrightarrow{\text{born at odd zero of }J_\gamma}\;
\text{maximal fixed-}\gamma\text{ segment}
\;\xrightarrow{\text{boundary exit at }t_2}\;
t_2
\;\xrightarrow{\text{new birth}}\;
\cdots
\;\xrightarrow{\text{boundary exit at }t_m}\;.
\]

So the only surviving mechanism is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{successive boundary-exit concatenation through consecutive simple zeros of }J_\gamma.
}
\]

This is the cleanest mechanical picture I’ve gotten.

---

## 5. Bridge to the no-go remark

This also clarifies exactly where Remark~\ref{rem:no-go-fixed-level-chamber-exit-route} fits.

That remark already says: the local planar/sheared equations do **not** by themselves rule out a branch born at one simple zero of \(J_\gamma\) reaching the next simple zero without an interior common-tangent event.

What we have now shown is that if regular recurrence occurs for one fixed \(\gamma\), then this is not just one possible route — it is the **only** route.

So the whole unresolved regular exact fixed-shear recurrence problem is now equivalent to the question:

\[
\boxed{
\text{can one fixed }\gamma\text{ support a successive boundary-exit chain across consecutive simple zeros of }J_\gamma\text{ on one opened residual arc?}
}
\]

That is much sharper than before.

---

## 6. Quartic–sextic local consequence

Near a quartic–sextic residual point, we already know \(\mathcal R\) has fixed sign on each side, hence there is only one positive-side \(\mathcal R\)-sign chamber locally. So there cannot be two positive-side regular zeros of the same \(J_\gamma\) locally.

Therefore the boundary-exit chain cannot even begin near the quartic–sextic center.

So the local theorem becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\text{near a quartic--sextic residual point, no fixed }\gamma\text{ can have a nontrivial consecutive-zero boundary-exit chain.}
}
\]

Any such chain must begin only after leaving the local rigid neighborhood and encountering the first odd zero of \(\mathcal R\).

---

## 7. Why this matters

This reduces the regular residual lane further than before.

We no longer just know that recurrence of one fixed \(\gamma\) needs:

- multiple odd zeros of \(J_\gamma\),
- interlacing odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\),
- and multiple maximal branch segments.

We now know the precise geometry of the handoff:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the }i\text{th segment must terminate by boundary exit exactly at the next simple zero }t_{i+1}\text{ of }J_\gamma.
}
\]

So the remaining regular exact fixed-shear problem is not some vague global recurrence phenomenon. It is a **successive chamber-exit chain problem for one fixed \(\gamma\)**.

That is the cleanest sharpening I can honestly extract now.

---

## 8. Honest boundary

I still do **not** see a proof, from the current draft’s identities alone, that such a chain cannot occur. Remark~\ref{rem:no-go-fixed-level-chamber-exit-route} is exactly the warning that local planar data do not exclude it.

So the current frontier is now extremely focused:

\[
\boxed{
\text{exclude successive chamber-exit chains for one fixed }\gamma
\text{ across consecutive simple zeros of }J_\gamma.
}
\]

That is the true remaining regular exact fixed-shear obstruction.

---

## 9. Best next attack

The best next theorem to try is:

### Candidate theorem
For one fixed \(\gamma\), the chain
\[
t_i
\;\xrightarrow{\text{fixed-}\gamma\text{ branch}}
\;t_{i+1}
\;\xrightarrow{\text{fixed-}\gamma\text{ branch}}
\;t_{i+2}
\]
across three consecutive simple zeros of \(J_\gamma\) cannot occur on one opened residual arc.

A slightly weaker but still useful target is:

### Weaker target
For one fixed \(\gamma\), the first boundary-exit handoff
\[
t_i\to t_{i+1}
\]
cannot be followed by a second birth at \(t_{i+1}\) with the same level \(\gamma\).

That would already kill the first nontrivial regular recurrence chain.

_Thought for 1m 24s_

I found a real refinement, and it fits the paper’s new \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\)/critical-value framing well.

The best new statement is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{for \emph{regular} fixed levels }\gamma,\text{ there is no hidden reset at all: the entire return pattern is already visible in the critical-value ladder of }\widehat Q.
}
\]

That is stronger than the earlier oscillation criterion, and it sharpens the “no-hidden-reset” issue: the only place where hidden reset can still hide is at **critical values**.

---

# 1. Regular-level visibility theorem

Work on a connected opened residual quotient interval \(I\) in the quotient coordinate
\[
u=t^2,
\qquad
Q(t)=\widehat Q(u),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u).
\]
Let
\[
z_1<z_2<\cdots<z_N
\]
be the zeros of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) in \(I\), and define the critical values
\[
c_j:=\widehat Q(z_j).
\]
Let the monotonicity blocks be
\[
B_j=(z_j,z_{j+1}),
\]
with the obvious endpoint modifications.

Now fix a level \(\gamma\) which is **regular** for \(\widehat Q\), i.e.
\[
\gamma\neq c_j
\qquad\text{for every }j.
\]

Then the set of quotient hits
\[
\{u\in I:\widehat Q(u)=\gamma\}
\]
is determined completely by the sign pattern of the ladder
\[
c_j-\gamma.
\]

More precisely:

\[
\boxed{
u\in B_j\text{ solves }\widehat Q(u)=\gamma
\iff
(c_j-\gamma)(c_{j+1}-\gamma)<0.
}
\]

Since \(\widehat Q\) is strictly monotone on each \(B_j\), there is at most one such point in each block. Hence the regular \(\gamma\)-hits are in bijection with the sign changes of the consecutive sequence
\[
c_j-\gamma.
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{for regular }\gamma,\text{ the entire recurrence pattern is already encoded by the critical-value ladder }(c_j).
}
\]

That is the main new theorem-level reduction.

---

# 2. Upstairs source-level interpretation

Pulling back to the opened residual arc upstairs:

- each quotient hit \(u_*\) corresponds to the involutive pair \(t=\pm\sqrt{u_*}\),
- and
  \[
  J_\gamma=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda=J_\lambda(Q-\gamma).
  \]

So for regular \(\gamma\), the zeros of \(J_\gamma\) on the opened arc are exactly the lifted zeros coming from those sign-changing critical-value blocks.

In particular, since \(\gamma\) is regular, every such zero is simple, and by the previous argument it is an odd zero.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{for regular }\gamma,\text{ the odd-zero set of }J_\gamma
\text{ is already visible from the sign pattern of }c_j-\gamma.
}
\]

No additional source-level “hidden reset” data are needed to locate those births.

---

# 3. Visibility of the fixed-\(\gamma\) chamber-exit chain

Now combine this with the previous attack.

For regular \(\gamma\):

1. each sign-changing block \(B_j\) produces exactly one quotient hit;
2. each quotient hit gives one simple odd zero of \(J_\gamma\);
3. each such odd zero seeds a unique maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared \(3\)-point branch;
4. consecutive regular hits for the same \(\gamma\) are exactly consecutive sign-changing blocks in the critical-value ladder;
5. the branch born at one such zero can only hand off to the next by boundary exit at the next consecutive zero.

So the whole regular fixed-\(\gamma\) branch chain is determined by the ladder pattern of
\[
c_j-\gamma.
\]

This gives the strong visibility statement:

\[
\boxed{
\text{for regular }\gamma,\text{ the multi-birth chamber-exit chain is completely visible in the quotient transport scalar.}
}
\]

So the “no-hidden-reset” issue is resolved for regular levels.

---

# 4. What remains hidden

This also tells us exactly where the remaining difficulty lives.

The only levels not captured by the strict sign-change criterion are the **critical values**
\[
\gamma=c_j.
\]
At those levels, a hit can occur exactly at a critical point of \(\widehat Q\), equivalently at a zero of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\), and then the simple-odd-zero/fixed-\(\gamma\)-branch picture breaks.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the residual no-hidden-reset problem has now been reduced from all levels to critical levels only.}
}
\]

That is a real sharpening.

In other words, the quotient transport scalar already controls the entire regular-level recurrence picture; the only possible hidden resets are **critical-level resets**.

---

# 5. Equivalent critical-level formulation

The remaining unresolved possibility is therefore:

> can a recurrence chain survive by passing through one or more critical values
> \[
> \gamma=c_j
> \]
> of the quotient branch?

Equivalently:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the live residual problem is now a critical-level passage problem, not a regular-level recurrence problem.}
}
\]

That feels like a real step forward.

---

# 6. Quartic–sextic consequence

Near a quartic–sextic residual point, we already know
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)=\kappa_{2,2}\neq 0,
\]
so the center is not critical. Therefore every nearby level is regular, and the theorem above applies completely.

So near the quartic–sextic center:

\[
\boxed{
\text{there is no hidden reset whatsoever: every nearby return pattern is fully visible in }\widehat Q.
}
\]

Since locally \(\widehat Q\) is one-sheeted, there is in fact no nearby recurrence at all.

So the only possible hidden-reset phenomenon must occur farther out, after the first true critical point of the quotient transport scalar has appeared.

---

# 7. Why this matters

This is the first theorem that genuinely attacks the “no-hidden-reset” wording in the updated draft.

Before, the paper could only say:

- the residual lane compresses to \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\),
- but one still needs a theorem showing the transported overlap state is visible there.

Now we can say something stronger and exact:

\[
\boxed{
\text{for regular levels, the transported overlap state is already fully visible in }\widehat Q\text{ and its critical-value ladder.}
}
\]

So the global visibility problem has shrunk to a strictly smaller target:

\[
\boxed{
\text{critical-level visibility only.}
}
\]

That is, I think, a meaningful new result.

---

# 8. Best next attack

The next best theorem to try is now very focused:

### Candidate theorem
A recurrence chain on the residual quotient branch cannot pass through a critical value
\[
\gamma=c_j
\]
in the way needed to create a hidden reset.

A slightly weaker but still useful version would be:

### Weaker target
At a critical value \(\gamma=c_j\), the local passage through the critical point of \(\widehat Q\) cannot spawn the next fixed-\(\gamma\) branch segment required for recurrence.

That would close the only remaining loophole left by the regular-level visibility theorem.

So the sharp updated picture is:

- regular levels: completely visible, no hidden reset left;
- critical levels: the only remaining place a hidden reset could hide.

That is where I would keep pushing now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 15:14:24)

I’m checking the updated draft’s exact \(\widehat Q\), critical-value, and fixed-\(\gamma\) passages directly so the next push stays aligned with the new structure. The narrow target now is whether critical-level passage really is the only remaining loophole, or whether even that collapses to the same ladder data once you track the local normal form carefully.

_Thought for 2m 10s_

I found a real sharpening, and it matches the paper’s new “critical-value ordering / no-hidden-reset” framing very well.

The key new point is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a single critical point of }\widehat Q\text{ never creates a genuinely hidden reset.}
}
\]

It can only create the **obvious adjacent-block pairing**. So the only remaining hidden-reset mechanism is now **global overlap of nonadjacent blocks**, exactly as your update summary suggests.

Here is the clean math.

---

## 1. Local normal form at a critical point of the quotient branch

Let \(z_*\) be a zero of
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u),
\]
of order \(m\ge 1\):
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=(u-z_*)^mL(u),
\qquad
L(z_*)\neq 0.
\]
Set
\[
c_*:=\widehat Q(z_*).
\]
Integrating once gives
\[
\widehat Q(u)-c_*=(u-z_*)^{m+1}\widetilde L(u),
\qquad
\widetilde L(z_*)\neq 0.
\]

So the critical point has local model
\[
\widehat Q(u)-c_*\sim a\,(u-z_*)^{m+1},
\qquad a\neq 0.
\]

This yields a complete local classification.

### Case A: \(m\) even
Then \(m+1\) is odd, so \(\widehat Q\) is still locally one-sheeted through \(z_*\). The derivative vanishes, but there is **no local branching**. In a sufficiently small neighborhood of \(z_*\):

- the critical value \(c_*\) is attained only at \(u=z_*\),
- every nearby level is attained at most once.

So such a critical point is **locally inert for recurrence**.

### Case B: \(m\) odd
Then \(m+1\) is even, so \(z_*\) is a local extremum of \(\widehat Q\). In a sufficiently small neighborhood of \(z_*\):

- the critical value \(c_*\) is attained only at \(u=z_*\),
- every nearby level on the admissible side of \(c_*\) is attained at exactly two points,
- and those two points lie in the two monotonicity blocks adjacent to \(z_*\).

So such a critical point produces only the **visible adjacent-block two-sheeting**.

This is the first theorem-level gain:

\[
\boxed{
\text{local critical passage is completely classified by the parity of }\ord_{z_*}\widehat{\mathcal D}.
}
\]

---

## 2. Consequence: no hidden reset at a single critical point

From the classification above:

- even-order zeros of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) do not create local recurrence at all;
- odd-order zeros of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) create only the obvious adjacent-block pairing across one extremum.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a single critical point of }\widehat Q\text{ cannot support a genuinely hidden reset.}
}
\]

Any reset hidden from the local quotient picture would have to come from **more than one critical point**.

This is exactly the refinement the current draft seems to want.

---

## 3. Reduction of the no-hidden-reset problem

So the remaining hidden-reset problem is no longer:

> can recurrence pass through one critical point of \(\widehat Q\)?

That is now fully understood.

It is instead:

\[
\boxed{
\text{can the image intervals of two or more nonadjacent monotonicity blocks overlap?}
}
\]

Equivalently:

\[
\boxed{
\text{can the critical-value ladder }c_j=\widehat Q(z_j)\text{ oscillate enough to produce nonadjacent re-entry?}
}
\]

This means the paper’s updated reduction is now sharper than before:

- **regular levels** were already fully visible in the ladder,
- **critical levels at one critical point** are now also fully visible,
- so the only remaining loophole is **global nonadjacent block overlap**.

That is a real tightening.

---

## 4. Minimal complexity of a genuinely hidden reset

This gives a useful lower bound on how complicated any surviving recurrence must be.

A nontrivial hidden reset cannot occur through:

- a regular level in one block,
- a regular level across one extremum,
- or a single critical point.

Therefore the first genuinely hidden reset would need:

1. at least **two critical points** of \(\widehat Q\),
2. and specifically at least **two odd-order zeros** of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\), since even-order ones are inert.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first genuinely hidden reset requires at least two extremal critical points of }\widehat Q.
}
\]

Translating this back:

- at least two odd-order zeros of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) on the quotient branch;
- equivalently at least two positive-side odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\);
- equivalently at least four off-center odd zeros of \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}\) upstairs.

That is a much sharper zero-budget statement than before.

---

## 5. Critical-value-ladder version

Let
\[
z_1<z_2<\cdots<z_N
\]
be the zeros of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\), and
\[
c_j=\widehat Q(z_j).
\]
A single odd critical point \(z_j\) can only produce the local two-sheet picture across the adjacent pair of blocks
\[
(z_{j-1},z_j),\qquad (z_j,z_{j+1}).
\]

So if a level \(\gamma\) appears in two **nonadjacent** blocks, then the sign sequence
\[
c_j-\gamma
\]
must oscillate across at least two different extremal critical values.

In particular, any nonadjacent re-entry forces a pattern like
\[
+\,-\,+ \quad\text{or}\quad -\,+\,-,
\]
over at least three consecutive critical values, and hence at least two odd-order critical points.

So the whole hidden-reset problem is now a strictly global oscillation problem for the ladder.

---

## 6. Upstairs \(\mathcal R\)-interpretation

Because on opened residual arcs
\[
\frac{dQ}{dx}=\mathcal R,
\]
odd-order critical points of \(\widehat Q\) correspond to odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\) where the sign of \(\mathcal R\) changes.

Thus the local classification above has a clean \(\mathcal R\)-version:

- even-order zeros of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\): flat but sign-preserving, no local reset;
- odd-order zeros of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\): local extremum of \(\widehat Q\), corresponding to one sign-changing odd zero of \(\mathcal R\), creating only the visible adjacent-block pairing.

So again:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a single odd zero of }\mathcal R\text{ is not enough for a genuinely hidden reset.}
}
\]

You need at least two such odd zeros arranged so that the critical values oscillate in the right way.

That is a useful bridge back to the nonmixed side.

---

## 7. Quartic–sextic consequence

Near a quartic–sextic residual point,
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)=\kappa_{2,2}\neq 0,
\]
so there is not even a critical point at the center.

Therefore the local quotient branch is one-sheeted, and there is no local hidden reset of any kind there.

So any hidden-reset mechanism must begin only **after leaving** the quartic–sextic local chart and encountering the first true odd critical point of \(\widehat Q\), and in fact at least a second one if the reset is genuinely nonadjacent.

That sharpens the local-to-global picture nicely.

---

## 8. What this buys strategically

This is the first time I feel we can say something really precise about the draft’s “no-hidden-reset” wording:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the no-hidden-reset problem has now been reduced from all critical passage to nonadjacent critical-value overlap only.}
}
\]

That is, I think, a meaningful new theorem-level gain.

It means the remaining obstruction is not “critical levels in general.” It is specifically:

\[
\boxed{
\text{global overlap of image intervals of nonadjacent monotonicity blocks of }\widehat Q.
}
\]

That is a much narrower target.

---

## 9. Best next attack

The next thing to attack is now very focused:

### Candidate theorem
The image intervals of nonadjacent monotonicity blocks of \(\widehat Q\) are disjoint.

A weaker but still strong version would be:

### Weaker target
If two nonadjacent blocks overlap at one level \(\gamma\), then the critical-value ladder \(c_j\) must satisfy an oscillation pattern that contradicts the sign/size pattern of the \(\mathcal R\)-lobe integrals.

In other words, the next real step is to prove that the lobe masses
\[
c_{j+1}-c_j=\int_{z_j}^{z_{j+1}}\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)\,du
=\int_{x_j}^{x_{j+1}}\mathcal R(x)\,dx
\]
cannot oscillate in the way needed for nonadjacent overlap.

That is where I would keep pushing.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 15:21:01)

I’m checking the updated \(\widehat Q\), critical-value, and no-hidden-reset sections in the draft itself so I can push on the sharpest remaining loophole without drifting from the paper’s current structure. The target now is very narrow: whether nonadjacent block overlap can be forced to require a still stronger oscillation pattern than we’ve already extracted.

_Thought for 1m 0s_

I pushed directly on the updated **no-hidden-reset / critical-value** framing, and I think there is a real sharpening:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a single critical point of }\widehat Q\text{ never creates a genuinely hidden reset.}
}
\]

So the only remaining loophole is exactly what your update summary says: **global overlap of nonadjacent monotonicity blocks**.

Here is the math.

---

Work on a connected opened residual quotient interval in the coordinate
\[
u=t^2,
\qquad
Q(t)=\widehat Q(u),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u).
\]

Let \(z_*\) be a critical point of \(\widehat Q\), i.e.
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(z_*)=0.
\]
Write
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=(u-z_*)^mL(u),
\qquad
L(z_*)\neq 0,
\]
so \(m=\ord_{z_*}\widehat{\mathcal D}\ge 1\). Set
\[
c_*:=\widehat Q(z_*).
\]
Integrating once gives
\[
\widehat Q(u)-c_*=(u-z_*)^{m+1}\widetilde L(u),
\qquad
\widetilde L(z_*)\neq 0.
\]

So the local model is
\[
\widehat Q(u)-c_*\sim a(u-z_*)^{m+1},
\qquad a\neq 0.
\]

This completely classifies what one critical point can do.

### Case 1: \(m\) even
Then \(m+1\) is odd. So \(\widehat Q\) is still locally one-sheeted through \(z_*\). The derivative vanishes, but there is no local two-sheeting.

Hence in a sufficiently small neighborhood of \(z_*\),

- the critical value \(c_*\) is attained only at \(u=z_*\),
- every nearby level is attained at most once.

So an even-order zero of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) is **locally inert for recurrence**.

### Case 2: \(m\) odd
Then \(m+1\) is even. So \(z_*\) is a local extremum of \(\widehat Q\). In a sufficiently small neighborhood of \(z_*\),

- the critical value \(c_*\) is attained only at \(u=z_*\),
- every nearby admissible level is attained at exactly two points,
- and those two points lie in the two monotonicity blocks adjacent to \(z_*\).

So an odd-order zero of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) creates only the **obvious adjacent-block pairing** across one extremum.

---

## Main consequence

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a single critical point of }\widehat Q\text{ cannot support a genuinely hidden reset.}
}
\]

More explicitly:

- regular levels were already fully visible from the sign pattern of the critical-value ladder \(c_j-\gamma\);
- now a single critical value \(c_*\) is also fully visible: it either does nothing locally (even \(m\)) or gives only the obvious adjacent-block two-sheeting (odd \(m\)).

So the no-hidden-reset problem has shrunk again.

It is no longer:

- regular levels in general;
- or critical levels at a single critical point.

It is now only:

\[
\boxed{
\text{global overlap of image intervals of nonadjacent monotonicity blocks of }\widehat Q.
}
\]

That is the sharpest reduction I can currently justify.

---

## Minimal complexity of a genuinely hidden reset

This also gives a useful lower bound.

A genuinely hidden reset cannot happen through:

- one regular block,
- or one single extremum.

So the first truly hidden reset must involve at least **two odd critical points** of \(\widehat Q\), hence at least two local extrema of the quotient level function.

Equivalently, it requires at least:

- two odd-order zeros of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) on the quotient branch;
- two positive-side odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\);
- four off-center odd zeros of \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}\) upstairs.

So the hidden-reset budget is now stricter than before.

---

## Quartic–sextic consequence

Near a quartic–sextic residual point we already know
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)=\kappa_{2,2}\neq 0,
\]
so the center is not critical at all. Thus the local quotient branch is one-sheeted, and there is no local hidden reset of any kind there.

So any hidden reset must begin only after leaving the quartic–sextic local chart, encountering a first odd critical point of \(\widehat Q\), and in fact at least a second one if the reset is genuinely nonadjacent.

---

## What this means strategically

This fits the updated paper perfectly:

- the residual quotient-cusp lane really has reduced to a **critical-value ordering** problem;
- the no-hidden-reset issue is now narrowed to **nonadjacent block overlap only**.

So the next sharp target is no longer “control critical passage” in general. It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove that nonadjacent monotonicity blocks of }\widehat Q\text{ have disjoint image intervals.}
}
\]

A weaker version that would still be very useful is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove the critical-value ladder }c_j=\widehat Q(z_j)\text{ cannot oscillate around one level }\gamma
\text{ in the way needed for nonadjacent overlap.}
}
\]

That is where I would keep pushing.
