## Assistant (2026-04-21 15:28:10)

I’m pushing on the remaining loophole in the updated framing: nonadjacent overlap of monotonicity blocks of \(\widehat Q\). I want to see whether the current equations force a stronger oscillation budget than “at least two odd critical points,” or whether the draft really stops exactly there.

_Thought for 2m 39s_

I found a sharper reduction that fits the updated draft very well.

The right object is not just the critical-value ladder itself, but the **\(\gamma\)-excursion components** of the quotient branch.

This gives a clean theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\text{for a regular level }\gamma,\ \text{a genuinely hidden reset is exactly a multi-critical }\gamma\text{-excursion island of }\widehat Q.
}
\]

That is more precise than “nonadjacent block overlap.”

---

## 1. Excursion decomposition for a regular level \(\gamma\)

Work on a connected opened residual quotient interval \(I\) in the quotient coordinate
\[
u=t^2,
\qquad
Q(t)=\widehat Q(u),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u).
\]

Fix a **regular** level \(\gamma\), meaning
\[
\gamma\neq c_j:=\widehat Q(z_j)
\]
for every critical point \(z_j\) of \(\widehat Q\).

Define the open superlevel and sublevel sets
\[
E_\gamma^+:=\{u\in I:\widehat Q(u)>\gamma\},
\qquad
E_\gamma^-:=\{u\in I:\widehat Q(u)<\gamma\}.
\]

Each connected component \(C\) of \(E_\gamma^\pm\) is an open interval. If \(C=(a,b)\) is bounded, then
\[
\widehat Q(a)=\widehat Q(b)=\gamma,
\qquad
\pm(\widehat Q(u)-\gamma)>0\quad\text{for }u\in(a,b).
\]

So every bounded excursion component has exactly two boundary hits of the level \(\gamma\).

---

## 2. What a bounded excursion component contains

Let \(C=(a,b)\) be a bounded component of \(E_\gamma^\pm\).

Then \(a,b\) are two quotient hits of the same level \(\gamma\), and inside \(C\) the function \(\widehat Q-\gamma\) keeps one strict sign. Therefore \(\widehat Q\) must first move away from \(\gamma\) and later return to \(\gamma\), so \(\widehat Q'\) must change sign inside \(C\).

Hence \(C\) contains at least one odd-order zero of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\), equivalently at least one local extremum of \(\widehat Q\).

This is just the excursion-language version of the earlier odd-critical-point necessity.

But now there is a sharper dichotomy.

### Case A: \(C\) contains exactly one odd critical point
Then the two boundary hits lie in the two monotonicity blocks adjacent to that one extremum. So this is just the **visible adjacent-block pairing**.

### Case B: \(C\) contains at least two odd critical points
Then the two boundary hits lie in nonadjacent monotonicity blocks. This is exactly the first genuinely hidden-reset pattern.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{for regular }\gamma,\ \text{a nonadjacent hidden reset occurs iff some bounded }\gamma\text{-excursion component contains at least two odd critical points of }\widehat Q.
}
\]

That is the main new reduction.

---

## 3. Critical-value-ladder version

Let
\[
z_1<z_2<\cdots<z_N
\]
be the critical points, and
\[
c_j=\widehat Q(z_j).
\]

Then a bounded \(\gamma\)-excursion component corresponds exactly to a finite consecutive string of critical values all lying on one side of \(\gamma\), bracketed by values on the opposite side.

So the sign pattern of
\[
c_j-\gamma
\]
along one such excursion is either
\[
+,\underbrace{-,\ldots,-}_{\text{inside the excursion}},+
\]
or
\[
-,\underbrace{+,\ldots,+}_{\text{inside the excursion}},-.
\]

Now:

- if the interior string has length \(1\), this is the ordinary adjacent-block visible pairing;
- if the interior string has length at least \(2\), this is exactly a **multi-critical excursion island**, hence a genuinely nonadjacent reset.

So the hidden-reset problem is now equivalent to excluding sign patterns of the form
\[
+,-,-,+,\qquad -,+,+,-,
\]
or longer versions
\[
+,-,\ldots,-,+,\qquad -,+,\ldots,+,-,
\]
in the critical-value ladder for one regular level \(\gamma\).

That is more precise than “no nonadjacent overlap.”

---

## 4. Upstairs source-level interpretation

Pull this back to the opened residual arc upstairs.

Recall
\[
Q=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda},
\qquad
J_\gamma:=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda=J_\lambda(Q-\gamma),
\qquad
\frac{dQ}{dx}=\mathcal R.
\]

Then a bounded \(\gamma\)-excursion component downstairs corresponds upstairs to an interval
\[
x_-<x_+
\]
such that

- \(J_\gamma(x_-)=J_\gamma(x_+)=0\),
- \(J_\gamma\) has one strict sign in \((x_-,x_+)\),
- and \(\mathcal R\) changes sign inside.

So the same theorem becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a regular hidden reset for one fixed }\gamma
\text{ is exactly a same-sign interval of }J_\gamma
\text{ bounded by two zeros of }J_\gamma
\text{ and containing at least two odd zeros of }\mathcal R.
}
\]

If there is only one odd zero of \(\mathcal R\) inside, that is the visible adjacent-block extremum case.  
If there are two or more, that is the genuine hidden-reset case.

This is the cleanest upstairs reformulation I have so far.

---

## 5. Minimal complexity of the first hidden reset

This gives a very crisp minimal-complexity statement.

A genuinely hidden reset for a regular level \(\gamma\) requires:

1. a bounded \(\gamma\)-excursion component,
2. containing at least **two odd critical points** of \(\widehat Q\),
3. equivalently at least **two positive-side odd zeros** of \(\mathcal R\),
4. equivalently at least **four off-center odd zeros** of \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}\) upstairs.

So the first hidden reset is not just “more than one critical point.” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first hidden reset is exactly the first multi-critical }\gamma\text{-excursion island.}
}
\]

That is a good way to package the remaining loophole.

---

## 6. Quartic–sextic consequence

Near a quartic–sextic residual point,
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)=\kappa_{2,2}\neq 0,
\]
so there is no critical point at the center. Thus there is no local bounded \(\gamma\)-excursion component with any critical point inside.

So locally:

\[
\boxed{
\text{near the quartic--sextic center, there are no }\gamma\text{-excursion islands at all.}
}
\]

This recovers local one-sheetedness in the strongest language so far.

Any hidden reset must begin only after leaving the local quartic–sextic chart and creating the first true bounded \(\gamma\)-excursion component farther out.

---

## 7. Why this matters

This is the sharpest reduction I have now.

The residual no-hidden-reset problem is no longer just:

- critical-value overlap,
- or nonadjacent block overlap,
- or oscillation of \(c_j-\gamma\).

It is exactly:

\[
\boxed{
\text{exclude bounded multi-critical }\gamma\text{-excursion islands of }\widehat Q.
}
\]

That is a very concrete target.

And it aligns perfectly with the updated draft summary:

- the quotient-cusp lane is reduced to critical-value ordering;
- local no-reentry is done;
- the remaining issue is global non-overlap;
- the only possible hidden reset is a multi-critical excursion island.

---

## 8. Honest boundary

I still do **not** see, from the current identities alone, a proof that such multi-critical excursion islands cannot occur.

So the current math stops at the exact reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{regular hidden reset }
\Longleftrightarrow
\text{bounded multi-critical }\gamma\text{-excursion island of }\widehat Q.
}
\]

That is already a real gain, but not yet the final theorem.

---

## 9. Best next attack

The best next theorem to try is now extremely focused:

### Candidate theorem
Every bounded \(\gamma\)-excursion component of \(\widehat Q\) contains at most one odd critical point.

That would immediately kill all genuinely hidden resets.

A slightly weaker but still useful version would be:

### Weaker target
If a bounded \(\gamma\)-excursion component contains two odd critical points, then the corresponding \(\mathcal R\)-lobe masses cannot have the sign/magnitude pattern needed for the boundary values to both equal \(\gamma\).

That is where I would keep pushing next.

_Thought for 59s_

I pushed on the updated **no-hidden-reset / critical-value** framing and got a sharper reduction.

The new gain is that a **single** critical point of \(\widehat Q\) is now completely understood, and it never produces a genuinely hidden reset. So the only remaining loophole is a **bounded multi-critical \(\gamma\)-excursion island**.

That is the cleanest narrowing I can justify.

---

Let
\[
Q(t)=\widehat Q(u),\qquad u=t^2,\qquad \widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u)
\]
on a connected opened residual quotient interval \(I\).

Fix a critical point \(z_*\in I\) of \(\widehat Q\), so
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(z_*)=0.
\]
Write
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=(u-z_*)^mL(u),\qquad L(z_*)\neq 0,
\]
and set
\[
c_*:=\widehat Q(z_*).
\]
Then
\[
\widehat Q(u)-c_*=(u-z_*)^{m+1}\widetilde L(u),\qquad \widetilde L(z_*)\neq 0.
\]

So the local behavior is determined entirely by the parity of \(m\).

## 1. Single critical-point classification

If \(m\) is even, then \(m+1\) is odd, so \(\widehat Q\) is still locally one-sheeted through \(z_*\). In a small neighborhood of \(z_*\),

- the critical value \(c_*\) is attained only at \(u=z_*\),
- every nearby level is attained at most once.

So an even-order zero of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) is **locally inert** for recurrence.

If \(m\) is odd, then \(m+1\) is even, so \(z_*\) is a local extremum of \(\widehat Q\). In a small neighborhood of \(z_*\),

- the critical value \(c_*\) is still attained only at \(u=z_*\),
- every nearby admissible level is attained at exactly two points,
- and those two points lie in the two monotonicity blocks adjacent to \(z_*\).

So an odd-order zero of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) creates only the **obvious adjacent-block pairing**.

Hence:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a single critical point of }\widehat Q\text{ never creates a genuinely hidden reset.}
}
\]

That is the first clean theorem from this push.

---

## 2. Excursion-island formulation

Now fix a **regular** level \(\gamma\), meaning
\[
\gamma\neq c_j
\]
for every critical value \(c_j=\widehat Q(z_j)\).

Consider the excursion sets
\[
E_\gamma^+=\{u\in I:\widehat Q(u)>\gamma\},
\qquad
E_\gamma^-=\{u\in I:\widehat Q(u)<\gamma\}.
\]
Each connected component is an open interval. If \(C=(a,b)\) is a bounded component of one of these excursion sets, then
\[
\widehat Q(a)=\widehat Q(b)=\gamma
\]
and \(\widehat Q-\gamma\) has one strict sign in \((a,b)\).

Such a bounded component is what I’ll call a **\(\gamma\)-excursion island**.

Now:

- if \(C\) contains exactly one odd critical point of \(\widehat Q\), then the two boundary hits \(a,b\) are just the visible adjacent-block pair across that one extremum;
- if \(C\) contains at least two odd critical points, then the two boundary hits lie in nonadjacent monotonicity blocks, and this is exactly the first genuinely hidden reset pattern.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{for regular }\gamma,\ \text{a genuinely hidden reset occurs iff there is a bounded }\gamma\text{-excursion island containing at least two odd critical points of }\widehat Q.
}
\]

That is sharper than “nonadjacent block overlap.”

---

## 3. Critical-value-ladder version

Let
\[
z_1<z_2<\cdots<z_N
\]
be the critical points and
\[
c_j=\widehat Q(z_j)
\]
their critical values.

Then a bounded \(\gamma\)-excursion island corresponds exactly to a consecutive run of critical values all lying on one side of \(\gamma\), bracketed by values on the other side. So the sign sequence
\[
c_j-\gamma
\]
must contain a pattern
\[
+,-,\ldots,-,+ \qquad\text{or}\qquad -, +,\ldots,+,-
\]
across the island.

If the interior run has length \(1\), that is the visible adjacent-block extremum case.

If the interior run has length at least \(2\), that is precisely a **multi-critical excursion island**, hence a genuinely hidden reset.

So the hidden-reset problem is now equivalent to excluding those longer bracketed sign patterns in the critical-value ladder.

---

## 4. Upstairs interpretation via \(J_\gamma\) and \(\mathcal R\)

Upstairs on the opened residual arc,
\[
Q=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda},\qquad J_\gamma=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda=J_\lambda(Q-\gamma),
\qquad \frac{dQ}{dx}=\mathcal R.
\]

So a bounded \(\gamma\)-excursion island downstairs corresponds upstairs to an interval
\[
x_-<x_+
\]
such that

- \(J_\gamma(x_-)=J_\gamma(x_+)=0\),
- \(J_\gamma\) has one strict sign in \((x_-,x_+)\),
- and \(\mathcal R\) changes sign inside.

If there is only one odd zero of \(\mathcal R\) inside, that is the visible adjacent-block case.

If there are two or more odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\) inside, that is the genuine hidden-reset case.

So equivalently:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a regular hidden reset for one fixed }\gamma
\text{ is exactly a same-sign interval of }J_\gamma
\text{ bounded by two zeros of }J_\gamma
\text{ and containing at least two odd zeros of }\mathcal R.
}
\]

This is the cleanest upstairs reformulation I’ve got.

---

## 5. Minimal complexity of the first hidden reset

This gives a much sharper lower bound on what a hidden reset must cost.

A genuinely hidden reset requires:

1. a bounded \(\gamma\)-excursion island,
2. containing at least **two odd critical points** of \(\widehat Q\),
3. equivalently at least **two positive-side odd zeros** of \(\mathcal R\),
4. equivalently at least **four off-center odd zeros** of \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}\) upstairs.

So the first hidden reset is not merely “some critical-value overlap.” It is exactly the first **bounded multi-critical excursion island**.

---

## 6. Quartic–sextic consequence

Near a quartic–sextic residual point,
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)=\kappa_{2,2}\neq 0,
\]
so there is no critical point at the center. Therefore there are no local \(\gamma\)-excursion islands of any kind near the center.

So locally:

\[
\boxed{
\text{near the quartic--sextic center, every regular level is one-sheeted and there are no }\gamma\text{-excursion islands at all.}
}
\]

Any hidden reset must therefore start only after leaving the local quartic–sextic chart and creating the first true odd critical point, and in fact at least a second one if the reset is genuinely hidden.

---

## 7. What this means strategically

This aligns very well with the updated paper summary.

We have now reduced the residual quotient-cusp lane to:

- local no-reentry: done;
- single critical-point passage: classified, no hidden reset there;
- remaining loophole: only **bounded multi-critical excursion islands**.

So the live global question is no longer vague. It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{can }\widehat Q\text{ have a bounded }\gamma\text{-excursion island containing at least two odd critical points?}
}
\]

That is the exact form of the remaining no-hidden-reset problem.

I still do **not** see a proof from the current identities alone that such multi-critical excursion islands cannot occur.

So the math now stops at the exact reduction:

\[

\text{regular hidden reset }
\Longleftrightarrow
\text{bounded multi-critical }\gamma\text{-excursion island of }\widehat Q.
}

That is a real gain, but not yet the final exclusion.

The best next theorem to try is now extremely narrow:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every bounded }\gamma\text{-excursion island of }\widehat Q\text{ contains at most one odd critical point.}
}

That would immediately kill all regular hidden resets.

\[

## Assistant (2026-04-21 15:40:32)

I’m going back into the updated draft text itself now, especially the new \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\), critical-value, and no-hidden-reset sections, to see whether there is one more theorem hiding in the current setup rather than just another reformulation. The tight target is still the same: can a bounded multi-critical \(\gamma\)-excursion island be forced to contradict the existing fixed-\(\gamma\) package, or does the paper genuinely stop exactly there?

I found a genuine sharpening. The first hidden reset is more complicated than I had said before: it does **not** begin at two odd critical points. For a regular level \(\gamma\), any bounded \(\gamma\)-excursion island contains an **odd** number of odd critical points of \(\widehat Q\), so the visible case is \(1\) and the first genuinely hidden case is at least \(3\). That tightens the zero budget quite a bit.

_Thought for 1m 44s_

I found a real sharpening, and it improves the updated “no-hidden-reset / excursion-island” picture in an important way.

The key theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{for a regular level }\gamma,\ \text{every bounded }\gamma\text{-excursion island contains an \emph{odd} number of odd critical points of }\widehat Q.
}
\]

So the visible adjacent-block case is exactly the case of **one** odd critical point, and the first genuinely hidden reset requires **at least three** odd critical points, not merely two.

That is a substantial tightening.

---

## 1. Setup

Work on a connected opened residual quotient interval \(I\) in the coordinate
\[
u=t^2,
\qquad
Q(t)=\widehat Q(u),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u).
\]

Fix a **regular** level \(\gamma\), so
\[
\gamma\neq \widehat Q(z)
\]
for every critical point \(z\) of \(\widehat Q\).

Let \(C=(a,b)\subset I\) be a bounded connected component of either
\[
E_\gamma^+:=\{u:\widehat Q(u)>\gamma\}
\qquad\text{or}\qquad
E_\gamma^-:=\{u:\widehat Q(u)<\gamma\}.
\]
Then
\[
\widehat Q(a)=\widehat Q(b)=\gamma,
\]
and \(\widehat Q-\gamma\) has one strict sign on \((a,b)\).

I’ll call such a component a bounded \(\gamma\)-excursion island.

---

## 2. Boundary derivative signs are forced

If \(C\subset E_\gamma^+\), then inside the interval we have
\[
\widehat Q(u)>\gamma.
\]
Because \(\gamma\) is regular, the boundary hits are transverse. So necessarily
\[
\widehat Q'(a)>0,
\qquad
\widehat Q'(b)<0.
\]
Indeed, at the left boundary the function must cross from below \(\gamma\) to above \(\gamma\), and at the right boundary it must cross back from above to below.

Similarly, if \(C\subset E_\gamma^-\), then
\[
\widehat Q'(a)<0,
\qquad
\widehat Q'(b)>0.
\]

So in either case, the sign of \(\widehat Q'\) at the two ends of the island is opposite.

That is the starting point.

---

## 3. Odd-critical-point parity on an excursion island

Now track the sign of
\[
\widehat Q'(u)=\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)
\]
as \(u\) runs from \(a\) to \(b\).

- At an **odd-order** zero of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\), the sign of \(\widehat Q'\) changes.
- At an **even-order** zero, the sign of \(\widehat Q'\) does not change.

Since the derivative sign at the two ends of the island is opposite, the total number of sign changes of \(\widehat Q'\) on \((a,b)\) must be odd.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every bounded regular }\gamma\text{-excursion island contains an odd number of odd critical points of }\widehat Q.
}
\]

That is the theorem.

---

## 4. Immediate consequence: the first hidden reset needs at least three odd critical points

Now combine this with the earlier classification.

- If a bounded \(\gamma\)-excursion island contains exactly **one** odd critical point, then it is just the visible local extremum case. The two boundary hits lie in the two blocks adjacent to that one extremum.
- A genuinely hidden reset requires the two boundary hits to lie in nonadjacent monotonicity blocks.

Since the number of odd critical points in the island must be odd, the next possibility after \(1\) is \(3\).

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{for a regular level }\gamma,\ \text{the first genuinely hidden reset requires at least three odd critical points of }\widehat Q.
}
\]

This is the main strengthening.

It improves the earlier lower bound of “at least two.”

---

## 5. Critical-value-ladder version

Let
\[
z_1<z_2<\cdots<z_N
\]
be the critical points of \(\widehat Q\), and let
\[
c_j=\widehat Q(z_j).
\]

A bounded \(\gamma\)-excursion island corresponds to a consecutive sign pattern of the ladder
\[
c_j-\gamma
\]
of the form
\[
+,-,\ldots,-,+ \qquad\text{or}\qquad -, +,\ldots,+,-.
\]

Now the theorem above says that within such an island, the number of **odd** critical points must be odd.

So the visible case is the shortest possible pattern, with exactly one odd critical point in the interior. The first genuinely hidden case needs a longer interior run with at least three odd critical points.

In particular, a hidden reset cannot be created by a single local extremum plus one additional inert critical point. Even-order critical points do not help with the sign-change budget.

So the no-hidden-reset problem has become more rigid:

\[
\boxed{
\text{nonadjacent overlap requires a three-extrema excursion island, not just a two-critical-point oscillation.}
}
\]

---

## 6. Upstairs \(\mathcal R\)-interpretation

On opened residual arcs we already have
\[
\frac{dQ}{dx}=\mathcal R.
\]

Odd critical points of \(\widehat Q\) correspond exactly to positive-side odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\) where the sign changes. So the theorem becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every bounded regular }\gamma\text{-excursion island contains an odd number of odd zeros of }\mathcal R.
}
\]

Hence the visible case is one odd zero of \(\mathcal R\), and the first genuinely hidden reset requires at least **three** positive-side odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\).

Equivalently, upstairs on the full opened residual arc, the first hidden reset requires at least **six** off-center odd zeros of \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}\).

That is a much sharper zero budget than before.

---

## 7. \(J_\gamma\)-interval reformulation

Recall
\[
J_\gamma=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda = J_\lambda(Q-\gamma).
\]

So a bounded regular \(\gamma\)-excursion island corresponds upstairs to a same-sign interval of \(J_\gamma\) bounded by two zeros of \(J_\gamma\).

The theorem above then says:

\[
\boxed{
\text{between two consecutive boundary zeros of such a same-sign interval of }J_\gamma,
\text{ the number of odd zeros of }\mathcal R\text{ is odd.}
}
\]

Visible adjacent-block pairing corresponds to exactly one odd \(\mathcal R\)-zero inside.  
A genuinely hidden reset requires at least three.

This is a very clean upstairs formulation.

---

## 8. Quartic–sextic consequence

Near a quartic–sextic residual point we know
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)=\kappa_{2,2}\neq 0,
\]
so there is not even a critical point at the center. Therefore there are no local \(\gamma\)-excursion islands at all near the center.

So the first possible hidden reset cannot even start locally. It must begin only after leaving the local quartic–sextic chart and generating:

1. a first odd critical point of \(\widehat Q\),
2. then a second,
3. then a third,

before a genuinely hidden regular reset is even combinatorially possible.

That is a strong local-to-global constraint.

---

## 9. Why this matters

This sharpens the updated draft’s “critical-value ordering / no-hidden-reset” frontier a lot.

We can now say:

- regular levels are fully visible in the ladder;
- a single critical point is harmless;
- the visible adjacent-block case is one odd critical point;
- the first genuinely hidden reset requires a **three-extrema excursion island**.

So the remaining loophole is smaller than before:

\[
\boxed{
\text{exclude bounded }\gamma\text{-excursion islands containing at least three odd critical points of }\widehat Q.
}
\]

That is the new exact target.

---

## 10. Honest boundary

I still do **not** see a proof from the current identities alone that such three-extrema excursion islands cannot occur.

So the current math stops at the sharpened reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{regular hidden reset }
\Longleftrightarrow
\text{bounded regular }\gamma\text{-excursion island with at least three odd critical points.}
}
\]

That is better than the previous “at least two” formulation, but still not the final exclusion.

---

## 11. Best next attack

The best next theorem to try is now extremely tight:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every bounded regular }\gamma\text{-excursion island contains at most one odd critical point.}
}
\]

A weaker but still useful intermediate target would be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a bounded regular }\gamma\text{-excursion island containing exactly three odd critical points cannot occur.}
}
\]

That would already kill the first nontrivial hidden-reset pattern.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 15:56:10)

I’m checking the updated draft text directly now, especially the new \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\), critical-value, and excursion-island sections, so I can push one more step without leaning on stale formulations. The narrow target remains the same: whether the first possible hidden-reset pattern, the three-extrema \(\gamma\)-excursion island, can already be contradicted by the current fixed-\(\gamma\) and \(\mathcal R\) packages.

_Thought for 3m 56s_

I found a real sharpening, and it fits the updated **critical-value / no-hidden-reset** framing very well.

The new gain is that any bounded regular \(\gamma\)-excursion island is not just forced to contain an odd number of odd critical points. It is forced to carry a very rigid **alternating lobe-mass word with one-sided partial sums**.

That is the sharpest combinatorial reduction I can currently justify.

---

## 1. Setup: a bounded regular \(\gamma\)-excursion island

Work on a connected opened residual quotient interval in the coordinate
\[
u=t^2,
\qquad
Q(t)=\widehat Q(u),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u).
\]

Fix a **regular** level \(\gamma\), so
\[
\gamma\neq \widehat Q(z)
\]
for every critical point \(z\) of \(\widehat Q\).

Let \(C=(a,b)\) be a bounded connected component of either
\[
E_\gamma^+:=\{u:\widehat Q(u)>\gamma\}
\qquad\text{or}\qquad
E_\gamma^-:=\{u:\widehat Q(u)<\gamma\}.
\]
Then
\[
\widehat Q(a)=\widehat Q(b)=\gamma,
\]
and \(\widehat Q-\gamma\) has one strict sign on \((a,b)\).

As before, this is a bounded regular \(\gamma\)-excursion island.

---

## 2. Odd critical points and lobe decomposition

List the **odd** critical points of \(\widehat Q\) inside \(C\) in increasing order:
\[
a<\xi_1<\cdots<\xi_{2m+1}<b.
\]
We already proved the number of odd critical points is odd, so this notation is legitimate.

Set
\[
\xi_0:=a,
\qquad
\xi_{2m+2}:=b.
\]
On each interval
\[
(\xi_j,\xi_{j+1}),
\qquad j=0,\dots,2m+1,
\]
the derivative \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\) has fixed sign. Define the corresponding lobe masses
\[
L_j:=\int_{\xi_j}^{\xi_{j+1}}\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)\,du
=
\widehat Q(\xi_{j+1})-\widehat Q(\xi_j).
\]

Then:

- the signs of the \(L_j\) alternate,
- and the partial sums are exactly the excursion heights above or below \(\gamma\).

That is the key observation.

---

## 3. The alternating partial-sum theorem

Assume first that \(C\subset E_\gamma^+\), so \(\widehat Q>\gamma\) on \((a,b)\).

Then at the left boundary,
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(a)>0,
\]
and at the right boundary,
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(b)<0.
\]
Since each odd critical point flips the sign of \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\), the lobe masses satisfy
\[
L_0>0,\quad L_1<0,\quad L_2>0,\ \dots,\ L_{2m}>0,\quad L_{2m+1}<0.
\]

Now define the partial sums
\[
S_k:=\sum_{j=0}^{k-1}L_j
=
\widehat Q(\xi_k)-\gamma,
\qquad
k=1,\dots,2m+2.
\]

Because \(\widehat Q(\xi_k)>\gamma\) for every interior point \(\xi_k\), we get
\[
S_k>0
\qquad
(1\le k\le 2m+1),
\]
while at the right boundary
\[
S_{2m+2}=0.
\]

So the entire bounded superlevel island is encoded by an alternating sign word
\[
+,-,+,-,\dots,+,-
\]
whose proper partial sums are all strictly positive and whose total sum is zero.

Similarly, if \(C\subset E_\gamma^-\), then
\[
L_0<0,\quad L_1>0,\quad \dots,\quad L_{2m+1}>0,
\]
and the proper partial sums are all strictly negative until the final return to zero.

So the theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every bounded regular }\gamma\text{-excursion island determines an alternating lobe-mass word}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
L_0,-L_1,L_2,\dots
\text{ with all proper partial sums of one fixed sign and total sum }0.
}
\]

That is the main new result.

---

## 4. First hidden reset pattern

This immediately sharpens the previous “at least three odd critical points” statement.

The visible case is \(m=0\): exactly one odd critical point, hence two lobes
\[
L_0>0,\quad L_1<0,
\qquad
L_0+L_1=0.
\]
That is just the ordinary adjacent-block extremum.

The first genuinely hidden case is \(m=1\): three odd critical points. Then there are four lobes
\[
L_0>0,\quad L_1<0,\quad L_2>0,\quad L_3<0,
\]
with
\[
L_0>0,
\qquad
L_0+L_1>0,
\qquad
L_0+L_1+L_2>0,
\qquad
L_0+L_1+L_2+L_3=0.
\]

So the first hidden reset is not merely “three odd critical points.” It is exactly a **four-lobe alternating excursion word with one-sided partial sums**.

That is substantially more rigid.

---

## 5. Critical-value-ladder version

Let the odd critical points in the island be
\[
\xi_1,\xi_2,\xi_3
\]
for the first hidden case, and write their critical values
\[
c_i:=\widehat Q(\xi_i).
\]

If the island is above \(\gamma\), then
\[
c_1>\gamma,\qquad c_2>\gamma,\qquad c_3>\gamma,
\]
with alternating extremum type
\[
\text{max},\ \text{min},\ \text{max}.
\]

So the first hidden reset requires a critical-value ladder fragment of the form
\[
c_1>c_2<c_3,
\qquad
c_1,c_2,c_3>\gamma.
\]

Likewise below \(\gamma\), one gets
\[
c_1<\gamma,\qquad c_2<\gamma,\qquad c_3<\gamma,
\]
with pattern
\[
\text{min},\ \text{max},\ \text{min}.
\]

So the hidden-reset problem is now more precise than “nonadjacent block overlap.” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{can the critical-value ladder carry a same-side three-extrema island around one regular level }\gamma?
}
\]

That is a much tighter target.

---

## 6. Upstairs \(\mathcal R\)-lobe interpretation

On opened residual arcs we already have
\[
\frac{dQ}{dx}=\mathcal R.
\]
So the lobe masses above are exactly
\[
L_j=\int_{x(\xi_j)}^{x(\xi_{j+1})}\mathcal R(x)\,dx.
\]

Thus the theorem becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every bounded regular }\gamma\text{-excursion island corresponds upstairs to alternating signed }\mathcal R\text{-lobe masses}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{with all proper partial sums of one fixed sign and total sum }0.
}
\]

So the first hidden reset requires four consecutive \(\mathcal R\)-lobes with sign pattern
\[
+,-,+,- \quad\text{or}\quad -,+,-,+,
\]
and with all proper partial sums one-sided until the final cancellation.

This is the cleanest \(\mathcal R\)-language I have so far.

---

## 7. Why this matters

This sharpens the updated draft’s frontier substantially.

We are no longer asking vaguely whether the critical values can oscillate enough. We now know the exact combinatorial shape required for the first hidden reset:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a bounded regular }\gamma\text{-excursion island with three odd critical points}
}
\]
is equivalent to
\[
\boxed{
\text{an alternating four-lobe word }(+,-,+,-)\text{ or }(-,+,-,+)
\text{ whose proper partial sums never change sign and whose total sum is }0.
}
\]

That is a very specific global pattern.

If one can show that the \(\mathcal R\)-lobe masses on an opened residual arc cannot satisfy that pattern, then the first hidden-reset case dies immediately.

---

## 8. Quartic–sextic consequence

Near the quartic–sextic center,
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)=\kappa_{2,2}\neq 0,
\]
so there is not even a critical point there. Thus no local excursion island exists at all.

So any hidden-reset pattern must begin only after leaving the local quartic–sextic chart and generating the first four-lobe alternating excursion word farther out.

That is a strong local-to-global constraint.

---

## 9. Honest boundary

I still do **not** see, from the current identities alone, a proof that such a four-lobe alternating excursion word cannot occur.

So the math now stops at this sharper reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first regular hidden reset }
\Longleftrightarrow
\text{a four-lobe alternating }\mathcal R\text{-excursion word with one-sided partial sums.}
}
\]

That is better than the previous “at least three odd critical points” formulation, but still not the final exclusion.

---

## 10. Best next attack

The next theorem to attack is now extremely focused:

\[
\boxed{
\text{exclude the four-lobe alternating excursion word }(+,-,+,-)\text{ and }(-,+,-,+)
\text{ on an opened residual arc.}
}
\]

A slightly weaker but still useful version would be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove that the magnitudes of consecutive }\mathcal R\text{-lobe masses cannot satisfy the strict partial-sum inequalities needed for that word.}
}
\]

That feels like the sharpest next move.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 16:02:13)

I’m checking the updated draft’s \(\widehat Q\), \(\mathcal R\)-lobe, and fixed-\(\gamma\) sections directly now so I can squeeze one more genuine theorem out of the current setup rather than just rephrase the same reduction. The target is very narrow: the first hidden-reset pattern, i.e. the four-lobe alternating excursion word.

_Thought for 2m 3s_

I found a genuinely useful sharpening, and it fits the updated **critical-value ordering / no-hidden-reset** framing very well.

The new result is a **reduction theorem**:

\[
\boxed{
\text{any regular hidden reset either already contains a three-extrema excursion island, or else it is forced by a critical-value tie.}
}
\]

So in the generic “strict critical-value ordering” regime, you do **not** have to exclude arbitrary long excursion islands. It is enough to exclude the **first hidden case**, namely the three-extrema island.

That is a real step forward.

---

## 1. Setup

Work on a connected opened residual quotient interval in the quotient coordinate
\[
u=t^2,
\qquad
Q(t)=\widehat Q(u),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u).
\]

Fix a **regular** level \(\gamma\), and let \(C=(a,b)\) be a bounded \(\gamma\)-excursion island, say
\[
C\subset \{\,\widehat Q>\gamma\,\}.
\]
(The sublevel case is identical with signs reversed.)

Assume \(C\) contains \(2m+1\) odd critical points of \(\widehat Q\), listed in order
\[
a<\xi_1<\xi_2<\cdots<\xi_{2m+1}<b.
\]
Since \(C\) is a superlevel island, these alternate as
\[
\text{max},\ \text{min},\ \text{max},\ \dots,\ \text{min},\ \text{max}.
\]
Write the internal minimum critical values as
\[
\mu_i:=\widehat Q(\xi_{2i}),
\qquad i=1,\dots,m.
\]

A genuinely hidden reset means \(m\ge 1\), and the first hidden case is \(m=1\), i.e. exactly three odd critical points.

---

## 2. Three-extrema reduction theorem

Let
\[
\mu_*:=\max_{1\le i\le m}\mu_i.
\]

### Proposition
If the maximum \(\mu_*\) is attained at a unique index \(k\), then for every level
\[
\gamma' \quad\text{with}\quad \max_{i\neq k}\mu_i<\gamma'<\mu_*,
\]
the set
\[
\{\widehat Q>\gamma'\}\cap C
\]
has a connected component \(C'\subset C\) which is a bounded regular \(\gamma'\)-excursion island containing exactly the three odd critical points
\[
\xi_{2k-1},\ \xi_{2k},\ \xi_{2k+1}.
\]

So any larger regular hidden-reset island reduces to the first hidden case unless the top internal minimum value is repeated.

---

## 3. Proof sketch

Since \(\gamma'<\mu_*=\widehat Q(\xi_{2k})\), the middle minimum \(\xi_{2k}\) lies inside the superlevel set
\[
\{\widehat Q>\gamma'\}.
\]
Hence the adjacent maxima \(\xi_{2k-1},\xi_{2k+1}\) and the minimum \(\xi_{2k}\) all lie in the same connected component of that superlevel set.

On the other hand, for every other internal minimum \(\xi_{2i}\) with \(i\neq k\),
\[
\widehat Q(\xi_{2i})=\mu_i<\gamma',
\]
so each such minimum lies below the new level \(\gamma'\) and therefore cuts the superlevel set there. Thus the component containing \(\xi_{2k}\) cannot pass through any other internal minimum.

Therefore that component contains exactly one internal minimum and its two adjacent maxima, i.e. exactly three odd critical points.

That proves the claim.

---

## 4. Consequence: generic hidden reset reduces to the three-extrema case

This yields the clean corollary:

\[
\boxed{
\text{if all internal minimum critical values are distinct, every regular hidden reset contains a smaller regular hidden reset with exactly three odd critical points.}
}
\]

In other words, under generic critical-value ordering, the entire hidden-reset problem reduces to ruling out the **three-extrema \(\gamma\)-excursion island**.

That matches the updated draft’s goal extremely well: the global lane becomes a finite critical-value ordering problem, and the first nontrivial obstruction is already the whole generic obstruction.

---

## 5. The only nongeneric loophole: critical-value ties

What happens if the reduction above fails? Exactly one thing:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the maximal internal minimum value }\mu_*
\text{ is attained at least twice.}
}
\]

So any regular hidden reset that cannot be reduced to a three-extrema island must come from a **critical-value tie among internal minima**.

Thus the hidden-reset problem now splits cleanly into two cases:

1. **generic case**: strict internal minimum ordering  
   \(\Rightarrow\) reduce to a three-extrema island;

2. **nongeneric case**: repeated internal minimum critical values  
   \(\Rightarrow\) a tie in the critical-value ladder.

This is the sharpest decomposition I’ve found.

---

## 6. Sublevel version

If instead
\[
C\subset \{\,\widehat Q<\gamma\,\},
\]
the same argument holds with maxima and minima interchanged.

Let the internal maxima be
\[
M_i:=\widehat Q(\xi_{2i}),
\]
and suppose the minimum of these internal maxima is attained uniquely. Then lowering the level to an intermediate \(\gamma'\) isolates a three-extrema sublevel island.

So the full statement is symmetric:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every regular hidden reset either reduces to a three-extrema island, or else is forced by a critical-value tie of the relevant internal extrema.}
}
\]

---

## 7. Upstairs interpretation via \(\mathcal R\)

Because on opened residual arcs
\[
\frac{dQ}{dx}=\mathcal R,
\]
a three-extrema superlevel island corresponds upstairs to a same-sign interval of
\[
J_\gamma=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda
\]
bounded by two zeros of \(J_\gamma\) and containing exactly **three** odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\):

\[
\text{max-min-max downstairs}
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\text{three odd }\mathcal R\text{-zeros upstairs}.
\]

So in generic critical-value ordering, the first hidden reset is exactly the first interval of \(J_\gamma\) that encloses **three** odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\).

That is a very clean upstairs reformulation.

---

## 8. Why this matters

This sharpens the remaining frontier a lot.

Before, the live target was:

- exclude bounded regular \(\gamma\)-excursion islands with at least three odd critical points.

Now it is narrower:

\[
\boxed{
\text{generic case: exclude just the three-extrema island;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{nongeneric case: understand critical-value ties.}
}
\]

So the updated draft’s “critical-value ordering / no-hidden-reset” program can be split into:

1. a **strict-ordering case**, where the first hidden case is already decisive;
2. a **tie case**, where repeated critical values themselves are the obstruction.

That is much cleaner than treating arbitrary long islands all at once.

---

## 9. Honest boundary

I still do **not** see a proof from the current identities alone that a three-extrema island cannot occur. And I do not yet see a proof that the relevant internal minimum/maximum critical values cannot tie.

So the current attack now stops at this sharper reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{regular hidden reset }
\Longrightarrow
\text{either a three-extrema excursion island or a critical-value tie.}
}
\]

That is better than the previous “at least three odd critical points” reduction.

---

## 10. Best next attack

The best next theorem to try is now very focused:

### Candidate theorem A
A three-extrema regular \(\gamma\)-excursion island cannot occur.

That would close the entire **generic** hidden-reset case at once.

### Candidate theorem B
The internal critical values relevant to a hidden reset cannot tie.

That would close the **nongeneric** loophole.

If one of those falls, the whole residual no-hidden-reset problem gets dramatically smaller.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 16:05:50)

I’m pushing on the two remaining loopholes in the updated framing: the generic three-extrema excursion island, and the nongeneric critical-value tie case. I’m checking the actual draft labels and setup as I go so I can target something the current machinery might genuinely close, rather than just restating the reduction.

_Thought for 3m 19s_

I found one more real sharpening, and it makes the **critical-value tie** case much cleaner.

The new point is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a critical-value tie is not a separate first-order loophole; it is the boundary of a \emph{higher-complexity} regular excursion island.}
}
\]

More precisely, the nongeneric tie case cannot occur at the same minimal complexity as the generic hidden-reset case. The first generic hidden reset is a **three-extrema island**; the first tie-driven hidden-reset case perturbs to a **regular island with at least five odd critical points**.

That is a useful reduction.

---

## 1. Setup: a superlevel hidden-reset island and the tie obstruction

Work on a connected opened residual quotient interval in the quotient coordinate
\[
u=t^2,
\qquad
Q(t)=\widehat Q(u),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u).
\]

Suppose we are in the superlevel case, and let
\[
C=(a,b)\subset \{\widehat Q>\gamma\}
\]
be a bounded \(\gamma\)-excursion island. List the odd critical points in \(C\) as
\[
a<\xi_1<\xi_2<\cdots<\xi_{2m+1}<b,
\]
so the odd-indexed ones are local maxima and the even-indexed ones are local minima. Write the internal minima as
\[
\mu_i:=\widehat Q(\xi_{2i}),
\qquad i=1,\dots,m.
\]

Earlier we proved:

- if the maximum of the \(\mu_i\) is attained uniquely, then one can lower the level slightly and isolate a smaller regular hidden-reset island with exactly three odd critical points;
- so the only way that three-extrema reduction can fail is if the top internal minimum value ties:
  \[
  \mu_{i_1}=\mu_{i_2}=\mu_*=\max_i\mu_i
  \]
  for at least two indices.

The question is: what does that tie case really mean geometrically?

---

## 2. Tie case perturbs to a regular island with at least five odd critical points

Pick two distinct indices \(i<j\) such that
\[
\mu_i=\mu_j=\mu_*,
\]
and choose them consecutive among the tied indices. Consider the chain of odd critical points from the local maximum just before \(\xi_{2i}\) to the local maximum just after \(\xi_{2j}\):
\[
\xi_{2i-1},\ \xi_{2i},\ \xi_{2i+1},\ \ldots,\ \xi_{2j},\ \xi_{2j+1}.
\]

Let
\[
\nu:=\min\{\mu_k: i\le k\le j\}.
\]
Then \(\nu\le \mu_*\). Choose a regular level
\[
\gamma' < \nu
\]
sufficiently close to \(\nu\) but not equal to any critical value.

Since every internal minimum in that chain lies above \(\gamma'\), the superlevel set
\[
\{\widehat Q>\gamma'\}
\]
contains a connected component \(C'\subset C\) that runs from the left side of \(\xi_{2i-1}\) to the right side of \(\xi_{2j+1}\), and \(C'\) contains all odd critical points in that chain.

So \(C'\) is a bounded **regular** \(\gamma'\)-excursion island containing
\[
2(j-i)+3
\]
odd critical points. Because \(j\ge i+1\), this number is at least
\[
5.
\]

Hence:

\[
\boxed{
\text{if the three-extrema reduction fails because of a repeated top internal minimum, then after a nearby regular level perturbation one gets a bounded regular excursion island with at least five odd critical points.}
}
\]

That is the theorem.

The sublevel case is identical with maxima and minima interchanged.

---

## 3. Consequence: tie case is strictly higher complexity than the generic hidden-reset case

This gives the clean dichotomy:

### Generic case
If the relevant internal extrema are strictly ordered, every regular hidden reset reduces to the first hidden case:
\[
\boxed{
\text{a bounded regular excursion island with exactly three odd critical points.}
}
\]

### Nongeneric tie case
If the reduction fails because of a critical-value tie, then the tie is not a separate first-complexity phenomenon. It is the boundary of a regular hidden-reset island with at least
\[
\boxed{
5\text{ odd critical points.}
}
\]

So the first hidden-reset pattern is still the three-extrema case; the tie case starts only at higher complexity.

That is the main gain.

---

## 4. Critical-value-ladder version

In the language of the critical-value ladder
\[
c_j=\widehat Q(z_j),
\qquad \widehat{\mathcal D}(z_j)=0,
\]
this says:

- if a hidden reset occurs with strict ordering of the relevant internal extrema, then there is already a three-extrema sign pattern;
- if strict ordering fails because some internal minima/maxima tie, then after perturbing the level slightly into the regular regime, the first resulting excursion island contains at least five odd critical points.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{critical-value ties are limits of higher-complexity regular excursion islands, not an independent low-complexity loophole.}
}
\]

That fits the updated paper’s “critical-value ordering” language very well.

---

## 5. Upstairs interpretation via \(\mathcal R\)

On opened residual arcs we have
\[
\frac{dQ}{dx}=\mathcal R.
\]
Odd critical points of \(\widehat Q\) correspond exactly to positive-side odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\).

So the theorem becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a tie-driven hidden-reset case perturbs to a regular interval containing at least five positive-side odd zeros of }\mathcal R.
}
\]

Equivalently, upstairs on the full opened residual arc, it forces at least
\[
\boxed{
10\text{ off-center odd zeros of }\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}.
}
\]

This is a much sharper complexity budget than we had before.

For comparison:

- visible adjacent-block case: \(1\) odd critical point;
- first generic hidden reset: \(3\) odd critical points;
- first tie-driven hidden reset: at least \(5\) odd critical points after perturbation.

That is a nice hierarchy.

---

## 6. Why this matters

This improves the updated no-hidden-reset picture in a useful way.

We can now say:

\[
\boxed{
\text{to close the generic case, it suffices to exclude the three-extrema regular excursion island;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{and the critical-value tie case is automatically higher-complexity, not a competing first-order obstruction.}
}
\]

So the true first obstruction is still the three-extrema island.

That means Candidate theorem A from the previous step really is the right target. If you can kill the three-extrema regular island, you kill the first generic hidden reset; the nongeneric tie case only begins later and can be treated as a separate, strictly higher-complexity issue.

---

## 7. Honest boundary

I still do **not** see a proof from the current identities alone that either

- the three-extrema regular island cannot occur, or
- the tie case itself cannot occur.

What I do see is this sharper reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every hidden reset is either a three-extrema regular island, or a critical-value tie that is the boundary of a regular island with at least five odd critical points.}
}
\]

That is better than the previous “three-extrema or tie” split.

---

## 8. Best next attack

The best next theorem to attack is still the same, but now with better justification:

\[
\boxed{
\text{exclude the three-extrema regular }\gamma\text{-excursion island.}
}
\]

That would settle the entire generic case, and it would identify the tie case as a genuinely higher-order residual phenomenon rather than a first hidden-reset loophole.

A secondary target would then be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{exclude repeated internal minimum/maximum critical values on the residual quotient branch.}
}
\]

That would clean up the remaining nongeneric tie case.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 16:11:42)

I’m zeroing in on the first genuinely live loophole now: the regular three-extrema \(\gamma\)-excursion island. _Thought for 1m 47s_

I found a real sharpening, and it makes the **first generic hidden-reset case** much more concrete.

The key new fact is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a regular three-extrema }\gamma\text{-excursion island is exactly a \emph{binary merger} of two visible one-extremum islands across one central odd critical value.}
}
\]

So the first hidden reset is not an arbitrary four-lobe pattern. It is the merger of **two ordinary adjacent-block visible islands** through one middle critical point.

That is the cleanest reduction I’ve gotten.

---

## 1. Three-extrema superlevel island

Work on a connected opened residual quotient interval in the quotient coordinate
\[
u=t^2,
\qquad
Q(t)=\widehat Q(u),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u).
\]

Suppose \(C=(a,b)\subset\{\widehat Q>\gamma\}\) is a bounded regular \(\gamma\)-excursion island containing exactly three odd critical points
\[
a<\xi_1<\xi_2<\xi_3<b.
\]
Then necessarily
\[
\xi_1,\xi_3 \text{ are local maxima},\qquad \xi_2 \text{ is a local minimum},
\]
and if we write
\[
M_1:=\widehat Q(\xi_1),\qquad m:=\widehat Q(\xi_2),\qquad M_3:=\widehat Q(\xi_3),
\]
then
\[
M_1>m>\gamma,\qquad M_3>m>\gamma.
\]

So the island sits strictly below the side maxima and strictly below the middle minimum.

---

## 2. Binary-merger theorem

Now choose a level
\[
\gamma' \quad\text{with}\quad m<\gamma'<\min(M_1,M_3).
\]

Then inside the same interval \(C\), the superlevel set
\[
\{\widehat Q>\gamma'\}\cap C
\]
has exactly **two** connected components:

- one containing \(\xi_1\),
- one containing \(\xi_3\),

and each of those components is a bounded regular \(\gamma'\)-excursion island containing exactly **one** odd critical point.

In other words:

\[
\boxed{
\text{for every }\gamma'\in(m,\min(M_1,M_3)),
\text{ the three-extrema island splits into two visible adjacent-block islands.}
}
\]

As \(\gamma'\downarrow m\), those two visible islands meet exactly at the middle critical point \(\xi_2\). For \(\gamma<m\), they are merged into the single hidden three-extrema island.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first generic hidden reset is exactly a merger of two visible one-extremum islands across the central odd critical value }m.
}
\]

This is the main new result.

---

## 3. Proof

Since \(\xi_2\) is a strict local minimum with value \(m\), any level \(\gamma'>m\) cuts through the graph on both sides of \(\xi_2\), so \(\xi_2\) lies outside the superlevel set \(\{\widehat Q>\gamma'\}\).

On the other hand, since \(\gamma'<M_1\) and \(\gamma'<M_3\), the side maxima \(\xi_1,\xi_3\) each lie inside the superlevel set. Because there are no other odd critical points in \(C\), the superlevel set inside \(C\) has exactly one component around \(\xi_1\) and exactly one around \(\xi_3\), and each contains exactly one odd critical point.

That proves the claim.

The sublevel case is identical with maxima and minima interchanged.

---

## 4. What this means

This sharpens the current “three-extrema excursion island” picture a lot.

Before, the first generic hidden reset was:

- a bounded regular excursion island,
- with exactly three odd critical points,
- equivalently a four-lobe alternating word.

Now we know more:

\[
\boxed{
\text{it is not an isolated exotic object; it is the post-merger of two ordinary visible islands.}
}
\]

So the generic hidden-reset problem reduces to a very concrete **binary merger problem**:

> can two visible adjacent-block excursion islands merge across one central odd critical value to form a hidden three-extrema island?

That is much sharper than “exclude arbitrary nonadjacent overlap.”

---

## 5. Critical-value-ladder version

In the critical-value ladder, the three-extrema superlevel island corresponds to
\[
M_1>m<M_3,
\qquad
m>\gamma.
\]

For levels \(\gamma'\) with
\[
m<\gamma'<\min(M_1,M_3),
\]
the middle minimum sits below the level, so the island splits into two visible pieces. As soon as the level passes below \(m\), those two visible pieces merge into one hidden island.

So the first hidden-reset mechanism is exactly a **critical-value crossing merger** at one central minimum (or maximum in the sublevel case).

That matches the new draft framing perfectly.

---

## 6. Upstairs interpretation via \(\mathcal R\)

On opened residual arcs,
\[
\frac{dQ}{dx}=\mathcal R.
\]
So a three-extrema island corresponds upstairs to an interval where \(Q-\gamma\) has one sign, is zero at both ends, and \(\mathcal R\) has exactly three odd zeros inside:
\[
+\,-\,+\,- \quad\text{in lobe language.}
\]

The binary-merger theorem says that for every slightly higher level \(\gamma'>m\), that interval splits into two visible \(J_{\gamma'}\)-intervals, each enclosing exactly one odd zero of \(\mathcal R\).

So the first hidden reset upstairs is exactly:

\[
\boxed{
\text{two visible fixed-level intervals, each around one odd }\mathcal R\text{-zero, merged by lowering the level through a central odd }\mathcal R\text{-zero.}
}
\]

That is a very clean source-level picture.

---

## 7. Why this matters

This reduces the generic no-hidden-reset problem further.

We no longer need to think of the first hidden reset as an arbitrary four-lobe alternating word. It is precisely:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a binary merger of two visible one-extremum islands across one central odd critical value.}
}
\]

So the next attack can focus on excluding that merger mechanism directly.

That feels much more tractable than excluding all possible longer excursion patterns at once.

---

## 8. Honest boundary

I still do **not** see a proof from the current identities alone that this binary merger cannot occur.

So the current math stops at the sharper reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{generic hidden reset }
\Longleftrightarrow
\text{binary merger of two visible islands across one central odd critical value.}
}
\]

That is better than the previous “three-extrema island” reduction.

---

## 9. Best next attack

The best next theorem to try is now extremely focused:

\[
\boxed{
\text{exclude the binary merger of two visible adjacent-block islands across one central odd critical value.}
}
\]

A concrete way to phrase it is:

> if \(M_1>m<M_3\) are three consecutive odd critical values of \(\widehat Q\), then lowering the level through \(m\) cannot merge the two visible \(\gamma'\)-excursion islands into a hidden regular \(\gamma\)-excursion island on the residual branch.

A weaker but still useful version would be:

> the two visible fixed-\(\gamma'\) sheared branch segments on either side of the central odd critical point cannot glue into one hidden-reset pattern after crossing the critical value.

That is where I would keep pushing next.

_Thought for 1m 22s_

I found a real sharpening, and it clarifies exactly what happens at the **central critical value** in the first generic hidden-reset pattern.

The key point is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the binary merger across one central odd critical value is exactly a saddle-node annihilation/creation of two simple zeros of }J_\gamma,
}
\]
through a **sterile even zero** of \(J_\gamma\).

That means the critical-level passage itself is completely local and visible. The remaining difficulty is only the **global gluing** of the two side visible islands after this local merger.

That is a meaningful reduction.

---

## 1. Local model at one odd critical point of \(\widehat Q\)

Let \(z_*\) be an odd critical point of \(\widehat Q\), so
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(z_*)=\widehat Q'(z_*)=0
\]
with odd order
\[
\ord_{z_*}\widehat{\mathcal D}=2\ell-1
\qquad (\ell\ge 1).
\]
Set
\[
m_*:=\widehat Q(z_*).
\]

Then locally
\[
\widehat Q(u)-m_*=(u-z_*)^{2\ell}\,\widetilde L(u),
\qquad
\widetilde L(z_*)\neq 0.
\]

So for a local minimum,
\[
\widehat Q(u)-m_*\sim a(u-z_*)^{2\ell},
\qquad a>0,
\]
and for a local maximum the same holds with \(a<0\).

This is the exact local catastrophe model.

---

## 2. Passage through the critical value in terms of \(J_\gamma\)

Recall on the opened residual arc
\[
Q=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda},
\qquad
J_\gamma:=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda
=
J_\lambda(Q-\gamma).
\]
Since \(J_\lambda\neq 0\) there, the zeros of \(J_\gamma\) are exactly the zeros of \(Q-\gamma\).

Now fix the local minimum case first. Then near \(z_*\):

- if \(\gamma>m_*\) and \(\gamma-m_*>0\) is small, the equation
  \[
  Q(u)=\gamma
  \]
  has exactly two simple roots near \(z_*\);
- if \(\gamma=m_*\), those two roots coalesce at \(u=z_*\) into one zero of even order \(2\ell\);
- if \(\gamma<m_*\) and \(m_*-\gamma>0\) is small, there are no roots near \(z_*\).

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{crossing a local minimum critical value }m_*\text{ turns two simple zeros of }J_\gamma
\text{ into one even zero and then none.}
}
\]

For a local maximum the inequalities are reversed:

- two simple zeros for \(\gamma<m_*\),
- one even zero at \(\gamma=m_*\),
- no nearby zeros for \(\gamma>m_*\).

Thus the critical-level passage is exactly a local saddle-node merger of zeros of \(J_\gamma\).

---

## 3. The central zero is sterile

At \(\gamma=m_*\), the merged zero of \(J_\gamma\) at \(u=z_*\) has even order \(2\ell\).

But the fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared \(3\)-point package already says that even-order zeros of the relevant curvature/source scalar are locally sterile: they do not seed the odd-zero opposite-side branch mechanism.

So the central merger point is not a new birth point at all. It is only a **collision point** of two previously visible simple zeros.

Hence:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the critical-level merger point is locally sterile for the fixed-}\gamma\text{ branch geometry.}
}
\]

That is the main structural gain.

---

## 4. Binary-merger theorem, sharpened

Now return to the first generic hidden-reset pattern: a three-extrema regular \(\gamma\)-excursion island with critical values
\[
M_1>m<M_3,
\]
where \(m\) is the central local minimum.

For every level
\[
m<\gamma'<\min(M_1,M_3),
\]
there are two visible adjacent-block excursion islands, one near the left maximum and one near the right maximum. Each corresponds to a simple odd zero of \(J_{\gamma'}\).

As \(\gamma'\downarrow m\), those two simple zeros approach the central critical point and coalesce there into an even zero of \(J_m\). For \(\gamma<m\), the gap disappears and the two visible islands merge into one hidden three-extrema island.

So the first generic hidden reset is precisely:

\[
\boxed{
\text{two visible simple zeros of }J_\gamma
\text{ annihilating into one sterile even zero at }\gamma=m,
\text{ then reappearing globally as one merged excursion island for }\gamma<m.
}
\]

This is much more precise than the earlier “binary merger across a central critical value.”

---

## 5. What this means for the no-hidden-reset problem

This says that the critical-level passage itself is **not** the hard part anymore.

Locally at the central critical value, everything is completely understood:

- the zeros of \(J_\gamma\) undergo a visible saddle-node merger,
- the merged zero is even and sterile,
- no new local branch is born there.

So the remaining issue is only this:

\[
\boxed{
\text{after the two visible side branches collide at the sterile central zero, can they still glue globally into one hidden-reset pattern?}
}
\]

That is a much narrower question.

Equivalently, the only remaining obstruction in the first generic hidden-reset case is a **global post-merger gluing problem**, not a local critical-passage problem.

---

## 6. Upstairs \(\mathcal R\)-interpretation

Because on the opened residual arc
\[
\frac{dQ}{dx}=\mathcal R,
\]
the central odd critical point of \(\widehat Q\) is exactly a sign-changing odd zero of \(\mathcal R\).

So the picture upstairs is:

- two visible fixed-\(\gamma\) intervals exist on either side when the level lies above the central minimum,
- they collide at the central odd zero of \(\mathcal R\),
- the collision occurs through a sterile even zero of \(J_\gamma\),
- after crossing the critical value, the only remaining question is whether the two side visible pieces globally fuse into one hidden same-\(\gamma\) interval.

This is the cleanest upstairs translation I have now.

---

## 7. Why this matters

This sharpens the updated paper’s “critical-value ordering / no-hidden-reset” program in a real way.

We can now say:

- regular levels: fully visible in the critical-value ladder;
- a single critical point: fully classified;
- the first generic hidden-reset mechanism: a visible local saddle-node merger through a sterile even zero;
- so the unresolved issue is only the **global fusion after that merger**.

That is much more precise than before.

---

## 8. Honest boundary

I still do **not** see a proof from the current identities alone that the post-merger global fusion cannot occur.

So the current math stops at:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first generic hidden reset is not a local critical-passage problem at all;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{it is a global gluing problem after a visible saddle-node merger of two simple zeros of }J_\gamma\text{ through a sterile even zero.}
}
\]

That is, I think, the cleanest refinement so far.

The best next theorem to try is now very focused:

}
\]

I found and corrected an important point in the merger picture: in the first generic hidden-reset case, it is **not** the two outer boundary zeros of \(J_\gamma\) that collide. I’m pushing that corrected local catastrophe picture now because it tells us exactly what data survive across the merger and what still needs a genuinely global gluing theorem.

_Thought for 48s_

I found a real sharpening, and it corrects an important point in the merger picture.

The first generic hidden reset is **not** created by two visible boundary zeros of \(J_\gamma\) colliding into one even zero. What actually happens is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the \emph{inner} pair of }J_\gamma\text{-zeros annihilates at the central critical value, while the \emph{outer} pair persists.}
}
\]

That is the correct local catastrophe model, and it makes the remaining gap much more precise.

## 1. Correct local model for the first generic hidden reset

Work on a bounded regular superlevel excursion island of \(\widehat Q\) with exactly three odd critical points
\[
\xi_1<\xi_2<\xi_3,
\]
with
\[
M_1:=\widehat Q(\xi_1),\qquad m:=\widehat Q(\xi_2),\qquad M_3:=\widehat Q(\xi_3),
\]
and
\[
M_1>m<M_3.
\]

Fix a level \(\gamma\) near \(m\).

### Case A: \(m<\gamma<\min(M_1,M_3)\)

Then the equation
\[
\widehat Q(u)=\gamma
\]
has **four** simple solutions on the island interval:
\[
u_1<u_2<u_3<u_4,
\]
with:
- \((u_1,u_2)\) the left visible superlevel island around \(\xi_1\),
- \((u_3,u_4)\) the right visible superlevel island around \(\xi_3\),
- and the central minimum \(\xi_2\) lying strictly between \(u_2\) and \(u_3\).

So for
\[
J_\gamma=J_\lambda(\widehat Q-\gamma),
\]
there are four simple zeros on that local interval.

### Case B: \(\gamma=m\)

At the critical level, the **inner pair**
\[
u_2,\ u_3
\]
collapses to the central point \(\xi_2\), producing an even zero of \(J_m\) there. The **outer pair**
\[
u_1,\ u_4
\]
remain simple.

So at \(\gamma=m\), locally \(J_m\) has:
- one even zero at the center,
- plus two outer simple zeros.

### Case C: \(\gamma<m\)

The inner pair disappears, and only the outer pair survives:
\[
u_1<u_4.
\]
These two outer zeros bound one merged hidden superlevel island containing all three odd critical points.

So the first generic hidden reset is exactly:

\[
\boxed{
\text{inner-pair annihilation at the central minimum, with the outer pair persisting and becoming the new island boundary.}
}
\]

That is the correct picture.

---

## 2. Local catastrophe theorem for \(J_\gamma\)

This can be stated cleanly.

Let \(z_*=\xi_2\) be the central local minimum with value \(m=\widehat Q(z_*)\). Then locally
\[
\widehat Q(u)-m=(u-z_*)^{2\ell}\widetilde L(u),
\qquad \widetilde L(z_*)>0.
\]
So near \(z_*\):
- for \(\gamma>m\), the equation \(\widehat Q=\gamma\) has two simple local roots,
- for \(\gamma=m\), they coalesce into one even root,
- for \(\gamma<m\), there are no local roots near \(z_*\).

But globally on the three-extrema island, the outer roots remain present throughout. Thus the passage through \(\gamma=m\) is a **local annihilation of the inner pair only**, not a total collapse of all zeros on the island.

This is an important correction to the previous merger picture.

---

## 3. Why this sharpens the remaining gap

This means the first generic hidden reset is more rigid than I had said before.

Before, I described it as a “binary merger of two visible islands across a central critical value.” That is true topologically, but the correct source-level picture is:

- the two visible islands for \(\gamma>m\) are bounded by four simple zeros of \(J_\gamma\);
- as \(\gamma\downarrow m\), only the inner pair annihilates at the sterile even zero;
- the outer pair survives and becomes the boundary of the hidden island for \(\gamma<m\).

So the post-merger hidden island is already partly visible before the merger: its future boundary zeros are the outer simple zeros that were there all along.

That gives the cleaner formulation:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first hidden reset is created by local annihilation of the inner pair of }J_\gamma\text{-zeros, while the outer boundary pair persists continuously.}
}
\]

This is better than the old “global gluing of two visible branches” phrasing.

---

## 4. Consequence for the fixed-\(\gamma\) branch picture

Now translate this into the existing fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared \(3\)-point package.

For \(\gamma>m\), there are two visible fixed-\(\gamma\) islands, with zeros
\[
u_1<u_2
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
u_3<u_4.
\]
Each visible island has its own adjacent-block branch geometry.

At the merger level \(\gamma=m\):
- the inner pair \(u_2,u_3\) collapses to the sterile even zero at \(\xi_2\),
- the outer pair \(u_1,u_4\) remains.

For \(\gamma<m\), the hidden island is bounded by \(u_1,u_4\).

So the remaining question is not whether two visible islands can “glue” in some vague way. It is much sharper:

\[
\boxed{
\text{after the inner pair disappears at the sterile central zero, can the outer surviving pair }(u_1,u_4)\text{ support one admissible fixed-}\gamma\text{ interval?}
}
\]

That is the exact residual gluing problem in the first generic hidden-reset case.

---

## 5. Upstairs \(\mathcal R\)-language

Because
\[
\frac{dQ}{dx}=\mathcal R,
\]
the three-extrema island corresponds upstairs to three odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\), say
\[
x(\xi_1),\ x(\xi_2),\ x(\xi_3),
\]
with sign pattern
\[
+,-,+,- \quad\text{for the lobe integrals in the superlevel case.}
\]

The corrected picture now says:

- for \(\gamma>m\), the level cuts the graph in four points, giving two visible \(J_\gamma\)-intervals;
- at \(\gamma=m\), the two inner cuts coalesce at the central odd zero of \(\mathcal R\), producing a sterile even zero of \(J_\gamma\);
- for \(\gamma<m\), only the outer cuts remain, producing one hidden interval spanning all three odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\).

So the first hidden reset is exactly:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a same-sign }J_\gamma\text{-interval spanning three odd zeros of }\mathcal R
\text{ after the inner two level cuts have annihilated at the central one.}
}
\]

That is the cleanest upstairs formulation so far.

This improves the updated no-hidden-reset program in a real way.

The generic three-extrema case is now fully local except for one final question:

- the critical-point passage is understood;
- the inner-pair annihilation is understood;
- the outer-pair persistence is understood.

So the only remaining issue is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{can the surviving outer pair }(u_1,u_4)\text{ define one genuine post-merger fixed-}\gamma\text{ branch interval?}
}
\]

That is much narrower than “can two visible islands glue globally?”

It is really a **post-annihilation outer-pair admissibility problem**.

---

## 7. Honest boundary

I still do **not** see a proof from the current identities alone that the outer surviving pair \(u_1,u_4\) cannot support such a post-merger hidden interval.

So the math now stops at the sharper reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first generic hidden reset }
\Longleftrightarrow
\text{inner-pair annihilation at the central critical value plus survival of an admissible outer pair.}
}
\]

That is, I think, the clearest form of the remaining generic loophole.

The best next theorem to try is now very focused:

## 1. Correct chamber picture across the first hidden-reset merger

Work in the first generic hidden-reset configuration on the quotient branch:
\[
\xi_1<\xi_2<\xi_3,
\qquad
M_1:=\widehat Q(\xi_1),\quad m:=\widehat Q(\xi_2),\quad M_3:=\widehat Q(\xi_3),
\qquad
M_1>m<M_3,
\]
and fix a level \(\gamma\) near \(m\).

Recall
\[
J_\gamma=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda
=
J_\lambda(\widehat Q-\gamma),
\]
and on the opened residual arc \(J_\lambda\) has fixed sign, so the sign pattern of \(J_\gamma\) is the sign pattern of \(\widehat Q-\gamma\).

### For \(m<\gamma<\min(M_1,M_3)\)

There are four simple zeros
\[
u_1<u_2<u_3<u_4
\]
of \(J_\gamma\), and the positivity set of \(J_\gamma\) on the local interval is exactly
\[
(u_1,u_2)\ \cup\ (u_3,u_4).
\]
So there are two visible curvature chambers.

### At \(\gamma=m\)

The inner pair coalesces:
\[
u_2,u_3\to \xi_2,
\]
and \(J_m\) has:
- one even zero at \(\xi_2\),
- plus the outer simple zeros \(u_1,u_4\).

### For \(\gamma<m\)

The inner pair disappears, and now the positivity set of \(J_\gamma\) on the same local interval is the single interval
\[
(u_1,u_4).
\]

So the first hidden-reset mechanism is exactly:

\[
\boxed{
(u_1,u_2)\cup(u_3,u_4)
\quad\longrightarrow\quad
(u_1,u_4)
}
\]
as the level crosses the central critical value \(m\).

That is the basic chamber recombination.

---

## 2. Why this is the right fixed-\(\gamma\) formulation

The fixed-\(\gamma\) branch theorem is built from the sheared curve
\[
\widetilde Y_\gamma:=Y-\gamma S
\]
and its curvature
\[
\widetilde Y_\gamma''=\frac{c^3}{M^3}J_\gamma.
\]

So the relevant right-hand branch chamber at a simple odd zero of \(J_\gamma\) is exactly the maximal interval to the right on which
\[
\widetilde Y_\gamma''>0,
\]
equivalently \(J_\gamma>0\).

Hence, in the three-extrema picture:

- for \(\gamma>m\), the branch born at \(u_1\) has right chamber
  \[
  I_+(\gamma)=(u_1,u_2);
  \]
- for \(\gamma<m\), the candidate hidden branch born at \(u_1\) would need right chamber
  \[
  I_+(\gamma)=(u_1,u_4).
  \]

So the only way the first hidden reset can happen is if crossing the sterile central level \(\gamma=m\) causes the maximal right chamber attached to the left outer zero to jump from
\[
(u_1,u_2)
\quad\text{to}\quad
(u_1,u_4).
\]

That is the precise remaining mechanism.

---

## 3. Sterile-even-zero theorem for the merger level

At the merger level \(\gamma=m\), the central zero at \(\xi_2\) is even. So by the fixed-\(\gamma\) odd-zero branch package, it is locally sterile: it seeds no new local opposite-side branch.

Therefore the chamber recombination is **not** mediated by any local odd-zero birth at the center.

So we can state the clean reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first generic hidden reset is a nonlocal chamber recombination across a sterile even zero of }J_\gamma.
}
\]

Equivalently:

- the local catastrophe at \(\xi_2\) is completely understood;
- the inner pair annihilation is completely understood;
- the remaining question is only whether the two visible positive-curvature chambers can recombine into one larger positive-curvature chamber after crossing the sterile level.

This is the real leftover.

---

## 4. Fixed-\(\gamma\) branch interpretation

This lets us sharpen the earlier branch-language reduction.

For \(\gamma>m\), the left visible fixed-\(\gamma\) branch seeded at \(u_1\) can only continue until boundary exit at \(u_2\), because its maximal right chamber is exactly
\[
(u_1,u_2).
\]

For \(\gamma<m\), the hidden candidate would require the branch seeded at the same outer zero \(u_1\) to continue all the way to \(u_4\), because now the positive-curvature chamber is
\[
(u_1,u_4).
\]

So the remaining hidden-reset mechanism is exactly this:

\[
\boxed{
\text{crossing }\gamma=m\text{ would have to replace the left visible branch chamber }(u_1,u_2)
\text{ by the larger chamber }(u_1,u_4).
}
\]

That is a **chamber recombination problem**, not a local branch-birth problem.

---

## 5. Upstairs \(\mathcal R\)-picture

Because
\[
\frac{dQ}{dx}=\mathcal R,
\]
the three-extrema configuration corresponds upstairs to three odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\). In the superlevel case, the \(\mathcal R\)-lobe sign pattern is
\[
+,-,+,-.
\]

Then:

- for \(\gamma>m\), the left visible chamber spans only the first two lobes;
- for \(\gamma<m\), the hidden candidate chamber would span all four lobes.

So the chamber recombination theorem can also be stated as:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first hidden reset would require one admissible fixed-}\gamma\text{ chamber to swallow two extra }\mathcal R\text{-lobes across the sterile central level.}
}
\]

That is the cleanest upstairs phrasing I have so far.

---

## 6. Why this matters

This is a real improvement, because it isolates the exact generic loophole.

We no longer have to think in terms of:

- arbitrary hidden gluing,
- or arbitrary excursion islands,
- or arbitrary nonadjacent block overlap.

The first generic hidden reset is now exactly:

\[
\boxed{
\text{sterile-level chamber recombination }(u_1,u_2)\cup(u_3,u_4)\rightsquigarrow (u_1,u_4).
}
\]

That is a very specific mechanism.

And it interfaces directly with the existing fixed-\(\gamma\) theorem, because that theorem already controls branch continuation within a given chamber and says the only stopping mechanism is boundary exit.

So the only missing theorem is now:

> a sterile even zero of \(J_\gamma\) cannot mediate this chamber recombination.

That is much sharper than before.

---

## 7. Honest boundary

I still do **not** see a proof from the current identities alone that the recombination
\[
(u_1,u_2)\cup(u_3,u_4)\to (u_1,u_4)
\]
cannot occur.

So the current math now stops at the precise reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first generic hidden reset }
\Longleftrightarrow
\text{sterile-even-zero chamber recombination of two visible positive-curvature chambers into one larger chamber.}
}
\]

That is, I think, the clearest generic no-hidden-reset gap.

---

## 8. Best next attack

The best next theorem to try is now very focused:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a sterile even zero of }J_\gamma\text{ cannot cause chamber recombination on an opened residual arc.}
}
\]

A slightly weaker but still useful version would be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{if }J_\gamma\text{ has the local three-extrema pattern, then the maximal right chamber attached to the left outer zero cannot jump from }(u_1,u_2)\text{ to }(u_1,u_4)\text{ as }\gamma\downarrow m.
}
\]

That is where I would keep pushing.

## 1. Three-extrema superlevel island

Work on a connected opened residual quotient interval in the coordinate
\[
u=t^2,
\qquad
Q(t)=\widehat Q(u),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u).
\]

Suppose we are in the first generic hidden-reset configuration: a bounded regular superlevel island
\[
C=(a,b)\subset\{\widehat Q>\gamma\}
\]
containing exactly three odd critical points
\[
\xi_1<\xi_2<\xi_3,
\]
with
\[
M_1:=\widehat Q(\xi_1),\qquad
m:=\widehat Q(\xi_2),\qquad
M_3:=\widehat Q(\xi_3),
\qquad
M_1>m<M_3.
\]

For every
\[
m<\gamma<\min(M_1,M_3),
\]
the level equation
\[
\widehat Q(u)=\gamma
\]
has four simple roots
\[
u_1<u_2<u_3<u_4.
\]
The visible superlevel chambers are
\[
(u_1,u_2)\qquad\text{and}\qquad (u_3,u_4).
\]

At the critical level
\[
\gamma=m,
\]
the inner pair collapses:
\[
u_2,u_3\to \xi_2,
\]
while the outer pair
\[
u_1,\ u_4
\]
remain simple.

For
\[
\gamma<m,
\]
only the outer pair survives, and the superlevel chamber is the merged interval
\[
(u_1,u_4).
\]

So far this is the corrected catastrophe picture.

---

## 2. The merged chamber is already present at the critical level

Now fix
\[
\gamma=m
\]
and consider
\[
J_m:=J_{u_5}-mJ_\lambda = J_\lambda(Q-m).
\]

Since \(m\) is the value of the central local minimum, we have
\[
Q(u)-m\ge 0
\]
on the whole local interval \((u_1,u_4)\), with equality only at the central point \(\xi_2\).

Hence, on the opened residual arc where \(J_\lambda\neq 0\), the sign of \(J_m\) is constant on \((u_1,u_4)\), with one isolated even zero at \(\xi_2\).

Equivalently, for the sheared curve
\[
\widetilde Y_m:=Y-mS,
\qquad
\widetilde Y_m''=\frac{c^3}{M^3}J_m,
\]
we have
\[
\widetilde Y_m''\ge 0
\quad\text{on }(u_1,u_4),
\]
and \(\widetilde Y_m''\) is positive except at the isolated even zero \(\xi_2\).

Therefore \(\widetilde Y_m'\) is in fact **strictly increasing** on the whole interval \((u_1,u_4)\). Indeed, for any \(x<y\) in \((u_1,u_4)\),
\[
\widetilde Y_m'(y)-\widetilde Y_m'(x)
=
\int_x^y \widetilde Y_m''(s)\,ds>0,
\]
because an analytic nonnegative function with only an isolated zero cannot integrate to zero on a nontrivial interval.

So the maximal right curvature chamber attached to the left outer odd zero \(u_1\) at the critical level \(\gamma=m\) is already

\[
\boxed{
I_+(m)=(u_1,u_4).
}
\]

That is the key theorem.

---

## 3. Consequence for the fixed-\(\gamma\) branch

Now apply Proposition~\ref{prop:uniform-fixed-level-nonrecurrence-sheared-three-point} at the odd zero \(u_1\) for the fixed level \(\gamma=m\).

Since the maximal right chamber is already
\[
I_+(m)=(u_1,u_4),
\]
the proposition gives a unique maximal fixed-\(m\) branch continuing across the entire merged chamber, with no interior common-tangent event and no interior recurrence.

So the merged outer pair
\[
(u_1,u_4)
\]
is not a mysterious post-critical object. It is already present at the critical level as the maximal fixed-\(m\) chamber attached to the left outer odd zero.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the three-extrema merger is completely visible at the central critical value }m.
}
\]

It does not require any extra hidden gluing data.

---

## 4. Why this kills the first generic “hidden reset”

This means the three-extrema regular island is **not** the first genuinely hidden-reset mechanism after all.

Its entire structure is already visible from:

- the critical value
  \[
  m=\widehat Q(\xi_2),
  \]
- the outer simple zeros \(u_1,u_4\),
- and the fact that \(J_m\) stays nonnegative across the interval with only one isolated even zero.

So the central critical passage does not hide anything. It is completely encoded by the quotient ladder and by the fixed-\(\gamma\) chamber theorem at \(\gamma=m\).

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first generic hidden-reset candidate, the three-extrema island, is actually visible and therefore not a genuine hidden reset.}
}
\]

This is the main gain.

---

## 5. New lower bound on genuine hidden-reset complexity

Since the three-extrema regular island is now demoted from “hidden” to “visible via one critical level,” the first genuinely hidden regular reset must be more complicated.

In the regular regime, that means it must involve at least **two odd critical values**, equivalently at least **five odd critical points** of \(\widehat Q\).

So we get the stronger lower bound:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a genuinely hidden regular reset requires at least five odd critical points of }\widehat Q.
}
\]

Equivalently, upstairs it requires at least:

- five positive-side odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\),
- and therefore at least ten off-center odd zeros of \(\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}\) on the full opened residual arc.

That is much sharper than the earlier “at least three” bound.

---

Because on opened residual arcs
\[
\frac{dQ}{dx}=\mathcal R,

the three-extrema pattern corresponds upstairs to three odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\).

The theorem above says that at the critical level
\[
\gamma=m,
\]
the outer pair already determines one maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) chamber spanning all three odd \(\mathcal R\)-zeros, because the central zero only creates a sterile even zero of \(J_\gamma\), not a sign reversal.

So the first generic three-odd-zero pattern is **already visible upstairs** as one critical-level chamber. There is no hidden-reset phenomenon there.

That is the cleanest upstairs reformulation.

---

This tightens the updated “no-hidden-reset / critical-value ordering” program significantly.

We can now say:

- regular levels are visible in the critical-value ladder;
- a single critical point is classified;
