- so the first genuinely hidden regular case must start only at the next complexity level.

Hence the live regular hidden-reset problem is no longer:

\[
\text{three-extrema island}
\]

but rather

\[
\boxed{
\text{a regular excursion island with at least five odd critical points, i.e. involving at least two odd critical values.}
}
\]

That is a real narrowing.

I still do **not** see a proof from the current identities alone that the five-critical-point case cannot occur.

}

\text{the first genuinely hidden regular reset requires at least two odd critical values, equivalently at least five odd critical points.}
}
\]

_Thought for 2m 2s_

I found a real structural theorem, and it is the first thing that feels close to a genuine **no-hidden-reset theorem on the quotient branch itself**.

The key point is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every bounded regular excursion island of }\widehat Q\text{ is obtained from visible one-extremum islands by successive adjacent mergers at critical values.}
}
\]

So at the level of **quotient-branch topology**, regular hidden reset is not hidden at all: it is completely encoded by the ordered critical values of \(\widehat Q\).

That is stronger than the earlier “three-extrema,” “five-extrema,” or “binary merger” reductions.

---

## 1. Merge-tree theorem for regular levels

Work on a connected opened residual quotient interval in the coordinate
\[
u=t^2,
\qquad
Q(t)=\widehat Q(u),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u).
\]

Fix a **regular** level \(\gamma\). Let
\[
C=(a,b)\subset \{\widehat Q>\gamma\}
\]
be a bounded regular superlevel island. List the odd critical points of \(\widehat Q\) in \(C\):
\[
a<\xi_1<\xi_2<\cdots<\xi_{2m+1}<b,
\]
so
\[
\xi_{2j-1}\ \text{are local maxima},\qquad
\xi_{2j}\ \text{are local minima}.
\]
Write
\[
M_j:=\widehat Q(\xi_{2j-1}),
\qquad
\mu_j:=\widehat Q(\xi_{2j}).
\]

Then:

### Theorem
There is a finite decreasing sequence of levels
\[
\lambda_0>\lambda_1>\cdots>\lambda_m>\gamma
\]
(with each \(\lambda_k\) chosen between consecutive relevant critical values) such that:

1. for \(\lambda_0\) sufficiently close to the side maxima and above all internal minima, the set
   \[
   \{\widehat Q>\lambda_0\}\cap C
   \]
   is a disjoint union of \(m+1\) bounded visible one-extremum islands, one around each local maximum \(\xi_{2j-1}\);

2. as \(\lambda\) decreases and crosses one internal minimum value \(\mu_j\), exactly the two superlevel components adjacent to \(\xi_{2j}\) merge into a single larger component;

3. no other topological change occurs.

Consequently, the full bounded island \(C\) is obtained by a finite sequence of **adjacent binary mergers** across the internal minima.

This is just the 1D merge tree of \(\widehat Q\) on the quotient branch.

---

## 2. Proof sketch

This is standard 1D Morse/Reeb behavior, but in our setting it is useful to spell it out.

For any regular level \(\lambda\), every bounded component of \(\{\widehat Q>\lambda\}\) is an interval whose boundary points are level hits. Between two nearby levels with no critical values in between, the topology of the superlevel set cannot change because \(\widehat Q'\neq 0\) on every boundary hit, so the endpoints move smoothly.

The only times the component structure can change are when \(\lambda\) crosses a critical value. For a superlevel set, only local minima matter:

- crossing a local maximum just creates or removes a tiny visible island near that maximum,
- crossing a local minimum merges the two adjacent superlevel components into one.

Since the line is ordered, the merge is automatically adjacent and binary. Iterating this from high level down to \(\gamma\) yields the whole island.

The sublevel case is identical with maxima/minima interchanged.

---

## 3. What this says about the first few cases

This recovers and clarifies all the earlier patterns:

### One odd critical point
A visible one-extremum island. No hidden reset.

### Three odd critical points
Exactly one adjacent binary merger of two visible islands across the central minimum. This was our earlier “binary merger” picture.

### Five odd critical points
Exactly two adjacent binary mergers in succession.

And so on.

So the earlier reductions were not isolated special cases. They are the first nodes of the same general merge tree.

---

## 4. Consequence: regular excursion topology is fully visible

This gives the strongest clean visibility statement I’ve found:

\[
\boxed{
\text{for regular levels, the entire excursion topology of the quotient branch is already visible from the ordered critical values of }\widehat Q.
}
\]

In particular:

- every regular bounded excursion island,
- every apparent “hidden reset,”
- every nonadjacent overlap of level hits,

is already encoded by the merge tree of \(\widehat Q\).

So at the level of the quotient function alone, there is no mysterious extra reset mechanism.

That is the theorem-level gain.

---

## 5. Critical-value-ladder formulation

Let
\[
z_1<z_2<\cdots<z_N
\]
be the critical points of \(\widehat Q\), and
\[
c_j:=\widehat Q(z_j).
\]

Then the merge tree is determined by the sequence \(c_j\) together with the order of the \(z_j\). Every regular excursion island corresponds to a finite consecutive cluster of extrema whose internal opposite-type critical values lie above the target level \(\gamma\) and whose outer bounding opposite-type critical values lie below it.

Equivalently:

\[
\boxed{
\text{for regular }\gamma,\text{ the excursion islands are exactly the nodes of the merge tree determined by the ladder }(z_j,c_j).
}
\]

So the regular no-hidden-reset problem on the quotient branch is solved, in the sense of level-set topology.

---

## 6. Upstairs interpretation via \(J_\gamma\) and \(\mathcal R\)

On opened residual arcs,
\[
Q=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda},
\qquad
J_\gamma=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda=J_\lambda(Q-\gamma),
\qquad
\frac{dQ}{dx}=\mathcal R.
\]

So upstairs, the theorem says:

- each bounded same-sign interval of \(J_\gamma\) is built by successive adjacent mergers across odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\);
- the first visible interval encloses one odd zero of \(\mathcal R\);
- each time the level crosses the next relevant critical value, two adjacent \(J_\gamma\)-intervals merge across the corresponding odd zero of \(\mathcal R\).

Thus the entire regular fixed-\(\gamma\) interval structure is already visible from the ordered odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\) and their critical values.

That is the cleanest source-level version.

---

## 7. What this does and does not solve

This is the honest boundary.

### What it *does* solve
It solves the **quotient-level topology** of regular recurrence. There is no further hidden topological reset on the quotient branch for regular levels.

### What it does *not* yet solve
The draft’s live issue is slightly stronger: it asks whether the full **transported overlap / critical-value state relevant to the later finite-core problem** is already visible in this quotient transport scalar.

The merge-tree theorem shows that the quotient-level combinatorics are visible. But it does **not yet prove** that no extra source-level or transported geometric data survive beyond that combinatorics.

So the remaining gap is no longer a quotient-topology gap. It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{does the finite-core transported overlap state depend only on the merge tree of }\widehat Q,\text{ or is there extra source-level data?}
}
\]

That is a much sharper formulation of the true remaining problem.

---

## 8. Why this matters strategically

This is a substantial narrowing.

We no longer need to attack:

- arbitrary nonadjacent overlap,
- arbitrary hidden reset,
- arbitrary excursion islands,
- or even the three-/five-extrema cases separately,

at the level of quotient-branch topology.

All of that is now subsumed by the merge tree.

So the remaining attack should be reformulated as:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove that the later transported overlap state is functorially determined by the quotient merge tree, with no extra hidden source-level branching data.}
}
\]

That is the right next target.

---

## 9. Best next attack

The next theorem I would try is now very focused:

### Candidate theorem
On the exact fixed-shear residual lane, the transported overlap state relevant to the later finite-core problem is constant on each quotient excursion component and changes only by the adjacent binary mergers encoded by the merge tree of \(\widehat Q\).

If that can be proved, then the updated draft’s no-hidden-reset problem would be closed for regular levels.

A weaker but still useful intermediate version would be:

### Weaker target
Any two points on the same bounded regular excursion island with the same quotient level \(\gamma\) induce the same downstream transported overlap state, because the only possible state changes are the visible adjacent mergers already recorded by the critical-value ladder.

That is where I would push next.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 16:44:23)

I’m checking the updated draft’s actual \(\widehat Q\), critical-value, and transported-overlap wording now, because the next step needs to hit the remaining gap precisely rather than keep circling the same reductions. The target is now very narrow: whether the merge-tree visibility on the quotient branch already forces enough visibility of the transported overlap state, or whether there is still one extra source-level degree of freedom hiding there.

_Thought for 1m 30s_

I found a useful next reduction, and it fits the updated draft very closely.

The right way to push now is **not** another local geometry calculation. It is an abstract **merge-tree induction** for the residual quotient branch.

The gain is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{if the later transported overlap state is stable on regular levels and has a unique update rule at a single odd critical merger, then it is already completely determined by the quotient merge tree.}
}
\]

So the remaining no-hidden-reset problem can be reduced to one very specific item:

\[
\boxed{
\text{verify uniqueness of the single-critical merge rule for the actual finite-core transported overlap state.}
}
\]

That is the sharpest structural reduction I can honestly justify now.

---

## 1. The quotient merge tree already classifies regular excursion topology

From the previous attack, on a connected opened residual quotient interval in the coordinate
\[
u=t^2,
\qquad
Q(t)=\widehat Q(u),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u),
\]
every bounded regular excursion island of \(\widehat Q\) is obtained from visible one-extremum islands by a finite sequence of **adjacent binary mergers** across odd critical values.

So for regular levels, the quotient branch has a canonical rooted merge tree:

- **leaves** = visible one-extremum islands,
- **internal nodes** = adjacent binary mergers at odd critical values,
- **even critical points** = topologically inert.

This is already forced by the 1D level-set structure of \(\widehat Q\).

---

## 2. Abstract transported-state setup

Let me write
\[
\Sigma(\gamma,C)
\]
for the later “transported overlap / critical-value state” attached to a bounded regular excursion island \(C\) of level \(\gamma\). I am deliberately keeping \(\Sigma\) abstract, because the remaining issue in the draft is precisely whether this state is already quotient-visible.

The natural properties such a state would need, if it is to be governed by the quotient branch, are:

### (R1) Regular persistence
If \(\gamma\) varies inside an interval containing no critical value of \(\widehat Q\), then the island \(C\) moves by analytic continuation and
\[
\Sigma(\gamma,C)
\]
is transported uniquely along that continuation.

### (R2) Visible leaf determination
On a visible one-extremum island, the state is determined by the local visible branch data already controlled by the fixed-\(\gamma\) package.

### (R3) Single-critical merger locality
When \(\gamma\) crosses one odd critical value and two adjacent visible islands merge into one larger island, the new state on the merged island is determined by:

- the two incoming child states,
- the one critical value being crossed,
- and the local single-critical model.

### (R4) Even critical points are inert
Crossing an even critical point produces no state bifurcation.

These are exactly the properties one would expect if the state is really “quotient-visible.”

---

## 3. Conditional merge-tree visibility theorem

Under these abstract properties, one gets the following.

### Theorem (conditional merge-tree visibility)
Assume a transported state \(\Sigma(\gamma,C)\) on bounded regular excursion islands satisfies (R1)–(R4). Then \(\Sigma(\gamma,C)\) is completely determined by the merge tree of \(\widehat Q\) on the relevant quotient interval. In particular, for regular levels there is no hidden reset beyond the quotient-visible merge structure.

### Proof sketch
Induct on the number of odd critical values below the level \(\gamma\) inside the island.

- Base case: a visible one-extremum island is a leaf, so \(\Sigma\) is determined by (R2).
- Inductive step: any larger regular island arises by crossing one adjacent binary merger in the merge tree. By induction, the child states are already determined by the subtree below that merger. Then (R3) uniquely determines the parent state. Even critical points do nothing by (R4), and regular continuation between critical values is unique by (R1).

So the whole state is recursively determined by the merge tree.

That is the structural theorem.

---

## 4. Why this is relevant here

This theorem shows that the draft’s current no-hidden-reset problem has become much narrower than it first sounded.

We no longer need to prove directly that every regular hidden reset is impossible from scratch. It is enough to prove that the actual downstream transported overlap state satisfies a **unique local update rule at one odd critical merger**.

Because once that is true, the entire regular-level state is forced by the quotient merge tree.

So the residual no-hidden-reset problem is now reduced to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{local uniqueness of the transported-state update across one odd critical merger.}
}
\]

That is a much smaller target than “global hidden reset.”

---

## 5. Why the three-extrema and five-extrema cases fit this perfectly

This also clarifies all the earlier reductions.

- A visible one-extremum island is a **leaf**.
- The three-extrema case is exactly **one binary merge** of two leaves.
- The five-extrema case is exactly **two binary merges** in succession.
- Longer cases are just deeper merge-tree nodes.

So once the single-merge rule is fixed, there is no independent new phenomenon at higher complexity. Everything is recursive.

This is, I think, the cleanest conceptual compression so far.

---

## 6. Single-critical passage is already basically classified at the quotient level

Our earlier work already showed:

- a single critical point of \(\widehat Q\) never creates a genuinely hidden reset;
- odd critical points produce only the visible adjacent-block merger pattern;
- even critical points are locally inert;
- in the three-extrema case, the central critical passage is a sterile even-zero event for \(J_\gamma\).

So at the **pure quotient-topology** level, the single-critical merge rule looks unique already.

What is **not yet proved** is that the actual later finite-core transported overlap state does not carry some extra binary/source-level datum that would allow two different downstream states on the same merged quotient island.

That is exactly the remaining issue.

---

## 7. Best new reduction

So the best clean new handback is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{regular no-hidden-reset reduces to a local single-critical merge-uniqueness problem for the transported overlap state.}
}
\]

More explicitly:

- the quotient merge tree is known;
- regular excursion topology is known;
- all regular multi-extrema cases are recursive compositions of one-step odd-critical mergers;
- therefore any extra hidden reset would have to come from **nonuniqueness** of the transported state across one such merger.

That is a strong reduction.

---

## 8. Honest boundary

I do **not** yet have a proof that the actual later transported overlap state in the draft satisfies the required uniqueness property across one odd critical merger.

So the attack stops here:

\[
\boxed{
\text{to close regular }K_v=0\text{ no-hidden-reset, it is enough to prove uniqueness of the single-critical transported-state update.}
}
\]

That is more precise than saying “show the transported overlap state is quotient-visible.”

---

## 9. Best next attack

The best next thing to attack is now extremely focused:

### Candidate theorem
When two adjacent visible regular excursion islands merge across one odd critical value of \(\widehat Q\), the downstream transported overlap state on the merged island is uniquely determined by the two child states and the local quotient-critical model, with no extra source-level choice.

A weaker but still useful intermediate target would be:

### Weaker target
At the central odd critical merger, the transported overlap state is continuous from the regular side and cannot bifurcate into two inequivalent post-merger states.

That is where I would keep pushing now.

_Thought for 1m 48s_

I think I found the sharpest regular-level result available from the current package.

It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every bounded regular }\gamma\text{-excursion island already determines a unique maximal fixed-}\gamma\text{ sheared }3\text{-point branch segment.}
}
\]

So, at the level of the planar/sheared branch geometry, **regular no-hidden-reset is essentially closed**. The only remaining possible hidden data would have to live in the later transported overlap / finite-core state, not in the quotient island or the fixed-\(\gamma\) branch itself.

That is a meaningful strengthening.

---

## 1. Setup

Work on an opened exact fixed-shear residual arc on which
\[
J_\lambda\neq 0,
\qquad
Q:=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda}.
\]
Fix a **regular** level \(\gamma\), and let
\[
C=(a,b)
\]
be a bounded regular superlevel excursion island of \(Q\), so
\[
Q(a)=Q(b)=\gamma,
\qquad
Q(x)>\gamma \quad (x\in(a,b)).
\]
Equivalently,
\[
J_\gamma:=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda
=
J_\lambda(Q-\gamma)
\]
has:

- simple odd zeros at \(a\) and \(b\),
- fixed sign on \((a,b)\).

Since \(J_\lambda\) has fixed sign on the opened arc, after possibly replacing \(\widetilde Y_\gamma\) by \(-\widetilde Y_\gamma\), we may assume
\[
J_\gamma>0\quad\text{on }(a,b).
\]

Define the sheared graph
\[
\widetilde Y_\gamma:=Y-\gamma S.
\]
Then
\[
\widetilde Y_\gamma''=\frac{c^3}{M^3}J_\gamma.
\]

So:
- \(\widetilde Y_\gamma''<0\) immediately to the left of \(a\),
- \(\widetilde Y_\gamma''>0\) on \((a,b)\),
- and \(b\) is the next zero of \(\widetilde Y_\gamma''\) to the right of \(a\).

---

## 2. Chamber-visibility theorem for a regular excursion island

Now apply Proposition~\ref{prop:uniform-fixed-level-nonrecurrence-sheared-three-point} at the odd zero \(a\) of \(J_\gamma\).

The left interval \(I_-\) is the usual small interval to the left of \(a\) where
\[
\widetilde Y_\gamma''<0.
\]
The maximal right interval \(I_+\) on which
\[
\widetilde Y_\gamma'
\]
is strictly increasing is exactly
\[
\boxed{
I_+=(a,b).
}
\]

Why? Because on \((a,b)\),
\[
\widetilde Y_\gamma''=\frac{c^3}{M^3}J_\gamma>0,
\]
and \(b\) is the first point to the right where this positivity can fail, since \(b\) is the next zero of \(J_\gamma\).

Therefore the proposition yields a unique maximal branch
\[
u_\gamma(m)\in(a,b)
\]
born at \(a\), continuing on the whole slope-overlap block determined by \(I_-\) and \(I_+=(a,b)\), with:

- no interior common-tangent point,
- no interior recurrence,
- no interior accumulation,
- and stopping only by boundary exit at \(b\) if the overlap closes before exhaustion.

So we get the theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a bounded regular superlevel excursion island }(a,b)\text{ is exactly one visible fixed-}\gamma\text{ branch chamber.}
}
\]

The same argument applies to a bounded regular sublevel island after replacing \(\widetilde Y_\gamma\) by \(-\widetilde Y_\gamma\).

That is the main result.

---

## 3. Consequence: regular no-hidden-reset is solved for the sheared branch geometry

This means that, for regular levels \(\gamma\),

- the quotient excursion island \(C\),
- the sign chamber of \(J_\gamma\),
- and the maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared \(3\)-point branch segment,

are all the same object viewed in three equivalent ways.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{there is no hidden reset left in the regular fixed-}\gamma\text{ sheared branch geometry itself.}
}
\]

Every regular recurrence pattern, every visible interval, every merge of intervals — all of that is already encoded by the quotient excursion structure of \(Q\), equivalently by the merge tree of \(\widehat Q\).

This is stronger than the earlier “merge-tree visibility” statement because it ties the quotient tree directly to the actual maximal branch segments of the fixed-\(\gamma\) theorem.

---

## 4. How this fits the three-extrema and higher cases

This immediately organizes all the previous patterns.

### One-extremum case
A visible leaf island gives one visible maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) branch segment.

### Three-extrema case
A merged island across one central minimum gives one larger visible maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) branch segment at the critical level and below it.

### General regular island
Any bounded regular island, no matter how many odd critical points it contains, is still just one sign chamber of \(J_\gamma\), hence one maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) branch chamber.

So the merge-tree theorem plus this chamber-visibility theorem together say:

\[
\boxed{
\text{regular branch geometry is already fully quotient-visible.}
}
\]

That is a substantial compression.

---

## 5. Upstairs \(\mathcal R\)-interpretation

Because on the opened residual arc
\[
\frac{dQ}{dx}=\mathcal R,
\]
each regular excursion island is built by successive mergers across odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\). But once the level \(\gamma\) is fixed, the resulting island is just one sign chamber of \(J_\gamma\), and therefore one maximal sheared branch chamber.

So the entire regular-level branch structure is already determined by:

- the ordered odd zeros of \(\mathcal R\),
- the critical-value ladder of \(Q\),
- and the corresponding sign chambers of \(J_\gamma\).

There is no extra “branch-reset” data hiding beyond that.

---

## 6. What remains unresolved

This is the honest boundary.

The draft’s live residual issue is now **not** the fixed-\(\gamma\) branch geometry itself. That part is visible.

What remains is exactly what the draft says:

\[
\boxed{
\text{does the later transported overlap / finite-core state depend only on this visible regular branch chamber, or is there extra source-level data?}
}
\]

So the remaining no-hidden-reset problem has been reduced one level further:

- regular quotient topology: visible;
- regular fixed-\(\gamma\) branch segments: visible;
- only the later transported overlap state may still carry hidden information.

That is the strongest honest reduction I can currently justify.

---

## 7. Best theorem-level formulation

Here is the clean statement I would keep.

### Proposition (regular excursion islands are exactly maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) branch chambers)
Let \(J\) be an opened residual arc with \(J_\lambda\neq 0\), fix a regular level \(\gamma\), and let \(C=(a,b)\) be a bounded regular excursion island of \(Q=J_{u_5}/J_\lambda\). Then \(a,b\) are simple odd zeros of \(J_\gamma=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda\), \(J_\gamma\) has fixed sign on \((a,b)\), and after the appropriate sign convention for \(\widetilde Y_\gamma=Y-\gamma S\), the interval \((a,b)\) is exactly the maximal right curvature chamber \(I_+\) attached to the left zero \(a\) in Proposition~\ref{prop:uniform-fixed-level-nonrecurrence-sheared-three-point}. Hence \(C\) determines a unique maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared \(3\)-point branch segment.

That is, I think, a real theorem-level gain.

---

The next thing to attack is now very narrow and concrete:

\[

}
\]

If that works, then regular no-hidden-reset on the residual lane is fully closed.

A weaker but still useful step would be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{show that any two points on the same bounded regular excursion island induce the same transported overlap state.}
}
\]

That is where I would push now.

## 1. Canonical correspondence on the regular side

Work on an opened residual arc where
\[
J_\lambda\neq 0,
\qquad
Q=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda}.
\]
Fix a regular level \(\gamma\).

Let
\[
C=(a,b)
\]
be a bounded regular excursion island of \(Q\), meaning either
\[
Q>\gamma\ \text{on }(a,b),\quad Q(a)=Q(b)=\gamma,
\]
or the same with \(Q<\gamma\).

Then:

1. \(a,b\) are simple odd zeros of
   \[
   J_\gamma:=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda = J_\lambda(Q-\gamma),
   \]
   and \(J_\gamma\) has one strict sign on \((a,b)\);

2. after the appropriate sign convention for
   \[
   \widetilde Y_\gamma:=Y-\gamma S,
   \]

   \[

   \]
   attached to the left zero \(a\) in
   Proposition~\ref{prop:uniform-fixed-level-nonrecurrence-sheared-three-point};

3. therefore \(C\) determines a unique maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared \(3\)-point branch segment.

So there is a canonical map
\[
C\longmapsto \mathcal B_\gamma(C),
\]
from bounded regular quotient excursion islands to maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared branch segments, and this map is actually bijective on the regular side.

That is the first theorem-level conclusion.

---

## 2. Compatibility with adjacent binary mergers

Now let \(\gamma\) vary.

From the quotient-branch analysis, every bounded regular excursion island is built from visible one-extremum islands by a finite sequence of **adjacent binary mergers** across odd critical values of \(\widehat Q\).

At the same time, from the fixed-\(\gamma\) branch package:

- each visible leaf island gives one unique visible maximal branch segment;
- at a one-critical merger, the central critical point is completely understood locally;
- the merged island corresponds to one larger sign chamber of \(J_\gamma\), hence one larger maximal branch segment.

So on the regular side we get a commutative picture:

\[
\text{quotient excursion leaves}
\quad\leftrightarrow\quad
\text{visible fixed-}\gamma\text{ branch leaves},
\]
and
\[
\text{adjacent binary merger of quotient islands}
\quad\leftrightarrow\quad
\text{adjacent binary merger of branch chambers}.
\]

In other words:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the regular fixed-}\gamma\text{ branch geometry is a functor of the quotient merge tree.}
}
\]

That is stronger than the earlier statements about three-extrema or five-extrema cases one by one.

---

## 3. What this means for the no-hidden-reset problem

This kills the regular geometric hidden-reset problem on the quotient/sheared side.

There is no longer any separate hidden geometry in:

- regular level sets of \(\widehat Q\),
- regular sign chambers of \(J_\gamma\),
- or maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared branch segments.

All of that is already visible in the quotient merge tree.

So the remaining live issue is narrower:

\[
\boxed{
\text{does the later transported overlap / finite-core state define a functor on this same merge tree, or can it carry extra hidden source-level data?}
}
\]

That is the exact remaining question.

---

## 4. Conditional closure theorem

This leads to a clean conditional theorem.

Suppose that the later transported overlap state \(\Sigma\) satisfies:

1. **regular transport invariance**: \(\Sigma\) is constant under analytic continuation along one maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) branch segment;

2. **single-merger uniqueness**: when two adjacent visible regular branch segments merge across one odd critical value, the post-merger state is uniquely determined by the two child states and the local one-critical model;

3. **even-critical inertness**: even critical points do not create state bifurcation.

Then:

\[
\boxed{
\Sigma\text{ is completely determined by the quotient merge tree,}
}
\]
hence regular no-hidden-reset is closed.

This is really just induction on the number of odd critical mergers: leaves are visible, and every larger regular island is formed by one adjacent binary merger of smaller ones.

So the remaining burden is now very precise:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove single-merger uniqueness for the actual transported overlap / finite-core state.}
}
\]

That is the sharpest reduction I can justify now.

---

## 5. Why this is probably the honest endpoint of the regular attack

I tried to squeeze more out of the pure derivative geometry, but I do not see a way to prove more without bringing in the actual downstream state one wants to transport.

Everything geometric that the quotient branch, \(J_\gamma\), and the fixed-\(\gamma\) theorem can tell us seems to have collapsed to:

- a canonical merge tree;
- a canonical family of maximal branch chambers;
- and uniqueness of regular continuation inside each chamber.

So the only place genuine hiddenness can still live is in the rule that takes one such visible branch chamber and assigns the later finite-core overlap state.

That is no longer a question about extra geometry on the quotient branch. It is a question about whether the downstream state forgets nothing and branches nowhere.

_Thought for 49s_

I found a cleaner structural closure on the **regular** side.

It is not another local formula. It is a topology statement:

\[
\boxed{
\text{on the regular exact fixed-shear quotient branch, there is no global monodromy at all.}
}
\]

So if any hidden reset survives, it cannot come from moving around the regular branch geometry. It can only come from **nonuniqueness at odd critical mergers**.

That is the sharpest clean reduction I can justify now.

---

## 1. The regular quotient branch carries a rooted merge tree, not a looped state space

From the current paper structure, on one connected opened residual quotient interval in the coordinate
\[
u=t^2,
\qquad
Q(t)=\widehat Q(u),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u),
\]
every bounded regular excursion island of \(\widehat Q\) is obtained from visible one-extremum leaves by successive **adjacent binary mergers** across odd critical values.

So the family of bounded regular excursion islands forms a finite rooted tree:

- leaves = visible one-extremum islands,
- internal vertices = odd-critical mergers,
- edges = regular continuation as the level varies between critical values.

Call this tree
\[
\mathcal T_{\widehat Q}.
\]

Because it is a tree, it is contractible. In particular:

\[
\boxed{
\pi_1(\mathcal T_{\widehat Q})=0.
}
\]

That sounds trivial, but it is exactly the right abstraction for the no-hidden-reset issue.

---

## 2. Consequence for any transported state on regular islands

Let \(\Sigma\) be any transported state assigned to bounded regular excursion islands, with the minimal natural properties:

1. **regular continuation**: \(\Sigma\) transports uniquely along a regular family of islands between critical values;

2. **locality of change**: \(\Sigma\) can change only when the level crosses an odd critical value and two adjacent islands merge;

3. **even critical inertness**: even critical points do not create bifurcation.

Then \(\Sigma\) is exactly a state carried on the tree \(\mathcal T_{\widehat Q}\).

Since \(\mathcal T_{\widehat Q}\) has no loops, there is no possibility of nontrivial monodromy under regular continuation.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{any regular hidden reset cannot be a global continuation phenomenon on the quotient branch.}
}
\]

It must come from a **local nonuniqueness at a merger vertex**.

That is the theorem-level gain.

---

## 3. Strong conditional no-hidden-reset theorem

This immediately gives the clean conditional statement:

### Theorem
Assume the transported overlap / finite-core state \(\Sigma\) is

- uniquely transported on regular excursion islands,
- and has a unique update rule at each odd-critical adjacent binary merger.

Then \(\Sigma\) is uniquely determined on the entire regular residual lane by the visible leaf data and the merge tree of \(\widehat Q\). In particular, there is no regular hidden reset on the \(K_v=0\) lane.

### Reason
Because every regular island is reached from the leaves by a unique path in the merge tree, and trees have no monodromy.

This is stronger than my previous “reduce to local merge uniqueness” wording because it explains **why** no global ambiguity survives once local uniqueness is known: there are no loops to carry it.

---

## 4. Why this is better than the earlier reductions

Before, we had compressed the problem to:

- three-extrema islands,
- then five-extrema islands,
- then merge trees,
- then single-merger uniqueness.

What is new here is that the merge-tree viewpoint shows that **all** regular hiddenness is local-at-vertices. There is no additional global degree of freedom hiding in long concatenations of regular chambers.

So the regular problem is now exactly:

\[
\boxed{
\text{does one odd-critical merger admit more than one transported-state update?}
}
\]

Nothing else can create hidden reset on the regular side.

That is a very sharp endpoint.

---

## 5. Relation to the existing branch geometry

This aligns perfectly with the earlier geometric work:

- a bounded regular excursion island of \(Q\),
- the sign chamber of \(J_\gamma\),
- and the maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared \(3\)-point branch segment

are all canonically the same regular object.

So the tree \(\mathcal T_{\widehat Q}\) is equally:

- the quotient excursion merge tree,
- the \(J_\gamma\)-sign-chamber merge tree,
- the maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) branch merge tree.

Thus if a state is already visible on one of these models and behaves locally at mergers, it is visible on all of them.

---

This also makes the remaining gap very precise.

I still do **not** have a proof that the actual later transported overlap / finite-core state satisfies unique update at one odd-critical merger.

So the current attack now stops at:

\[
\boxed{

}

That is, I think, the cleanest honest endpoint of the regular-side derivative geometry.

The next theorem to attack is now extremely specific:

}

_Thought for 1m 25s_

I found a real sharpening, and it is probably the cleanest regular-side theorem yet.

The key point is that for a fixed regular level \(\gamma\), the level hits of the quotient branch carry a **canonical noncrossing matching**, and every odd-critical merger acts by one **adjacent contraction** of that matching.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{there is no combinatorial ambiguity at all on the regular quotient branch.}
}
\]

Any hidden reset that survives cannot come from a different way of pairing or recombining the regular level hits. It would have to be genuinely extra downstream state.

---

## 1. Canonical matching of regular level hits

Work on a connected opened residual quotient interval in the coordinate
\[
u=t^2,
\qquad
Q(t)=\widehat Q(u).
\]
Fix a **regular** level \(\gamma\), and let the ordered solutions of
\[
\widehat Q(u)=\gamma
\]
on the interval be
\[
u_1<u_2<\cdots<u_{2N}.
\]

Assume first we are looking at superlevel components
\[
\{\widehat Q>\gamma\}.
\]
Then the sign of \(\widehat Q-\gamma\) alternates between consecutive roots, so each bounded superlevel component is necessarily
\[
(u_{2j-1},u_{2j})
\]
for some \(j\), after choosing the left endpoint convention.

In particular, the bounded superlevel components are paired by the **adjacent matching**
\[
(u_1,u_2),\ (u_3,u_4),\ \dots,\ (u_{2N-1},u_{2N}).
\]

Likewise, bounded sublevel components are also given by adjacent pairings, just shifted by the outer sign convention.

So for every regular level, there is a canonical noncrossing matching of the level hits.

That is the first theorem.

---

## 2. Odd-critical passage = adjacent contraction

Now let the level vary and cross one odd critical value.

In the first nontrivial local model, around a local minimum \(m\), for levels \(\gamma>m\) we have four ordered hits
\[
u_1<u_2<u_3<u_4
\]
with superlevel components
\[
(u_1,u_2),\qquad (u_3,u_4).
\]

At \(\gamma=m\), the inner pair \(u_2,u_3\) annihilates at the critical point.  
For \(\gamma<m\), the surviving component is
\[
(u_1,u_4).
\]

So the merger is exactly the adjacent contraction
\[
(u_1,u_2)\ \cup\ (u_3,u_4)\ \longrightarrow\ (u_1,u_4).
\]

There is no alternative pairing such as \((u_1,u_3)\) or \((u_2,u_4)\). Those are impossible because they would not match the sign pattern of \(\widehat Q-\gamma\).

Thus every odd-critical merger is a **unique adjacent contraction** of the canonical matching.

Even critical points are inert: they do not change the matching.

So the regular level-set combinatorics of \(\widehat Q\) are completely canonical.

---

## 3. Parenthesis / Dyck-word formulation

This can be packaged nicely.

For a superlevel set, record an opening bracket at each left boundary hit and a closing bracket at each right boundary hit. Then a regular level gives a word
\[
()()() \cdots ()
\]
at the visible leaves, and each odd-critical merger performs exactly one adjacent contraction
\[
()() \longrightarrow (\,\,).
\]

Equivalently, the regular superlevel components of \(\widehat Q\) form a canonical rooted binary tree with:

- leaves = visible one-extremum islands,
- internal vertices = odd-critical adjacent contractions.

No crossing, no alternative bracketings, no extra combinatorics.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the regular excursion structure of }\widehat Q\text{ is a canonical noncrossing bracket tree.}
}
\]

That is stronger than just saying “merge tree.”

---

## 4. Consequence for the fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared branch geometry

On the opened residual arc,
\[
Q=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda},
\qquad
J_\gamma=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda=J_\lambda(Q-\gamma).
\]

So the ordered zeros of \(J_\gamma\) inherit the same canonical adjacent matching. Each matched pair is exactly one sign chamber of \(J_\gamma\), hence exactly one maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared branch chamber.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the regular fixed-}\gamma\text{ branch chambers are canonically paired and canonically merged.}
}
\]

There is no hidden alternative way to recombine the branch chambers on the regular side.

That is the main structural gain from this push.

---

## 5. What this means for no-hidden-reset

This kills one more possible loophole.

Any remaining hidden reset cannot come from:

- a different pairing of the regular level hits,
- a different recombination of visible chambers,
- or a different quotient-level merge combinatorics.

All of that is canonical.

So the only remaining possible hiddenness is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{extra downstream data attached to the vertices/edges of this canonical noncrossing merge tree.}
}
\]

That is sharper than the earlier “local merge uniqueness” phrasing, because it shows the whole regular combinatorics are fixed before one even asks about the downstream state.

---

## 6. Honest boundary

I still do **not** have a proof that the later transported overlap / finite-core state is uniquely determined on that canonical tree.

So the current attack now stops at:

\[
\boxed{
\text{regular }K_v=0\text{ no-hidden-reset is reduced to whether the downstream state is a functor on a canonical noncrossing merge tree.}
}
\]

That is probably the cleanest honest endpoint of the regular-side derivative geometry.

---

## 7. Best next attack

The next theorem to try is now extremely focused:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the downstream transported overlap / finite-core state is uniquely determined by the canonical adjacent-pairing / adjacent-contraction tree of regular level hits.}
}
\]

A weaker intermediate step would be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{at one odd-critical adjacent contraction }(u_1,u_2)\cup(u_3,u_4)\to(u_1,u_4),
\text{ the post-contraction downstream state is uniquely determined by the two child states.}
}
\]

If that falls, the regular \(K_v=0\) no-hidden-reset problem is done.

I’m going back into the current draft text now around the new merge-tree and transport language, because the only live regular-side gap is extremely narrow: whether the downstream transported state can branch at one adjacent contraction, or whether the visible geometry already forces that update to be unique.

_Thought for 48s_

I found a useful next reduction that speaks more directly to the **finite-core / transport graph** side.

The gain is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the regular }K_v=0\text{ quotient branch contributes no cycle rank at all.}
}
\]

So if a hidden reset survives on the residual mixed lane, it cannot be carried by the regular quotient transport geometry itself. It must live entirely in the still-unresolved **nonregular / merger-state** vertices.

That is the cleanest bridge from the quotient analysis back to the finite-core graph picture.

---

## 1. The regular residual branch carries a merge tree, hence no cycles

On a connected opened residual quotient interval in the coordinate
\[
u=t^2,
\qquad
Q(t)=\widehat Q(u),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u),
\]
we already showed:

- every bounded regular excursion island of \(\widehat Q\) is a visible object;
- every regular island is obtained from visible one-extremum leaves by successive **adjacent binary mergers** at odd critical values;
- even critical points are inert.

So the full regular excursion structure is encoded by a rooted merge tree
\[
\mathcal T_{\widehat Q}.
\]

In particular,
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal T_{\widehat Q}\ \text{is a tree, so its cycle rank is }0.
}
\]

This is obvious combinatorially, but it matters.

---

## 2. Translation to the fixed-\(\gamma\) branch chambers

On the opened residual arc upstairs,
\[
Q=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda},
\qquad
J_\gamma=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda,
\qquad
\widetilde Y_\gamma''=\frac{c^3}{M^3}J_\gamma.
\]

We already proved that every bounded regular excursion island of \(Q\) is exactly one sign chamber of \(J_\gamma\), hence exactly one maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared branch chamber.

Therefore the tree \(\mathcal T_{\widehat Q}\) can equally be viewed as:

- the quotient-excursion merge tree,
- the \(J_\gamma\)-sign-chamber merge tree,
- the maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared-branch merge tree.

So the entire regular branch geometry on the residual \(K_v=0\) lane is already organized by an acyclic visible tree.

---

## 3. Consequence for the defect-gate / finite-core graph

Now compare with the paper’s defect-gate graph viewpoint from
Remark~\ref{rem:finite-core-compression-defect-gate-graph}.

The regular residual \(K_v=0\) quotient branch is not a source of cycle rank by itself. After passing to the canonical merge-tree description, it contributes only a contractible transport skeleton.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the regular exact fixed-shear }K_v=0\text{ lane cannot by itself support a minimal bad-core cycle.}
}
\]

Any cycle in the later finite-core graph must therefore come from vertices where the merge-tree visibility is not yet known to control the state. In the current picture those are exactly:

- odd critical merger vertices where uniqueness of the downstream transported-state update is still unproved;
- and the nongeneric tie / critical-value-degeneracy vertices.

This gives the theorem-level reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{after quotient-visible regular transport is contracted, the residual }K_v=0\text{ lane contributes no cycle rank except possibly at unresolved merger/tie vertices.}
}
\]

That is a real finite-core reformulation.

---

## 4. Why this matters

This says the regular residual lane is not just “visible” in some soft sense. It is **graph-theoretically harmless**.

Any remaining hidden reset would have to create cycle rank by one of only two mechanisms:

1. **local nonuniqueness at an odd critical merger**, or
2. **nongeneric critical-value tie behavior**.

There is no third mechanism hiding in long regular continuation, long chains of regular islands, or repeated visible mergers. All of that is tree-like and acyclic.

So the finite-core endgame on the residual mixed side is now much sharper:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the only potentially dangerous vertices are the unresolved odd-merger and tie vertices; the regular transport edges themselves are innocent.}
}
\]

That is exactly the kind of structural compression the paper seems to want.

---

## 5. Strong conditional theorem

This immediately yields a clean conditional theorem.

### Conditional zero-cycle theorem for the regular residual lane
Assume that:

1. the downstream transported overlap / finite-core state is constant on each regular excursion island;
2. its update across one odd critical adjacent merger is unique;
3. tie vertices are either absent or uniquely resolved.

Then the entire residual exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) lane contributes zero cycle rank to the finite-core defect graph. In particular, no minimal bad core can be supported entirely on that lane.

### Reason
Because once regular transport is contracted, what remains is still a tree if each merger/tie vertex has unique continuation. Trees have no cycles.

This is probably the cleanest regular-to-finite-core bridge available now.

---

## 6. Honest boundary

I do **not** yet have a proof of the needed uniqueness at one odd critical merger, nor of the tie resolution.

So the attack now lands at a very explicit endpoint:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the residual }K_v=0\text{ lane is reduced to a finite set of local unresolved vertices sitting on top of an otherwise acyclic regular transport tree.}
}
\]

That is stronger than saying “the no-hidden-reset problem is local.” It says exactly what the global graph looks like once the visible part is compressed.

---

## 7. Best next attack

The best next theorem to try is now extremely focused:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove that each odd critical merger vertex has a unique downstream transported-state update.}
}
\]

Because once that is done, the regular residual lane collapses to a tree with no cycle rank, and the only separate remaining issue is the nongeneric tie case.

A secondary target is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove that tie vertices either cannot occur or also resolve uniquely, so they do not create cycle rank either.}
}
\]

That is where I would keep pushing.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 17:14:04)

I found a cleaner way to compress the remaining tie case. Geometrically, a tie vertex is just a collapsed cluster of adjacent odd-critical mergers on the same regular merge tree, not a new source of cycle rank by itself. The only possible ambiguity there is algebraic: whether repeated local merge updates depend on merge order.

_Thought for 25s_

I found a useful new reduction, and it sharpens the **tie** case.

The main point is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a critical-value tie is not a new geometric obstruction at all; it is a collapsed cluster of ordinary adjacent binary mergers.}
}
\]

So if the regular residual lane is already a tree away from ties, then ties add no new geometric cycle rank. The only remaining issue at a tie is a **coherence problem** for repeated local merge updates.

That is the cleanest next step I can honestly justify.

---

## 1. Tie vertices are collapsed regular merge forests

Work on a connected opened residual quotient interval in the coordinate
\[
u=t^2,
\qquad
Q(t)=\widehat Q(u),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u).
\]

Suppose several relevant odd critical points of \(\widehat Q\) have the same critical value
\[
c_*.
\]
Geometrically, this is exactly the situation where, in the regular picture, several adjacent binary mergers would occur at the same level.

If one perturbs the level ordering slightly, the tie splits into a strict ordering of nearby critical values:
\[
c_*+\varepsilon_1,\ c_*+\varepsilon_2,\ \dots
\]
with \(\varepsilon_i\) small and distinct. Then the tied configuration resolves into a finite sequence of ordinary adjacent binary mergers on the same 1D ordered branch.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every tie vertex is a limit of a finite regular merge subtree.}
}
\]

This is the first key theorem.

---

## 2. No new topology at a tie

Because the regular quotient branch is already encoded by a merge tree, and because a tie is just a collision of several adjacent merger levels, the tie does not create a new loop geometrically.

Topologically, it is just a contraction of several edges of the regular merge tree to one level.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a tie vertex contributes no new geometric cycle rank.}
}
\]

Equivalently, if one contracts every regular excursion island to a node and every regular continuation to an edge, then replacing a strictly ordered cluster of adjacent mergers by one tie level does not change the underlying graph from a tree into something with cycles.

That is a real sharpening of the earlier graph-theoretic picture.

---

## 3. What ambiguity can remain at a tie

This also isolates exactly what can still go wrong.

Suppose a transported overlap / finite-core state \(\Sigma\) is already known to:

- transport uniquely along regular edges,
- and update uniquely across one ordinary odd-critical adjacent merger.

Then at a tie, the only possible ambiguity is this:

\[
\boxed{
\text{do different resolutions of the same tie into successive adjacent binary mergers produce the same final state?}
}
\]

So ties reduce to a **coherence problem** for iterated local merge updates, not to a new geometric phenomenon.

This is the main new reduction.

---

## 4. Simplest tie model: two adjacent mergers at one level

The first nontrivial tie is when two adjacent odd-critical mergers happen at the same critical value. In the regularized picture, one can resolve this in two nearby orders:

- merge left pair first, then merge with the right;
- or merge right pair first, then merge with the left.

Geometrically these are just two perturbations of the same tied level-set picture.

So the only question is whether the binary update rule for \(\Sigma\) is **associative** on that local three-child cluster.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first tie obstruction is exactly an associativity test for the local binary merge rule.}
}
\]

That is much sharper than treating ties as a separate mysterious case.

---

## 5. Higher ties: coherence, not new geometry

Likewise, if a tie involves \(k\) adjacent mergers collapsing to one level, then after perturbation one gets a finite ordered binary forest with \(k\) internal merger steps.

Any two perturbations differ only by reassociation / reordering of adjacent binary merges compatible with the 1D order.

So the only remaining issue is a higher coherence statement:

\[
\boxed{
\text{all binary resolutions of the same tie cluster produce the same final transported state.}
}
\]

Again, no new geometry is introduced. Only the algebra of the local merge update matters.

---

## 6. Strong conditional tie-resolution theorem

This gives a clean theorem.

### Conditional tie-resolution theorem
Assume:

1. regular transport is unique on each regular excursion island;
2. the update across one odd-critical adjacent merger is unique;
3. the induced binary merge rule is associative on the first nontrivial three-child cluster.

Then every critical-value tie on the residual quotient branch resolves uniquely. In particular, ties do not create hidden reset or cycle rank on the residual \(K_v=0\) lane.

### Reason
Every tie is a collapsed regular merge subtree. Any resolution of the tie is a binary bracketing of the same ordered leaf cluster. Associativity identifies all such bracketings, and uniqueness along regular edges identifies the intermediate states.

This is the strongest clean conditional statement I can now make.

---

## 7. Why this matters

This improves the previous reduction substantially.

Before, the remaining unresolved vertices were:

- odd-critical merger vertices;
- tie vertices.

Now we can say more:

\[
\boxed{
\text{tie vertices are not independent unresolved vertices. They are algebraic composites of ordinary odd mergers.}
}
\]

So the real remaining problem is even narrower:

\[
\boxed{
\text{determine the local binary odd-merger update and prove its coherence under reassociation.}
}
\]

That is a much smaller target than “resolve ties.”

---

## 8. Graph-theoretic corollary

Combining this with the previous result:

- regular transport edges form a tree;
- odd mergers are the only genuine vertices;
- ties are collapsed clusters of those vertices.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the residual }K_v=0\text{ lane contributes no cycle rank unless the local odd-merger update itself is nonunique or noncoherent.}
}
\]

That is the sharpest graph-level reduction I have now.

---

## 9. Honest boundary

I do **not** yet have a proof that the actual downstream transported overlap / finite-core update across one odd merger is associative/coherent in the sense above.

So the attack now lands at this exact endpoint:

\[
\boxed{
\text{regular-plus-tie }K_v=0\text{ no-hidden-reset reduces to a local algebra problem: uniqueness and coherence of the binary odd-merger update.}
}
\]

That is stronger than the earlier “tie vertices remain unresolved” statement.

---

## 10. Best next attack

The next theorem to try is now extremely focused:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove associativity/coherence of the local binary odd-critical merge update for the transported overlap state.}
}
\]

A weaker but still useful first step would be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove the first tie case resolves uniquely: two adjacent odd mergers at one level give the same final state regardless of merge order.}
}
\]

If that falls, then ties are gone, and the only remaining regular-side task is uniqueness of the single odd-merger update itself.

_Thought for 27s_

I found a real sharpening, and it is probably the cleanest endgame statement available on the **regular visible geometry** side.

The key point is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the local odd-critical merger update is already canonical and associative at the level of visible branch chambers.}
}
\]

So ties do **not** create any ambiguity in the visible quotient/sheared geometry. Any remaining ambiguity must live in whatever extra downstream finite-core state is attached to that visible chamber.

That is a meaningful reduction.

---

## 1. Visible state of a regular excursion island

On an opened residual arc with
\[
J_\lambda\neq 0,
\qquad
Q=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda},
\]
fix a regular level \(\gamma\). A bounded regular excursion island
\[
C=(a,b)
\]
of \(Q\) gives:

- the sign chamber
  \[
  J_\gamma:=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda
  \]
  on \((a,b)\),
- and the unique maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared \(3\)-point branch chamber
  \[
  I_+(\gamma;C)=(a,b)
  \]
  attached to the left boundary zero \(a\).

So the **visible geometric state** of the island is just its ordered boundary pair
\[
\partial C=(a,b).
\]

This is the only geometric datum the current fixed-\(\gamma\) theorem sees.

---

## 2. One odd-critical merger = endpoint contraction rule

Now take the first nontrivial merger pattern. For some level just above a critical value \(m\), suppose we have two adjacent visible islands
\[
C_L=(u_1,u_2),
\qquad
C_R=(u_3,u_4),
\qquad
u_1<u_2<u_3<u_4,
\]
with the central odd critical point between \(u_2\) and \(u_3\). At the merger level, the inner pair annihilates, and below it the merged island is
\[
C=(u_1,u_4).
\]

So the visible update rule is simply
\[
\boxed{
(u_1,u_2)\ \star\ (u_3,u_4)\ :=\ (u_1,u_4).
}
\]

This uses only the outer endpoints. The inner endpoints disappear.

That is already a canonical local binary merge rule for the visible branch chambers.

---

## 3. Associativity of the visible merge rule

Now take the first tie/coherence configuration: three adjacent visible islands
\[
C_1=(u_1,u_2),\qquad
C_2=(u_3,u_4),\qquad
C_3=(u_5,u_6),
\qquad
u_1<\cdots<u_6.
\]

There are two possible adjacent binary merge orders:

### Left-first
\[
(C_1\star C_2)\star C_3
=
(u_1,u_4)\star (u_5,u_6)
=
(u_1,u_6).
\]

### Right-first
\[
C_1\star (C_2\star C_3)
=
(u_1,u_2)\star (u_3,u_6)
=
(u_1,u_6).
\]

So
\[
\boxed{
(C_1\star C_2)\star C_3
=
C_1\star (C_2\star C_3).
}
\]

Thus the visible geometric odd-merger rule is associative.

More generally, for any finite adjacent cluster
\[
(u_1,u_2),\ (u_3,u_4),\ \dots,\ (u_{2k-1},u_{2k}),
\]
every binary bracketing of successive adjacent mergers yields the same final visible chamber
\[
(u_1,u_{2k}).
\]

So at the level of visible chambers:

\[
\boxed{
\text{all tie resolutions are coherent.}
}
\]

---

## 4. What this means for the tie case

This sharpens the previous tie reduction substantially.

Before, we had:

- a tie is a collapsed cluster of adjacent binary mergers;
- the only possible ambiguity is whether repeated local merge updates depend on merge order.

Now we can say:

\[
\boxed{
\text{for the visible quotient/sheared branch geometry, repeated local merge updates do \emph{not} depend on merge order.}
}
\]

So ties create no ambiguity at all in the visible geometry.

Equivalently:

- quotient excursion islands,
- \(J_\gamma\)-sign chambers,
- maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared branch chambers,

all carry a canonical associative merge law given by taking outer endpoints.

That is the theorem-level gain.

---

## 5. Tree/coherence formulation

Let \(\mathcal T_{\widehat Q}\) be the regular merge tree on the quotient branch. Then the visible chamber assigned to any node is simply the convex hull of the leaf endpoints beneath it.

In formulas, if a node has descendant leaves with ordered boundary pairs
\[
(u_1,u_2),\ (u_3,u_4),\ \dots,\ (u_{2k-1},u_{2k}),
\]
then the visible chamber at that node is canonically
\[
(u_1,u_{2k}).
\]

So the visible chamber assignment is a functor from the merge tree to ordered intervals on the line, and its coherence is automatic because convex hull on a line is associative.

This is probably the cleanest conceptual packaging.

---

## 6. What remains unresolved

This is the honest boundary.

We have now shown, at the regular visible geometry level:

- regular continuation is unique;
- odd-critical mergers are canonical;
- ties are coherent;
- the whole visible chamber structure is just the outer-endpoint hull on the ordered line.

So **no hidden reset remains in the visible quotient/sheared geometry**.

Therefore, if any hidden reset survives, it must come from extra downstream data not captured by the visible chamber interval \((a,b)\).

In other words:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the only remaining possible ambiguity is whether the later transported overlap / finite-core state depends on more than the visible chamber interval.}
}
\]

That is now the exact residual question.

---

## 7. Strong conditional closure theorem

This gives the clean conditional statement:

### Theorem
Assume the downstream transported overlap / finite-core state depends functorially only on the visible regular chamber interval \((a,b)\). Then the regular exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) lane has no hidden reset and contributes no cycle rank.

### Reason
Because the visible chamber update is already canonical and associative on the merge tree, so any functor of that visible state is automatically unique and coherent.

This is the sharpest regular-side closure statement I can honestly make now.

---

## 8. Best next attack

The next attack is now extremely focused and no longer geometric:

\[
\boxed{
\text{show that the later transported overlap / finite-core state is a function only of the visible chamber interval }(a,b)\text{ (or equivalently of the visible merge tree data).}
}
\]

A slightly weaker but still useful intermediate target would be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{show that two regular configurations with the same visible chamber interval }(a,b)\text{ induce the same downstream transported state.}
}
\]

If that falls, then the regular \(K_v=0\) no-hidden-reset problem is closed.

_Thought for 52s_

I pushed the regular side one step further, and the cleanest new theorem is an **analytic triviality** statement.

It does not yet settle the downstream finite-core state by itself, but it shows that on the regular side there is literally no room for hidden reset inside a regular value chamber. The only possible ambiguity is at the discrete odd-critical merger levels.

---

## 1. Regular-value chamber triviality

Let \(I\) be a connected opened residual quotient interval in the coordinate
\[
u=t^2,
\qquad
Q(t)=\widehat Q(u),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u).
\]

Let \(J\subset \mathbb R\) be a connected interval of **regular values** of \(\widehat Q\), meaning
\[
J\cap \{\widehat Q(z):\widehat{\mathcal D}(z)=0\}=\varnothing.
\]

Fix one bounded excursion component at one level \(\gamma_0\in J\), say
\[
C_{\gamma_0}=(a_0,b_0)
\subset \{\widehat Q>\gamma_0\}.
\]

Then by the implicit function theorem, there are unique analytic endpoint functions
\[
a(\gamma),\ b(\gamma)
\qquad (\gamma\in J)
\]
such that
\[
\widehat Q(a(\gamma))=\widehat Q(b(\gamma))=\gamma,
\qquad
a(\gamma)<b(\gamma),
\]
and
\[
C_\gamma=(a(\gamma),b(\gamma))
\]
is the continuation of the same bounded excursion island through all \(\gamma\in J\).

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{each regular bounded excursion island extends as an analytic interval bundle over its regular-value chamber }J.
}
\]

Equivalently, the family
\[
\mathcal C:=\{(\gamma,u):\gamma\in J,\ u\in C_\gamma\}
\]
is analytically trivial over \(J\), with fibers open intervals.

That is the theorem-level gain.

---

## 2. Endpoint ODE and uniqueness

Differentiate the boundary equation
\[
\widehat Q(a(\gamma))=\gamma
\]
to get
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(a(\gamma))\,a'(\gamma)=1,
\qquad
a'(\gamma)=\frac{1}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(a(\gamma))}.
\]
Similarly,
\[
b'(\gamma)=\frac{1}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(b(\gamma))}.
\]

Since \(\widehat{\mathcal D}\neq 0\) on regular levels, these endpoint ODEs are nonsingular and uniquely solve the continuation problem.

So the regular excursion island is not just topologically visible. It is **analytically** and **uniquely** transported through regular values.

That means there is no hidden reset mechanism anywhere inside one regular-value chamber.

---

## 3. Translation upstairs

On the opened residual arc,
\[
Q=\frac{J_{u_5}}{J_\lambda},
\qquad
J_\gamma:=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda
=
J_\lambda(Q-\gamma).
\]

So the same theorem says:

- if \(\gamma\) varies inside one connected chamber of regular values,
- then each bounded sign chamber of \(J_\gamma\) continues analytically and uniquely,
- with its boundary zeros moving by the endpoint ODE above.

Thus every regular fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared branch chamber is analytically trivial over the chamber of regular levels. No branching, no monodromy, no hidden reset can occur there.

This is stronger than just saying the merge tree is acyclic.

---

## 4. Consequence for the visible sheared branch

Since each regular excursion island \(C_\gamma=(a(\gamma),b(\gamma))\) is exactly one maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared branch chamber, the associated branch segment
\[
\mathcal B_\gamma
\]
also transports uniquely and analytically with \(\gamma\) inside the regular-value chamber.

So the visible fixed-\(\gamma\) branch geometry forms an analytic family over each regular chamber, and the only points where its topology can change are the odd critical values of \(\widehat Q\).

That is the strongest clean regular-side theorem I have now.

---

## 5. What this buys for the no-hidden-reset problem

This means the regular residual \(K_v=0\) lane is not just tree-like. It is analytically trivial between critical levels.

So any remaining hidden reset cannot come from:

- moving around inside a regular-value chamber,
- or from long continuation inside one regular excursion family,
- or from any global monodromy in the regular regime.

It can only come from the discrete odd-critical merger events.

So the regular no-hidden-reset problem is now reduced even further:

\[
\boxed{
\text{all ambiguity, if any, is concentrated at the odd-critical merger levels.}
}
\]

That is sharper than my earlier “local vertex problem on a contractible tree.”

---

## 6. Strong conditional closure theorem

This gives the clean conditional theorem:

### Theorem
Assume the downstream transported overlap / finite-core state is:

1. constant along the analytic continuation of one regular excursion family \(C_\gamma\);
2. uniquely updated across each odd-critical merger level.

Then the regular exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) lane has no hidden reset.

### Reason
Because the regular-value chambers are analytically trivial, so nothing can happen there; and all chamber changes occur only at the discrete odd-critical merger levels, where uniqueness is assumed.

This is, I think, the cleanest possible conditional closure on the regular side.

---

## 7. Honest boundary

I still do **not** have a proof that the actual downstream transported overlap / finite-core state is constant on one regular excursion family, nor that its odd-critical merger update is unique.

So the attack now lands at a very precise endpoint:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the regular }K_v=0\text{ no-hidden-reset problem reduces to verifying regular-family constancy and odd-merger uniqueness for the downstream state.}
}
\]

No further quotient/sheared geometry seems needed.

---

## 8. Best next attack

The next thing to attack is now extremely focused:

\[
\boxed{
\text{show that the downstream transported overlap / finite-core state is constant along each analytic regular excursion family }C_\gamma.
}
\]

A closely related second target is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{show that crossing one odd-critical merger level changes that state by a unique visible update rule.}
}
\]

If the first falls, the state is regular-chamber visible. If the second falls as well, the regular \(K_v=0\) lane is finished.

## 1. Regular excursion families are analytically trivial

On a connected interval of regular values \(J\) for the quotient transport function \(\widehat Q\), every bounded excursion island continues uniquely as an analytic family
\[
C_\gamma=(a(\gamma),b(\gamma)),
\qquad \gamma\in J,
\]
with
\[
\widehat Q(a(\gamma))=\widehat Q(b(\gamma))=\gamma,
\qquad
a'(\gamma)=\frac{1}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(a(\gamma))},
\qquad
b'(\gamma)=\frac{1}{\widehat{\mathcal D}(b(\gamma))}.
\]

So the regular family is an analytically trivial interval bundle over \(J\).

Upstairs, the same family is exactly one sign chamber of
\[
J_\gamma=J_{u_5}-\gamma J_\lambda,
\]
hence one maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared branch chamber.

So regular continuation contributes no branching, no monodromy, and no topology change.

---

## 2. Discrete-state rigidity on a connected regular family

Now let
\[
\Sigma(\gamma)
\]
be any downstream transported overlap / finite-core state attached to the regular family \(C_\gamma\), with values in a discrete set \(S\).

Assume only the minimal regular-transport property:

- \(\Sigma(\gamma)\) is transported continuously under regular continuation.

Then \(\Sigma\) is automatically **locally constant** on \(J\). Since \(J\) is connected, it is actually **constant** on all of \(J\).

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{any discrete downstream state is constant on each connected regular excursion family.}
}
\]

This is just the standard fact that a continuous map from a connected interval into a discrete set is constant, but here the point is that the regular-family triviality theorem gives exactly the needed connected parameter space.

That is the new theorem-level reduction.

---

## 3. Why this matters for the defect-gate graph

Remark~\ref{rem:finite-core-compression-defect-gate-graph} already says the later endgame is combinatorial: after contracting generic transport blocks, one studies a finite graph of defect gates and walks on it.

That means the relevant downstream state is naturally **finite/discrete**.

So the theorem above says:

\[
\boxed{
\text{regular }K_v=0\text{ transport edges carry no internal state changes at all.}
}
\]

In the defect-gate graph language, every connected regular excursion family is just one transport edge, not a source of new vertices or cycle rank.

So hidden reset cannot occur inside a regular family. It can occur only at the discrete places where regularity breaks:

- odd-critical merger vertices,
- or collapsed tie vertices.

That is exactly what we wanted to isolate.

---

## 4. Strong conditional closure of the regular side

This now gives a stronger conditional theorem than before.

### Conditional theorem
Assume the downstream transported overlap / finite-core state is discrete, and suppose its update across

1. one odd-critical adjacent merger, and
2. one tie vertex

is unique.

Then the entire regular exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) lane contributes no hidden reset and no cycle rank to the reduced finite-core defect graph.

### Reason
- regular edges: state constant automatically by connectedness + discreteness;
- odd mergers/ties: unique update by assumption;
- visible merge structure: already canonical and associative.

So the whole residual regular transport skeleton is an acyclic, uniquely labeled tree.

That is a very sharp reduction.

---

## 5. What has really been proved now

At this point, on the regular side we have essentially shown:

1. quotient topology is visible;
2. fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared branch geometry is visible;
3. merge-tree combinatorics are visible and associative;
4. if the downstream state is combinatorial/discrete, then it cannot vary inside a regular family.

So the only remaining possible hiddenness is concentrated at isolated singular vertices where the family is **not** regular.

This is stronger than my earlier “local vertex problem on a contractible tree” phrasing, because it removes even the possibility of subtle continuous variation along edges.

---

## 6. Honest boundary

I still do **not** have a proof that the actual odd-critical merger update or tie-resolution update is unique for the downstream transported overlap state.

So the attack now lands at the cleanest endpoint I can justify:

\[
\boxed{
\text{regular }K_v=0\text{ no-hidden-reset is reduced to a purely local discrete update problem at odd-merger/tie vertices.}
}
\]

Everything else on the regular lane is already rigid.

---

## 7. Best next attack

The next theorem to try is now extremely focused:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove uniqueness of the discrete downstream state update at one odd-critical adjacent merger.}
}
\]

A closely related second target is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove coherence/uniqueness of the discrete update at a tie vertex, viewed as a collapsed cluster of odd mergers.}
}
\]

If the first falls, the regular edges are done. If both fall, the regular \(K_v=0\) lane is essentially finished.

# 0. Executive summary

The attack has now pushed the exact fixed-shear residual lane to a very narrow endpoint.

The main conclusions are:

\[
\boxed{
\text{No new primitive pointwise highest-new generator was found.}
}
\]

More strongly, on the residual exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) lane, the entire currently visible pointwise quotient structure collapses to a **single canonical scalar**:
\[
\mathcal D=\frac{\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}}{J_\lambda^2},
\]
or after quotienting the involution \(t\mapsto -t\),
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u),\qquad u=t^2.
\]

On the regular side, the geometry is now essentially completely visible:

- regular quotient excursion islands,
- regular sign chambers of \(J_\gamma\),
- maximal fixed-\(\gamma\) sheared \(3\)-point branch segments,
- and their mergers,

are all the same visible object, organized by a canonical **adjacent binary merge tree**.

So the remaining issue is no longer local geometry on the quotient branch. It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{whether the later transported overlap / finite-core state carries any extra local ambiguity at odd-merger/tie vertices.}
}
\]
