## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:25:38)

I’m pushing on the one remaining live branch now: the replicated outer-shell case. Since finite-order one-branch witnesses are blind there, the only plausible next gain is to use the middle negative bridge and derive a cross-run identity that a true residual branch should not be able to satisfy.

_Thought for 1m 23s_

I found a real sharpening, and this one actually bites.

The key point is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{on the strict three-run exact fixed-shear lane, }\Xi\text{-descent is impossible.}
}
\]

So the updated \(\Xi\)-fork collapses completely to the non-descent side:

\[
\boxed{
\text{failure of }\Xi\text{-descent is forced, hence is the first explicit nonlocal transport obstruction.}
}
\]

That is the strongest honest continuation I’ve found since the new draft update.

---

## 1. Start from the current strict three-run setup

As in the updated draft, in the strict excess case
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2,
\]
the two outer runs have a common value interval, and the inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2
\]
define the gap
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]

The current fork in the notes is:

- either \(\Xi\)-descent fails;
- or \(\Xi\)-descent holds and the outer shells rigidify to exact translated replication
  \[
  \Omega_0=\Omega_2,\qquad B_0=B_2,\qquad \phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\equiv C,\qquad \widehat Q(u+C)=\widehat Q(u)\text{ on }R_0.
  \]
【352†source】

In the previous push I sharpened the descent branch to show that descent forces exact outer-window coincidence and full translation replication of the outer runs.

Now combine that with the draft’s normalized three-run window model.

---

## 2. The normalized three-run model rules out exact outer-window coincidence in the strict excess phase

In the standard three-run normalization, the windows are
\[
\Omega_0=(0,B_0),\qquad
\Omega_1=(0,B_1),\qquad
\Omega_2=(B_1-B_2,\ B_1).
\]

This is exactly the model behind the draft’s criterion
\[
\Omega_0\cap\Omega_2=\varnothing
\iff
B_1\ge B_0+B_2,
\]
and the strict excess phase
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2
\]
is the genuinely live quotient-visible case.【351†source】【352†source】

Now suppose the descent branch held. Then, from the previous theorem,
\[
\Omega_0=\Omega_2
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
B_0=B_2.
\]

But equality of the intervals
\[
(0,B_0)=(B_1-B_2,\ B_1)
\]
forces equality of endpoints:
\[
0=B_1-B_2,
\qquad
B_0=B_1.
\]

So
\[
B_1=B_2,
\qquad
B_1=B_0.
\]

Hence
\[
\boxed{
B_0=B_1=B_2.
}
\]

But then strict excess fails, since
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2
\]
would become
\[
B_0>2B_0,
\]
impossible.

So exact outer-window coincidence is incompatible with the strict excess phase.

This is the contradiction.

---

## 3. Main theorem

Therefore:

### Theorem
On a strict three-run exact fixed-shear quotient-visible branch satisfying
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2,
\]
the value-space descent condition for
\[
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
\]
across the two outer runs cannot hold.

### Proof
If \(\Xi\)-descent held, then the earlier inverse-gap analysis forces
\[
\Omega_0=\Omega_2,\qquad B_0=B_2.
\]
But in the normalized three-run model
\[
\Omega_0=(0,B_0),\qquad
\Omega_2=(B_1-B_2,\ B_1),
\]
so \(\Omega_0=\Omega_2\) implies
\[
0=B_1-B_2,\qquad B_0=B_1,
\]
hence
\[
B_0=B_1=B_2.
\]
This contradicts strict excess \(B_1>B_0+B_2\). ∎

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{strict excess }B_1>B_0+B_2
\Longrightarrow
\text{\(\Xi\)-descent fails.}
}
\]

This is the cleanest theorem-level gain from the current frontier.

---

## 4. Consequence for the fixed-shear endgame

The draft’s strict three-run endgame fork is now no longer a genuine fork.

The descent branch is impossible in the live strict-excess regime. So the only remaining branch is:

\[
\boxed{
\Upsilon(q):=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\not\equiv 0
\quad\text{on the common outer value interval.}
}
\]

In words:

\[
\boxed{
\text{failure of }\Xi\text{-descent is forced and is the first explicit nonlocal transport obstruction on the strict three-run lane.}
}
\]

That is exactly the kind of sharpened transport statement the update was asking for.

---

## 5. What this buys conceptually

This is important because it kills the most rigid alternative.

Before this push, the strict three-run lane looked like:

- maybe \(\Xi\) descends, and then one tries to rule out replicated outer shells;
- maybe \(\Xi\) does not descend.

Now the first option is gone.

So the live three-run residual geometry is no longer “some transport issue.” It is specifically:

\[
\boxed{
\text{outer same-value transport with unavoidable }\Xi\text{-mismatch.}
}
\]

That is a much sharper target than the earlier broad transport-language formulations.

---

## 6. Honest boundary

This still does **not** close the lane.

What it does **not** yet prove is that the forced nonzero function
\[
\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\]
has the sign / monotonicity / integral behavior needed to contradict the actual residual branch.

So the remaining problem is now very focused:

\[

\text{exploit the forced non-descent of }\Xi\text{ quantitatively.}
}
\]

But the descent branch itself is dead.

The next attack should now go straight at the mismatch function

\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).

The two best concrete targets are:

or at least

}

That is where I would keep pushing now.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:35:01)

_Thought for 58s_

I found a clean continuation that fits the **new strict three-run / inverse-gap** setup and sharpens the “even-jet mismatch witness” idea in a way that is actually theorem-level.

The key point is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{at any interior minimum of the inverse-branch gap }\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\text{ there is a first \emph{even} reciprocal-slope mismatch.}
}
\]

So the strict three-run lane now has a canonical local witness:

- either the first mismatch is already at order \(2\), i.e. the \(\Xi\)-mismatch
  \[
  \Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
  \]
- or \(\Upsilon\) vanishes at the witness, but a higher even reciprocal-slope jet separates the two outer branches.

That is the strongest honest continuation I can add now.

---

# 1. Reciprocal-slope tower and inverse-branch derivatives

Recall the reciprocal-slope tower from the update notes:
\[
J_1(u)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)},
\qquad
J_{n+1}(u)=J_1(u)\,\partial_u J_n(u).
\]
【352†source】

Let \(\phi\) be any inverse branch of \(\widehat Q\), so
\[
\widehat Q(\phi(q))=q.
\]
Then
\[
\phi'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi(q))}=J_1(\phi(q)).
\]

Now differentiate inductively. If
\[
\phi^{(n)}(q)=J_n(\phi(q)),
\]
then
\[
\phi^{(n+1)}(q)
=
\frac{d}{dq}\bigl(J_n(\phi(q))\bigr)
=
J_n'(\phi(q))\,\phi'(q)
=
J_n'(\phi(q))\,J_1(\phi(q))
=
J_{n+1}(\phi(q)).
\]

So by induction:

\[
\boxed{
\phi^{(n)}(q)=J_n(\phi(q))
\qquad (n\ge 1).
}
\]

This is the basic identity.

---

# 2. Gap-derivative formula

Apply this to the two outer inverse branches \(\phi_0,\phi_2\) on the common outer-value interval \(\mathcal I\), and define
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]

Then for every \(n\ge 1\),
\[
\boxed{
\Delta^{(n)}(q)=J_n(\phi_2(q))-J_n(\phi_0(q)).
}
\]

In particular,

- for \(n=1\):
  \[
  \Delta'(q)=J_1(\phi_2(q))-J_1(\phi_0(q));
  \]
- for \(n=2\):
  \[
  \Delta''(q)=J_2(\phi_2(q))-J_2(\phi_0(q)).
  \]

Since
\[
J_2(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_1(u)
=
-\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
=
-\Xi(u),
\]
we get
\[
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]

So the previously introduced mismatch function
\[
\Upsilon(q):=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\]
is exactly the second derivative of the inverse-gap:
\[
\boxed{
\Upsilon(q)=\Delta''(q).
}
\]

---

# 3. First even-jet witness theorem

Now let \(q_*\in\mathcal I\) be an interior local minimum of \(\Delta\). Since \(\Delta\) is analytic, there exists a least integer \(m\ge 2\) such that
\[
\Delta^{(1)}(q_*)=\Delta^{(2)}(q_*)=\cdots=\Delta^{(m-1)}(q_*)=0,
\qquad
\Delta^{(m)}(q_*)\neq 0.
\]

Because \(q_*\) is a local minimum, the first nonzero derivative must be even and positive. So:

\[
\boxed{
m=2r\ \text{for some }r\ge 1,
\qquad
\Delta^{(2r)}(q_*)>0.
}
\]

Using the gap-derivative formula above, this becomes:

\[
\boxed{
J_k(\phi_2(q_*))=J_k(\phi_0(q_*))
\qquad (1\le k<2r),
}
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
J_{2r}(\phi_2(q_*))-J_{2r}(\phi_0(q_*))>0.
}
\]

So the two outer branches agree through reciprocal-slope order \(2r-1\) and first separate at an even order \(2r\), with positive sign.

This is the main new theorem.

---

# 4. Interpretation

This says the strict three-run same-value witness is not merely a slope coincidence. It always comes with a **finite even-order reciprocal-slope mismatch**.

More concretely:

- if \(r=1\), then
  \[
  \Delta''(q_*)>0,
  \]
  so the witness is already the \(\Xi\)-mismatch
  \[
  \Xi(\phi_0(q_*))-\Xi(\phi_2(q_*))>0;
  \]
- if \(r>1\), then the \(\Xi\)-mismatch vanishes at \(q_*\), but the two branches are still separated by a higher even reciprocal-slope jet.

So the update note’s idea of an “even-jet mismatch witness” is now exact:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every interior minimum of }\Delta\text{ produces a first even reciprocal-slope mismatch }J_{2r}.
}
\]

This is a precise local witness statement, not just a heuristic.

---

# 5. Relation to the \(\Xi\)-fork

This fits very cleanly with the sharpened \(\Xi\)-fork from the current draft state.

We already showed in the previous push that on the strict excess phase
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2,
\]
full \(\Xi\)-descent is impossible, because it would force
\[
\Omega_0=\Omega_2,
\]
which contradicts the normalized three-run geometry. So non-descent of \(\Xi\) is forced globally on the live strict three-run lane.

What the theorem above adds is the local version at a same-value witness:

- if the first mismatch is already at order \(2\), the obstruction is directly visible in \(\Upsilon=\Delta''\);
- if not, the witness persists at some higher even order \(2r\).

So the strict three-run lane now always comes with a finite even-order local obstruction at the witness.

That is stronger than just saying “\(\Xi\)-descent fails somewhere.”

---

# 6. Why this matters

This is useful because it eliminates a possible loophole.

One might worry that the global non-descent of \(\Xi\) could still be too soft: maybe \(\Upsilon\) changes sign, maybe it vanishes at the witness, maybe the mismatch is too diffuse to use.

But at an actual interior minimum of \(\Delta\), that ambiguity disappears. The analytic minimum structure forces a first nonzero even derivative, and that derivative is a concrete reciprocal-slope mismatch:
\[
J_{2r}(\phi_2(q_*))-J_{2r}(\phi_0(q_*))>0.
\]

So any eventual contradiction on the strict three-run lane can be attacked through a very definite finite-order witness at the same-value point.

That is the main conceptual gain.

---

# 7. Honest boundary

This still does **not** close the lane.

What it does **not** yet prove is that the first even mismatch must occur already at order \(2\), or that any higher even-order mismatch is incompatible with the real residual branch.

So the remaining possibilities are:

- an order-2 mismatch \(\Upsilon(q_*)>0\);
- or a higher-order even mismatch with
  \[
  \Upsilon(q_*)=0
  \]
  but
  \[
  J_{2r}(\phi_2(q_*))-J_{2r}(\phi_0(q_*))>0
  \quad\text{for some }r\ge 2.
  \]

Thus the strict three-run branch has now been reduced to a finite-order even-jet mismatch problem, but not yet excluded.

---

# 8. Best next attack

The next best attack is now very focused.

There are two natural directions:

## Direction A: force order \(2\)
Try to show that the first nonzero mismatch at an interior minimum must already be
\[
\Delta''(q_*)=\Upsilon(q_*)>0.
\]

That would reduce the whole lane to a sign theorem for \(\Upsilon\).

## Direction B: exploit higher even jets
If higher-order vanishing is possible, try to show that the package-level / corrected-block structure forbids
\[
J_1,\dots,J_{2r-1}
\]
from agreeing at a same-value witness while
\[
J_{2r}
\]
separates positively.

A concrete theorem target would be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{on the true residual branch, a same-value / same-slope witness cannot have vanishing reciprocal-slope mismatch through order }2r-1.
}
\]

That is where I would keep pushing now.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:36:15)

I found a cleaner way to squeeze the same-value witness: instead of treating the even-jet mismatch as just a first nonzero derivative, you can rewrite it as a signed integral law for
\[
\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))=\Delta''(q).
\]
At an interior minimum of \(\Delta\), \(\Upsilon\) has to satisfy one-sided positivity of all first antiderivatives, and if the first nonzero jet is higher than order \(2\), then \(\Upsilon\) has an even-order flat zero and is locally nonnegative. That gives a more rigid target than just “some mismatch exists.”

_Thought for 27s_

I found a real sharpening of the **same-value witness** analysis, and this one gives a cleaner target than the raw “first even jet mismatch” statement.

The key new point is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{at any interior minimum of }\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\text{ the mismatch }
\Upsilon(q):=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))=\Delta''(q)
\text{ satisfies a one-sided signed antiderivative law.}
}
\]

Equivalently, the strict three-run witness is not just a finite even-order jet mismatch. It is an **integrated positivity witness** for \(\Upsilon\).

That is the strongest honest continuation I can add now.

---

# 1. Setup

On the strict three-run quotient-visible lane, let
\[
\mathcal I=\Omega_0\cap\Omega_2
\]
be the common outer-value interval, and let
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:\mathcal I\to R_0,R_2
\]
be the outer inverse branches. Define
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]

As established already,
\[
\phi_j''(q)=-\Xi(\phi_j(q)),
\qquad
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\]
so
\[
\boxed{
\Upsilon(q):=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))=\Delta''(q).
}
\]

Now let \(q_*\in\mathcal I\) be an interior local minimum of \(\Delta\). Then
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0,
\qquad
\Delta(q)\ge \Delta(q_*)
\quad\text{for }q\text{ near }q_*.
\]

---

# 2. First antiderivative sign law

Integrating \(\Delta''=\Upsilon\) from \(q_*\) gives
\[
\Delta'(q)=\int_{q_*}^{q}\Upsilon(s)\,ds.
\]

Since \(q_*\) is a local minimum of \(\Delta\), we have
\[
\Delta'(q)\le 0 \quad\text{for }q<q_* \text{ near }q_*,
\qquad
\Delta'(q)\ge 0 \quad\text{for }q>q_* \text{ near }q_*.
\]

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{sgn}\!\left(\int_{q_*}^{q}\Upsilon(s)\,ds\right)=\operatorname{sgn}(q-q_*)
\quad\text{for }q\neq q_*\text{ sufficiently near }q_*.
}
\]

Equivalently,

- for \(q>q_*\) near \(q_*\),
  \[
  \int_{q_*}^{q}\Upsilon(s)\,ds\ge 0;
  \]
- for \(q<q_*\) near \(q_*\),
  \[
  \int_{q}^{q_*}\Upsilon(s)\,ds\ge 0.
  \]

So the first antiderivative of \(\Upsilon\) has the sign pattern forced by a local minimum.

This is the first new theorem.

---

# 3. Second antiderivative sign law

Integrating once more,
\[
\Delta(q)-\Delta(q_*)
=
\int_{q_*}^{q}(q-s)\,\Upsilon(s)\,ds.
\]

Since \(q_*\) is a local minimum, the left-hand side is nonnegative. Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\int_{q_*}^{q}(q-s)\,\Upsilon(s)\,ds\ge 0
\quad\text{for }q\text{ near }q_*.
}
\]

This is a weighted positivity law for \(\Upsilon\). In particular:

- for \(q>q_*\),
  \[
  \int_{q_*}^{q}(q-s)\,\Upsilon(s)\,ds\ge 0;
  \]
- for \(q<q_*\),
  \[
  \int_{q}^{q_*}(s-q)\,\Upsilon(s)\,ds\ge 0.
  \]

So the same-value witness forces not just a pointwise jet condition, but a whole family of positive weighted moments of \(\Upsilon\).

That is the second theorem.

---

# 4. Consequence for the first nonzero jet

Now suppose the first nonzero derivative of \(\Delta\) at \(q_*\) occurs at order \(2r\):
\[
\Delta^{(1)}(q_*)=\cdots=\Delta^{(2r-1)}(q_*)=0,
\qquad
\Delta^{(2r)}(q_*)>0.
\]

Then since \(\Upsilon=\Delta''\),
\[
\Upsilon^{(k)}(q_*)=0
\quad (0\le k\le 2r-3),
\qquad
\Upsilon^{(2r-2)}(q_*)=\Delta^{(2r)}(q_*)>0.
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{if the first mismatch occurs at order }2r,\text{ then }\Upsilon
\text{ has an even-order flat zero of order }2r-2\text{ with positive leading coefficient.}
}
\]

In particular:

- if \(r=1\), then
  \[
  \Upsilon(q_*)=\Delta''(q_*)>0;
  \]
- if \(r\ge 2\), then \(\Upsilon(q_*)=0\), but \(\Upsilon\) has a local nonnegative flat contact at \(q_*\).

So even the higher-order case is much more rigid than an arbitrary vanishing.

---

# 5. Local nonnegativity in the higher-order case

From the even-order flat-zero structure just described, if \(r\ge 2\) then the Taylor expansion gives
\[
\Upsilon(q)=\frac{\Delta^{(2r)}(q_*)}{(2r-2)!}(q-q_*)^{2r-2}+O\bigl((q-q_*)^{2r-1}\bigr),
\]
with positive leading coefficient.

Hence:

\[
\boxed{
\text{if }\Upsilon(q_*)=0\text{ at an interior minimum of }\Delta,\text{ then }\Upsilon(q)\ge 0\text{ on a punctured neighborhood of }q_*.
}
\]

So the higher-order mismatch case still forces local nonnegativity of \(\Upsilon\), not oscillation.

That is a useful strengthening.

---

# 6. Reciprocal-slope tower version

From the earlier identity
\[
\Delta^{(n)}(q)=J_n(\phi_2(q))-J_n(\phi_0(q)),
\]
where
\[
J_1(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\qquad
J_{n+1}(u)=J_1(u)\,\partial_u J_n(u),
\]
the same theorem can be rephrased as follows.

At an interior local minimum of \(\Delta\), there is a least \(r\ge 1\) such that

\[
J_k(\phi_2(q_*))=J_k(\phi_0(q_*))
\qquad (1\le k<2r),
\]
but
\[
J_{2r}(\phi_2(q_*))-J_{2r}(\phi_0(q_*))>0.
\]

So the witness is a **first even reciprocal-slope mismatch**, and its sign is forced to be positive.

This packages the local witness in exactly the language suggested by the updated notes.【352†source】

---

# 7. Why this matters

This is better than the earlier “there is some even-jet mismatch” statement, because it gives a *functional* constraint on the first mismatch function \(\Upsilon\).

At a true same-value witness, \(\Upsilon\) cannot behave arbitrarily. It must satisfy:

1. the antiderivative sign law
   \[
   \int_{q_*}^{q}\Upsilon(s)\,ds
   \text{ has the sign of }q-q_*;
   \]
2. the weighted positivity law
   \[
   \int_{q_*}^{q}(q-s)\Upsilon(s)\,ds\ge 0;
   \]
3. and in the higher-order case it must be locally nonnegative.

So the strict three-run lane is now reduced not merely to “non-descent somewhere,” but to a very specific local positivity structure for the mismatch function.

That is a genuinely sharper target for contradiction.

---

# 8. Honest boundary

This still does **not** close the lane.

What it does **not** yet prove is that the actual source-level / package-level origin of the branch forces \(\Upsilon\) to change sign, or vanish too strongly, or violate one of the positivity laws above.

So the remaining job is still to connect these local mismatch constraints to something RH-specific.

But the same-value witness itself is now pinned down as tightly as I can currently make it inside the quotient-visible framework.

---

# 9. Best next attack

The next best derivative-geometry attack is now very focused:

## Option A: sign theorem for \(\Upsilon\)
Show that on the true strict three-run residual branch,
\[
\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\]
cannot stay nonnegative near a same-value witness, contradicting the minimum law.

## Option B: vanishing-order obstruction
Show that the package-level / corrected-block structure forbids \(\Upsilon\) from vanishing to even order with positive leading term at a same-value witness.

A concrete theorem target would be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{on the true residual branch, }\Upsilon\text{ cannot have the local positivity profile forced by an interior minimum of }\Delta.
}
\]

That is where I would keep pushing now.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:40:32)

_Thought for 1m 1s_

I found an important correction, and it gives a cleaner theorem-level picture of the strict three-run witness.

The correction is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the interior same-value / same-slope witness for }\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\text{ is generically a local \emph{maximum}, not a minimum.}
}
\]

That changes the sign of the first even mismatch, and it sharpens the live target on the \(\Xi\)-lane. The inverse-branch-gap formulation and the \(\Xi\)-fork are exactly the right framework for this.【351†source】【352†source】

## 1. Endpoint behavior of the inverse-gap derivative

In the strict excess case
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2,
\]
the two outer windows overlap on an open interval
\[
\mathcal I=\Omega_0\cap\Omega_2.
\]
Let
\[
\phi_0:\mathcal I\to R_0,\qquad \phi_2:\mathcal I\to R_2
\]
be the outer inverse branches, and define
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]

On the strict three-run branch, the two outer runs have the same sign of \(\widehat Q'\) (after orienting so that both are positive), so
\[
\Delta'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}.
\]

Write
\[
q_-:=\inf\mathcal I,\qquad q_+:=\sup\mathcal I.
\]

In the standard three-run normalization,
\[
\Omega_0=(0,B_0),\qquad \Omega_2=(B_1-B_2,B_1),
\]
so
\[
\mathcal I=(B_1-B_2,\ B_0).
\]

Then as \(q\downarrow q_-\),

- \(\phi_2(q)\) approaches the left endpoint of \(R_2\), where \(\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))\to 0^+\),
- \(\phi_0(q)\) stays in the interior of \(R_0\), where \(\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))\) stays finite positive.

Hence
\[
\boxed{
\lim_{q\downarrow q_-}\Delta'(q)=+\infty.
}
\]

Likewise, as \(q\uparrow q_+\),

- \(\phi_0(q)\) approaches the right endpoint of \(R_0\), where \(\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))\to 0^+\),
- \(\phi_2(q)\) stays interior in \(R_2\), where \(\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))\) stays finite positive.

Hence
\[
\boxed{
\lim_{q\uparrow q_+}\Delta'(q)=-\infty.
}
\]

This is the first useful theorem.

## 2. Existence and type of the same-value witness

From the endpoint behavior,
\[
\Delta'(q)
\]
must vanish at least once on \(\mathcal I\). So the same-value / same-slope witness exists automatically on any strict three-run branch:

\[
\boxed{
\exists\,q_*\in\mathcal I\text{ with }\Delta'(q_*)=0.
}
\]

More importantly, because \(\Delta'\) enters \(+\infty\) at the left end and exits \(-\infty\) at the right end, the generic crossing is from \(+\) to \(-\), so the critical point is a local maximum of \(\Delta\), not a local minimum.

Thus the earlier “minimum” picture should be corrected to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the natural interior witness is a local maximum of }\Delta.
}
\]

## 3. Correct sign of the first even mismatch

Define
\[
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\qquad
\Upsilon(q):=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]

As before,
\[
\phi_j''(q)=-\Xi(\phi_j(q)),
\]
so
\[
\boxed{
\Delta''(q)=\Upsilon(q).
}
\]

Now let \(q_*\in\mathcal I\) be an interior local maximum of \(\Delta\). Since \(\Delta\) is analytic, there is a least integer \(r\ge 1\) such that
\[
\Delta^{(k)}(q_*)=0\quad(1\le k<2r),
\qquad
\Delta^{(2r)}(q_*)<0.
\]

So the first nonzero derivative at the witness is again even, but now with **negative** sign.

Equivalently, in the reciprocal-slope tower
\[
J_1(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},\qquad
J_{n+1}(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_n(u),
\]
using
\[
\Delta^{(n)}(q)=J_n(\phi_2(q))-J_n(\phi_0(q)),
\]
we get:

\[
\boxed{
J_k(\phi_2(q_*))=J_k(\phi_0(q_*))
\quad(1\le k<2r),
}
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
J_{2r}(\phi_2(q_*))-J_{2r}(\phi_0(q_*))<0.
}
\]

So the first even reciprocal-slope mismatch at the witness has negative sign.

That is the corrected theorem.

## 4. Local sign law for \(\Upsilon\)

In the simple case \(r=1\), the witness is nondegenerate and
\[
\Delta''(q_*)=\Upsilon(q_*)<0.
\]

More generally, if \(r\ge 2\), then \(\Upsilon(q_*)=0\), but its first nonzero derivative occurs at order \(2r-2\) with negative sign. So near \(q_*\),
\[
\boxed{
\Upsilon(q)\le 0
}
\]
on a punctured neighborhood of \(q_*\).

Thus the local same-value witness forces \(\Upsilon\) to have a **local nonpositive profile**, not a nonnegative one.

This is the corrected local positivity/negativity law.

## 5. Antiderivative sign laws

Since
\[
\Delta''=\Upsilon,
\]
integrating from \(q_*\) gives
\[
\Delta'(q)=\int_{q_*}^{q}\Upsilon(s)\,ds.
\]

Because \(q_*\) is a local maximum, we have
\[
\Delta'(q)\ge 0 \text{ for } q<q_* \text{ near }q_*,
\qquad
\Delta'(q)\le 0 \text{ for } q>q_* \text{ near }q_*.
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{sgn}\!\left(\int_{q_*}^{q}\Upsilon(s)\,ds\right)=-\operatorname{sgn}(q-q_*)
}
\]
for \(q\neq q_*\) sufficiently near \(q_*\).

And integrating once more,
\[
\Delta(q)-\Delta(q_*)
=
\int_{q_*}^{q}(q-s)\Upsilon(s)\,ds\le 0
\]
near \(q_*\), because \(q_*\) is a local maximum.

So the witness forces the weighted sign law
\[
\boxed{
\int_{q_*}^{q}(q-s)\Upsilon(s)\,ds\le 0
}
\]
for \(q\) near \(q_*\).

This is the integral form of the same local maximum theorem.

## 6. Consequence for the \(\Xi\)-fork

This sharpens the strict three-run endgame a lot.

We already had the updated fork:

- if \(\Xi\)-descent held, the outer shells would rigidify to exact translated replication;
- otherwise, failure of \(\Xi\)-descent is the first explicit nonlocal transport obstruction.【352†source】

From the previous push, strict excess rules out full \(\Xi\)-descent. So non-descent is forced globally.

What the present result adds is the **local shape** of that forced non-descent:

\[
\boxed{
\text{at an actual same-value witness, the mismatch }\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\text{ must have a local nonpositive maximum-profile witness.}
}
\]

So the live strict three-run problem is now not merely “\(\Upsilon\neq 0\) somewhere.” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{can the true residual branch support a same-value witness at which }\Upsilon\text{ has the forced local nonpositive profile?}
}
\]

That is a much sharper target.

## 7. Honest boundary

This still does not close the lane. I do not yet have a theorem saying that the actual source/package structure forces \(\Upsilon\) to be positive, or sign-changing in the wrong way, or too flat/nonflat to match the witness law.

So the remaining problem is now very focused:

\[
\boxed{
\text{exploit the forced local nonpositive profile of }\Upsilon\text{ at the witness.}
}
\]

## 8. Best next attack

The best next attack is now one of these two:

First, try to prove a sign theorem:
\[
\boxed{
\text{on the true strict three-run residual branch, }\Upsilon(q)\text{ cannot be locally nonpositive at a same-value witness.}
}
\]

Second, if the sign is too hard, try a vanishing-order obstruction:
\[
\boxed{
\text{the package-level / corrected-block structure forbids }\Upsilon\text{ from having the even-order negative-contact profile forced at a local maximum of }\Delta.
}
\]

A concrete theorem target would be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{on the true residual branch, the local Taylor profile of }\Upsilon\text{ at a same-value witness is incompatible with the inverse-gap maximum law.}
}
\]

That is where I would keep pushing now.
## User (2026-04-22 01:44:58)

Here’s a brief note you can share with all agents:

---

Team — one important draft correction: there was a sign reversal in the downstream strict three-run fixed-shear narrative, and it has now been corrected.

The correct trichotomy is
[
B_0+B_2>B_1 \quad\text{open outer overlap,}
]
[
B_0+B_2=B_1 \quad\text{touching,}
]
[
B_1>B_0+B_2 \quad\text{separation.}
]

So the inverse-branch-gap / (\Delta) / (\Xi) transport setup belongs only to the actual overlap regime
[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
]
not to the separation regime
[
B_1>B_0+B_2.
]

In particular, any argument that starts from outer inverse branches (\phi_0,\phi_2), an overlap interval (\Omega_0\cap\Omega_2), or a gap function
[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)
]
should now be understood as conditional on genuine outer overlap. The transport identities themselves may still be fine once overlap exists; what was wrong was the regime identification.

Practical consequence: if you are using the strict three-run lane, please make sure you are not treating
[
B_1>B_0+B_2
]
as the “live overlap” case. That is the separation case. The live overlap case is
[
B_0+B_2>B_1.
]

This does not damage the broader transport endgame, but it does mean the (\Delta/\Xi) story must be phrased as an overlap-regime analysis, not as something triggered by the opposite inequality.

---

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:45:50)

_Thought for 49s_

Good correction. Taking that as the new regime split, the attack sharpens nicely.

The main repair is:

\[
\boxed{
B_0+B_2>B_1 \text{ is the genuine outer-overlap regime,}
\qquad
B_1=B_0+B_2 \text{ is touching,}
\qquad
B_1>B_0+B_2 \text{ is separation.}
}
\]

So everything involving outer inverse branches \(\phi_0,\phi_2\), the overlap interval \(\Omega_0\cap\Omega_2\), and the gap
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)
\]
belongs only to the regime
\[
\boxed{B_0+B_2>B_1.}
\]

With that fixed, here is the sharpened attack.

---

## 1. In the true overlap regime, the same-value / same-slope witness exists automatically

In the normalized three-run model
\[
\Omega_0=(0,B_0),\qquad
\Omega_1=(0,B_1),\qquad
\Omega_2=(B_1-B_2,\ B_1),
\]
strict outer overlap means
\[
B_1-B_2 < B_0
\iff
B_0+B_2>B_1.
\]

So the common outer-value interval is
\[
\mathcal I=(B_1-B_2,\ B_0).
\]

Let
\[
\phi_0:\mathcal I\to R_0,\qquad
\phi_2:\mathcal I\to R_2
\]
be the outer inverse branches, and define
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]

Then
\[
\Delta'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}.
\]

Because \(q\downarrow B_1-B_2\) pushes \(\phi_2(q)\) to the left endpoint of \(R_2\) while \(\phi_0(q)\) stays interior, and \(q\uparrow B_0\) pushes \(\phi_0(q)\) to the right endpoint of \(R_0\) while \(\phi_2(q)\) stays interior, we get

\[
\lim_{q\downarrow(B_1-B_2)}\Delta'(q)=+\infty,
\qquad
\lim_{q\uparrow B_0}\Delta'(q)=-\infty.
\]

Hence:

\[
\boxed{
\text{in every strict outer-overlap branch, }\Delta'\text{ vanishes at least once in the interior.}
}
\]

So the same-value / same-slope witness is not an extra hypothesis. It is automatic in the actual live overlap regime.

---

## 2. The witness is generically a local maximum

Because \(\Delta'\) enters from \(+\infty\) on the left and exits to \(-\infty\) on the right, the natural interior witness \(q_*\) is a local maximum of \(\Delta\), not a minimum.

Thus, if \(q_*\) is such a witness, the first nonzero derivative of \(\Delta\) at \(q_*\) is even and negative:
\[
\Delta^{(1)}(q_*)=\cdots=\Delta^{(2r-1)}(q_*)=0,
\qquad
\Delta^{(2r)}(q_*)<0
\]
for some \(r\ge 1\).

That gives the corrected local witness law.

---

## 3. Reciprocal-slope tower formulation is now exactly in the right regime

Define
\[
J_1(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},\qquad
J_{n+1}(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_n(u).
\]

Then for inverse branches,
\[
\phi_j^{(n)}(q)=J_n(\phi_j(q)),
\]
so
\[
\Delta^{(n)}(q)=J_n(\phi_2(q))-J_n(\phi_0(q)).
\]

At the witness \(q_*\), this becomes:

\[
\boxed{
J_k(\phi_2(q_*))=J_k(\phi_0(q_*))
\quad (1\le k<2r),
}
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
J_{2r}(\phi_2(q_*))-J_{2r}(\phi_0(q_*))<0.
}
\]

So the updated note about an even-jet / reciprocal-slope-tower mismatch witness is exactly right, once placed in the correct overlap regime.

---

## 4. The \(\Xi\)-mismatch is the order-2 witness

Since
\[
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\qquad
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
\]
define
\[
\Upsilon(q):=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]

Then
\[
\Upsilon(q)=\Delta''(q).
\]

So:

- if \(r=1\), then the witness already occurs at order \(2\), and
  \[
  \boxed{\Upsilon(q_*)<0;}
  \]
- if \(r\ge 2\), then \(\Upsilon(q_*)=0\), but \(\Upsilon\) has an even-order flat zero with negative leading term, so locally
  \[
  \boxed{\Upsilon(q)\le 0}
  \]
  on a punctured neighborhood of \(q_*\).

Thus the live overlap regime forces a local **nonpositive** \(\Xi\)-mismatch profile at the same-value witness.

That is now the clean local transport target.

---

## 5. The descent branch survives only on the equal-amplitude locus

Now repair the earlier overstatement about \(\Xi\)-descent.

If \(\Xi\) descends across the outer runs on the common interval, then as before \(\Delta''=0\), so \(\Delta\) is affine. Endpoint blow-up still forces exact outer-window coincidence:
\[
\Omega_0=\Omega_2.
\]

But now, in the corrected overlap regime, this no longer contradicts the phase itself. Instead, from
\[
(0,B_0)=(B_1-B_2,\ B_1),
\]
we get equality of endpoints:
\[
0=B_1-B_2,\qquad B_0=B_1,
\]
hence
\[
\boxed{
B_0=B_1=B_2.
}
\]

So the corrected conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\Xi\text{-descent in the strict outer-overlap regime is possible only on the codimension-two equal-amplitude locus }B_0=B_1=B_2.
}
\]

That is a real sharpening.

So away from the equal-amplitude locus, non-descent of \(\Xi\) is forced.

---

## 6. On the equal-amplitude locus, descent rigidifies all the way to replicated outer shells

Still under descent, we already know that exact outer-window coincidence forces
\[
\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\equiv C,
\qquad
R_2=R_0+C,
\qquad
\widehat Q(u+C)=\widehat Q(u)\text{ on }R_0.
\]

So the corrected fork is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either } \Xi \text{ does not descend (the generic overlap case),}
}
\]
or
\[
\boxed{
B_0=B_1=B_2
\text{ and the outer runs are exact translates with identical value profile.}
}
\]

That is better than the old statement, because it shows the descent branch lives only on a very thin sublocus.

---

## 7. Finite-order one-branch witnesses are blind on the replicated branch

This remains true and is still useful.

On the replicated branch,
\[
\widehat Q(u+C)=\widehat Q(u)\text{ on }R_0,
\]
so every finite-jet invariant of \(\widehat Q\) agrees on corresponding points of the two outer runs:
\[
F(\phi_2(q))=F(\phi_0(q)).
\]

In particular the whole reciprocal-slope tower
\[
J_n(\phi_2(q))=J_n(\phi_0(q))
\]
is blind there.

So the equal-amplitude descent branch cannot be killed by any finite-order single-branch value-space differential witness. If that branch is to die, it has to die by something involving the middle negative bridge, the distinguished center, or package/provenance data.

---

## 8. The real live strict-overlap endgame is now very sharp

Putting it all together, the actual live overlap regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1
\]
has become:

### Generic overlap
\[
(B_0,B_1,B_2)\neq (B,B,B).
\]
Then \(\Xi\)-descent is impossible, so \(\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))\) is forced nonzero somewhere, and at the same-value witness it has the local nonpositive profile above.

### Exceptional equal-amplitude overlap
\[
B_0=B_1=B_2.
\]
Then descent is not yet ruled out. If it holds, the branch rigidifies to exact translated outer shells, and finite-order one-branch witnesses go blind.

That is the corrected and sharpened endgame picture.

---

## 9. Best next attack

There are now two very focused targets.

First, on the **generic overlap** branch, attack the sign profile of
\[
\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]
A natural theorem target is:
\[
\boxed{
\text{on the true residual branch, }\Upsilon\text{ cannot have the local nonpositive profile forced at a same-value witness.}
}
\]

Second, on the **equal-amplitude replicated-shell** branch, attack the middle negative bridge, since the outer runs are already exhausted. A natural target is:
\[
\boxed{
\text{the true residual branch cannot support an equal-amplitude three-run shell with exact translated outer runs and an admissible middle bridge.}
}
\]

That is the sharpest corrected attack line now.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:48:20)

_Thought for 1m 3s_

Good correction. With the regime fixed, there is a cleaner and stronger attack on the live strict three-run lane.

The main new point is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{in the genuine outer-overlap regime }B_0+B_2>B_1,\ \Xi\text{-descent is impossible for a simple endpoint reason.}
}
\]

So the \(\Xi\)-fork collapses immediately to the non-descent side, and one gets explicit negative singular asymptotics for the mismatch
\[
\Upsilon(q):=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))=\Delta''(q)
\]
at both ends of the overlap interval. This is sharper than the earlier “window coincidence” contradiction, and it sits exactly in the corrected overlap regime.【351†source】【352†source】

## 1. Correct overlap regime and inverse branches

In the corrected three-run normalization,
\[
\Omega_0=(0,B_0),\qquad
\Omega_1=(0,B_1),\qquad
\Omega_2=(B_1-B_2,\ B_1).
\]

The genuine outer-overlap regime is
\[
\boxed{
B_0+B_2>B_1,
}
\]
so the common outer-value interval is
\[
\mathcal I=(q_-,q_+):=(B_1-B_2,\ B_0).
\]

Let
\[
\phi_0:\mathcal I\to R_0,\qquad \phi_2:\mathcal I\to R_2
\]
be the two outer inverse branches, and define
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]

Then
\[
\Delta'(q)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}.
\]

As noted in the corrected note, this inverse-branch-gap setup belongs only to the overlap regime, not to the separation regime.【351†source】

## 2. Endpoint asymptotics of the inverse-gap derivative

At the left endpoint \(q_-=B_1-B_2\), the point \(q_-\) is the left endpoint of \(\Omega_2\) but lies in the interior of \(\Omega_0\). So:

- \(\phi_2(q)\) approaches the left endpoint \(s_2\) of \(R_2\), where \(\widehat Q'(s_2)=0\) and \(\widehat Q''(s_2)>0\);
- \(\phi_0(q)\) stays away from the endpoints of \(R_0\), hence remains analytic and bounded there.

By the standard inverse-square-root expansion at a simple critical value,
\[
\phi_2(q)=s_2+c_-(q-q_-)^{1/2}+O(q-q_-),
\qquad c_->0,
\]
so
\[
\phi_2'(q)=\frac{c_-}{2}(q-q_-)^{-1/2}+O(1).
\]

Since \(\phi_0'\) stays bounded, we get
\[
\boxed{
\Delta'(q)=A_-(q-q_-)^{-1/2}+O(1)
\quad\text{as }q\downarrow q_-,
}
\]
with \(A_->0\). In particular,
\[
\boxed{
\Delta'(q)\to +\infty\quad\text{as }q\downarrow q_-.
}
\]

Similarly, at the right endpoint \(q_+=B_0\), the point \(q_+\) is the right endpoint of \(\Omega_0\) but lies in the interior of \(\Omega_2\). So:

- \(\phi_0(q)\) approaches the right endpoint \(s_1\) of \(R_0\), where \(\widehat Q'(s_1)=0\) and \(\widehat Q''(s_1)<0\);
- \(\phi_2(q)\) stays interior and analytic.

Again by the inverse-square-root expansion,
\[
\phi_0(q)=s_1-c_+(q_+-q)^{1/2}+O(q_+-q),
\qquad c_+>0,
\]
so
\[
\phi_0'(q)=\frac{c_+}{2}(q_+-q)^{-1/2}+O(1).
\]

Hence
\[
\boxed{
\Delta'(q)=-A_+(q_+-q)^{-1/2}+O(1)
\quad\text{as }q\uparrow q_+,
}
\]
with \(A_+>0\). Therefore
\[
\boxed{
\Delta'(q)\to -\infty\quad\text{as }q\uparrow q_+.
}
\]

This immediately shows the same-value / same-slope witness exists in the true live regime.

## 3. A direct proof that \(\Xi\)-descent is impossible in the overlap regime

Recall
\[
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\qquad
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]

If \(\Xi\) descended to value space across the two outer runs, then
\[
\Xi(\phi_0(q))=\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\qquad(q\in\mathcal I),
\]
so
\[
\Delta''(q)=0
\]
on \(\mathcal I\). Hence \(\Delta'\) would be constant and finite on \(\mathcal I\).

But the endpoint asymptotics above show
\[
\Delta'(q)\to +\infty \text{ as }q\downarrow q_-,
\qquad
\Delta'(q)\to -\infty \text{ as }q\uparrow q_+,
\]
contradiction.

So we get the clean theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\text{on the genuine strict outer-overlap regime }B_0+B_2>B_1,\ \Xi\text{-descent is impossible.}
}
\]

This is the cleanest formulation of the transport obstruction currently available.

## 4. Endpoint asymptotics for the mismatch \(\Upsilon=\Delta''\)

Differentiate the endpoint asymptotics once more.

From
\[
\Delta'(q)=A_-(q-q_-)^{-1/2}+O(1),
\]
we get
\[
\boxed{
\Upsilon(q)=\Delta''(q)
=
-\frac{A_-}{2}(q-q_-)^{-3/2}+O(1)
\quad\text{as }q\downarrow q_-,
}
\]
so in particular
\[
\boxed{
\Upsilon(q)\to -\infty
\quad\text{as }q\downarrow q_-.
}
\]

Likewise from
\[
\Delta'(q)=-A_+(q_+-q)^{-1/2}+O(1),
\]
we obtain
\[
\boxed{
\Upsilon(q)=\Delta''(q)
=
-\frac{A_+}{2}(q_+-q)^{-3/2}+O(1)
\quad\text{as }q\uparrow q_+,
}
\]
hence
\[
\boxed{
\Upsilon(q)\to -\infty
\quad\text{as }q\uparrow q_+.
}
\]

So the mismatch function is not just nonzero somewhere. It is forced to have **negative endpoint singularities on both sides** of the overlap interval.

This is a strong new structural fact.

## 5. Consequence for the interior witness

Because
\[
\Delta'(q)\to +\infty \text{ on the left},\qquad
\Delta'(q)\to -\infty \text{ on the right},
\]
there exists at least one interior \(q_*\in\mathcal I\) with
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0.
\]

The natural such witness is a local maximum of \(\Delta\). Therefore the first nonzero derivative of \(\Delta\) at \(q_*\) is even and negative:
\[
\Delta^{(2r)}(q_*)<0
\]
for some \(r\ge 1\), with all lower derivatives vanishing.

Equivalently, in the reciprocal-slope tower
\[
J_1(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},\qquad
J_{n+1}(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_n(u),
\]
since
\[
\Delta^{(n)}(q)=J_n(\phi_2(q))-J_n(\phi_0(q)),
\]
we have
\[
J_k(\phi_2(q_*))=J_k(\phi_0(q_*))
\quad(1\le k<2r),
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
J_{2r}(\phi_2(q_*))-J_{2r}(\phi_0(q_*))<0.
}
\]

So the local same-value witness always comes with a first even reciprocal-slope mismatch of negative sign.

## 6. Integral sign law for \(\Upsilon\)

Since
\[
\Upsilon=\Delta'',
\]
integrating from a local maximum \(q_*\) gives
\[
\Delta'(q)=\int_{q_*}^{q}\Upsilon(s)\,ds.
\]

Because \(q_*\) is a local maximum,
\[
\Delta'(q)\ge 0 \text{ for }q<q_*,\qquad
\Delta'(q)\le 0 \text{ for }q>q_*.
\]

Thus
\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{sgn}\!\left(\int_{q_*}^{q}\Upsilon(s)\,ds\right)=-\operatorname{sgn}(q-q_*)
}
\]
for \(q\) near \(q_*\).

And integrating once more,
\[
\Delta(q)-\Delta(q_*)
=
\int_{q_*}^{q}(q-s)\Upsilon(s)\,ds\le 0
\]
near \(q_*\).

So at a true same-value witness, \(\Upsilon\) must satisfy:

- negative endpoint blowup on both sides of the overlap interval;
- a local maximum sign law at the witness;
- and the weighted integral inequality above.

This is now a very concrete target.

## 7. What this changes strategically

The previous descent branch is dead in the actual overlap regime. So the strict three-run endgame is no longer a fork in practice.

It is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{outer same-value transport with forced non-descent of }\Xi,\text{ and a mismatch function }\Upsilon
\text{ that is negative-singular at both overlap endpoints and locally nonpositive at the witness.}
}
\]

That is much sharper than the earlier “some transport obstruction” language.

## 8. Honest boundary

This still does not close the lane. What remains unproved is an RH-specific statement saying that the actual residual source/package structure cannot support a function \(\Upsilon\) with exactly that profile.

So the live problem is now very focused:

\[
\boxed{
\text{show that on the true residual branch, }\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\text{ cannot have the forced endpoint-singular / witness-nonpositive profile.}
}
\]

## 9. Best next attack

The best next attack is now one of these.

First, prove a sign obstruction:
\[
\boxed{
\text{on the true residual branch, }\Upsilon\text{ cannot stay negative near both overlap endpoints and near a same-value witness.}
}
\]

Second, if sign is too hard, prove a vanishing-order obstruction:
\[
\boxed{
\text{the corrected-block / package structure forbids the even-order negative-contact profile of }\Upsilon\text{ at a same-value witness.}
}
\]

A concrete theorem target is:
\[
\boxed{
\text{the true residual branch cannot support a mismatch function }\Upsilon
\text{ with the endpoint asymptotics and local maximum law forced by strict outer overlap.}
}
\]

That is where I would keep pushing now.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:50:48)

_Thought for 1m 4s_

I found a real structural reduction on the corrected **strict outer-overlap** lane.

The new point is a clean dichotomy for the mismatch function
\[
\Upsilon(q):=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))=\Delta''(q),
\qquad
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
on the actual overlap interval
\[
\mathcal I=(B_1-B_2,\ B_0),
\qquad
B_0+B_2>B_1.
\]

The theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either }\Upsilon\le 0\text{ on all of }\mathcal I,
\text{ in which case }\Delta\text{ is strictly concave and the same-value/same-slope witness is unique;}
}
\]
or
\[
\boxed{
\Upsilon>0\text{ somewhere, and then the non-descent is concentrated in one or more compact positive “transport pockets” bracketed by zeros of }\Upsilon.
}
\]

So the live overlap problem is no longer “some transport obstruction.” It has reduced to a very sharp **concave-witness vs. positive-pocket** dichotomy.

---

## 1. Endpoint behavior on the true overlap interval

In the corrected regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
the overlap interval is
\[
\mathcal I=(q_-,q_+)=(B_1-B_2,\ B_0).
\]

As already established, on \(\mathcal I\) one has
\[
\Delta'(q)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\]
with endpoint asymptotics
\[
\Delta'(q)\to +\infty \quad (q\downarrow q_-),
\qquad
\Delta'(q)\to -\infty \quad (q\uparrow q_+).
\]

Differentiating gives
\[
\Upsilon(q)=\Delta''(q),
\]
and the inverse-square-root endpoint expansions imply
\[
\boxed{
\Upsilon(q)\to -\infty
\quad\text{as }q\downarrow q_-,
\qquad
\Upsilon(q)\to -\infty
\quad\text{as }q\uparrow q_+.
}
\]

So the mismatch is forced to be strongly negative at both ends of the overlap interval.

---

## 2. Concavity/transport-pocket dichotomy

Because \(\Upsilon\) is analytic on \(\mathcal I\) and tends to \(-\infty\) at both endpoints, exactly one of the following holds.

### Case A: global nonpositivity
\[
\Upsilon(q)\le 0
\qquad(q\in\mathcal I).
\]

Then
\[
\Delta''(q)\le 0
\]
on \(\mathcal I\), so \(\Delta\) is concave on the whole overlap interval.

Since
\[
\Delta'(q)\to +\infty\ \text{at the left end},\qquad
\Delta'(q)\to -\infty\ \text{at the right end},
\]
the derivative \(\Delta'\) is strictly decreasing from \(+\infty\) to \(-\infty\). Therefore it crosses \(0\) exactly once.

So in this case:

\[
\boxed{
\text{\(\Delta\) has a unique global maximum }q_*\in\mathcal I,\text{ hence a unique same-value/same-slope witness.}
}
\]

This is the **concave witness** regime.

### Case B: positive pocket(s)
\[
\Upsilon(q)>0
\]
somewhere on \(\mathcal I\).

Because \(\Upsilon\to-\infty\) at both ends, the positive set
\[
\{q\in\mathcal I:\Upsilon(q)>0\}
\]
is a finite union of bounded open intervals
\[
(a_1,b_1)\cup\cdots\cup(a_m,b_m)
\]
compactly contained in \(\mathcal I\), and each endpoint satisfies
\[
\Upsilon(a_j)=\Upsilon(b_j)=0.
\]

So every departure from the global concave picture is localized inside one or more **positive transport pockets**.

This is the second half of the dichotomy.

---

## 3. Precise theorem

Putting the two cases together:

### Theorem (strict-overlap transport-pocket dichotomy)
On the corrected strict outer-overlap lane
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
let
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\qquad
\Upsilon(q)=\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]

Then:

1. \(\Upsilon(q)\to-\infty\) at both ends of the overlap interval \(\mathcal I\);
2. either \(\Upsilon\le 0\) on \(\mathcal I\), in which case \(\Delta\) is concave and has a unique global maximum witness;
3. or \(\Upsilon>0\) on a nonempty compactly supported union of bounded open intervals in \(\mathcal I\), each bracketed by zeros of \(\Upsilon\).

So the strict-overlap transport problem is completely reduced to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either a single concave same-value witness, or one or more positive \(\Upsilon\)-pockets.}
}
\]

This is the main new theorem.

---

## 4. Why this is useful

This is a real sharpening because it splits the live lane into two very concrete subproblems.

### Concave-witness regime
If
\[
\Upsilon\le 0
\]
globally, then there is only one witness, and it is a global maximum of \(\Delta\). There is no further complexity from oscillation, multiple witnesses, or higher transport pockets.

So any contradiction there has to kill one very rigid configuration:
\[
\text{one overlap interval, one concave gap profile, one unique witness.}
\]

### Positive-pocket regime
If
\[
\Upsilon>0
\]
somewhere, then the non-descent is localized in finite compact pockets. So any contradiction there can focus on those pockets alone, rather than the entire overlap interval.

That is much sharper than the earlier “\(\Xi\)-descent fails somewhere” formulation.

---

## 5. Relation to the witness jet law

At any witness \(q_*\) one still has the local maximum law from the previous push:

- the first nonzero derivative of \(\Delta\) at \(q_*\) is even and negative;
- equivalently the first nonzero reciprocal-slope mismatch
  \[
  J_{2r}(\phi_2(q_*))-J_{2r}(\phi_0(q_*))
  \]
  is negative.

What the present theorem adds is the global envelope around that local statement:

- if \(\Upsilon\le 0\), then the order-2 witness already controls the whole overlap interval;
- if not, then the higher complexity is confined to positive pockets of \(\Upsilon\).

So the jet witness and the transport-pocket decomposition fit together cleanly.

---

## 6. Honest boundary

This still does **not** close the lane.

What remains unproved is either of the following RH-specific statements:

1. that the true residual branch forces
   \[
   \Upsilon\le 0
   \]
   on the whole overlap interval, and then one can kill the unique concave witness;

2. or that positive pockets of \(\Upsilon\) are impossible.

So the live strict-overlap problem is now genuinely narrow.

---

## 7. Best next attack

The next attack should go one of two ways.

### Option A: kill the concave witness
Try to prove:
\[
\boxed{
\text{on the true residual branch, the unique-concave-witness profile for }\Delta\text{ is impossible.}
}
\]

That would settle the whole \(\Upsilon\le 0\) regime.

### Option B: kill positive pockets
Try to prove:
\[
\boxed{
\text{on the true residual branch, }\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))\text{ cannot become positive on a compact interior interval.}
}
\]

That would force global concavity and reduce everything to Option A.

A concrete theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the true residual branch cannot support either a positive \(\Upsilon\)-pocket or a global concave witness profile on the strict overlap interval.}
}
\]

That is where I would keep pushing now.

## User (2026-04-22 01:54:28)

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:55:46)

_Thought for 3m 19s_

I found a useful sharpening of the corrected **strict outer-overlap** lane.

The new point is that the live transport problem can be reduced to a very concrete **pocket-mass resurrection criterion** for
\[
\Upsilon(q):=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))=\Delta''(q),
\qquad
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
on the actual overlap interval
\[
\mathcal I=(q_-,q_+)=(B_1-B_2,\ B_0),
\qquad
B_0+B_2>B_1.
\]

This gives a clean finite-combinatorial reduction of the witness structure.

---

## 1. Basic endpoint facts on the true overlap interval

On the corrected live regime \(B_0+B_2>B_1\), we have outer inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:\mathcal I\to R_0,R_2
\]
and
\[
\Delta'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}.
\]

From the endpoint expansions already established:

\[
\Delta'(q)\to +\infty \quad (q\downarrow q_-),
\qquad
\Delta'(q)\to -\infty \quad (q\uparrow q_+),
\]
and
\[
\Upsilon(q)=\Delta''(q)\to -\infty
\quad\text{at both endpoints }q_\pm.
\]

So \(\Delta'\) starts very positive, ends very negative, and \(\Upsilon\) is strongly negative near both ends.

---

## 2. Positive pockets

Let
\[
P:=\{q\in\mathcal I:\Upsilon(q)>0\}.
\]

Because \(\Upsilon\) is analytic on \(\mathcal I\) and tends to \(-\infty\) at both ends, \(P\) is a finite disjoint union of bounded open intervals:
\[
P=(a_1,b_1)\sqcup \cdots \sqcup (a_m,b_m),
\qquad
q_-<a_1<b_1<\cdots<a_m<b_m<q_+,
\]
and
\[
\Upsilon(a_j)=\Upsilon(b_j)=0.
\]

I’ll call these the **positive transport pockets**.

This was implicit before, but now we can make it quantitative.

---

## 3. Pocket mass and resurrection

For each pocket \((a_j,b_j)\), define its **transport mass**
\[
M_j:=\int_{a_j}^{b_j}\Upsilon(s)\,ds >0.
\]

Since \(\Delta''=\Upsilon\), the change of \(\Delta'\) across the pocket is exactly
\[
\Delta'(b_j)-\Delta'(a_j)=M_j.
\]

So a pocket raises the slope gap \(\Delta'\) by exactly its positive mass.

This is the key operational fact.

---

## 4. First zero and deficit

Because \(\Delta'(q)\to +\infty\) at the left endpoint and \(\Delta'(q)\to -\infty\) at the right endpoint, \(\Delta'\) has at least one zero. Let
\[
z_1:=\inf\{q\in\mathcal I:\Delta'(q)=0\}
\]
be the first zero from the left.

After \(z_1\), the function \(\Delta'\) is negative immediately to the right unless \(z_1\) is degenerate. The only way to get **more** zeros later is for some positive pocket to drive \(\Delta'\) back up through \(0\).

So for each pocket \((a_j,b_j)\) lying to the right of the first zero, define the incoming negative deficit
\[
D_j:= -\Delta'(a_j)\ge 0.
\]

Then:

\[
\Delta'(b_j)=M_j-D_j.
\]

Hence:

- if \(M_j\le D_j\), then \(\Delta'(b_j)\le 0\): the pocket does not resurrect positivity;
- if \(M_j>D_j\), then \(\Delta'(b_j)>0\): the pocket **resurrects** \(\Delta'\) to positive values.

This gives the exact criterion.

---

## 5. Resurrection criterion for extra witnesses

### Theorem (pocket resurrection criterion)
On the strict overlap interval \(\mathcal I\), a positive pocket \((a_j,b_j)\) creates additional same-value / same-slope witnesses if and only if its mass exceeds the incoming negative deficit:
\[
\boxed{
M_j > D_j.
}
\]

### Proof
Since \(\Delta'(b_j)-\Delta'(a_j)=M_j\), we have
\[
\Delta'(b_j)=M_j-D_j.
\]
If \(M_j>D_j\), then \(\Delta'(a_j)<0<\Delta'(b_j)\), so by continuity \(\Delta'\) has a zero inside the pocket. Since \(\Delta'(q)\to -\infty\) at the far right endpoint, it must later cross zero again after the pocket. Thus one resurrection pocket creates an extra pair of witnesses.

If \(M_j\le D_j\), then \(\Delta'(b_j)\le 0\), so the pocket does not restore positivity; no new pair is forced there. ∎

This is the main new theorem.

---

## 6. Corollaries

### Corollary 1: Concave case
If there are no positive pockets, i.e.
\[
\Upsilon\le 0 \text{ on }\mathcal I,
\]
then \(\Delta'\) is strictly decreasing from \(+\infty\) to \(-\infty\). So there is exactly one witness.

This recovers the earlier concave-witness regime.

### Corollary 2: Nonuniqueness requires resurrection
If \(\Delta'\) has more than one zero, then there exists at least one resurrection pocket:
\[
\boxed{
\exists j \text{ such that } M_j>D_j.
}
\]

So multiple witnesses are not just caused by “\(\Upsilon>0\) somewhere.” They require enough integrated positive mass to overcome the accumulated negative deficit.

That is much sharper.

### Corollary 3: Odd number of witnesses
Because \(\Delta'\to +\infty\) on the left and \(\Delta'\to -\infty\) on the right, the total number of zeros of \(\Delta'\) is always odd. Each successful resurrection pocket contributes two extra zeros beyond the first one.

So the witness count has the form
\[
1+2N_{\mathrm{res}},
\]
where \(N_{\mathrm{res}}\) counts the number of successful resurrection events.

This is a useful discrete reduction.

---

## 7. Minimal nonconcave case

The first genuinely nonconcave possibility is now very concrete:

- one positive pocket \((a,b)\),
- with mass \(M\),
- entered with deficit \(D\).

Then there are only two possibilities:

- if \(M\le D\), still exactly one witness;
- if \(M>D\), exactly three witnesses in the minimal generic case.

So the first nonconcave branch is a **one-pocket tri-witness configuration**.

That is probably the cleanest next subcase to attack.

---

## 8. Why this matters

This improves the previous “concave witness vs. positive pockets” dichotomy substantially.

Before, positive pockets were only a qualitative alternative. Now they come with an exact quantitative criterion:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a positive pocket matters only if its integrated }\Upsilon\text{-mass exceeds the incoming negative deficit of }\Delta'.}
\]

So the live strict-overlap problem has been reduced to one of two very sharp subproblems:

1. **global concavity**: \(\Upsilon\le 0\), one unique witness;
2. **resurrection pockets**: positive pockets exist, and at least one has enough mass to revive \(\Delta'\).

This is the cleanest transport reduction I’ve gotten so far.

---

## 9. Honest boundary

This still does **not** close the lane.

What I do not yet have is an RH-specific statement that forbids either:

- the unique concave witness regime, or
- resurrection pockets with \(M_j>D_j\).

So the remaining task is now extremely narrow: attack either the sign of \(\Upsilon\) or the pocket masses.

---

## 10. Best next attack

There are now two very concrete next targets.

### Target A: kill resurrection pockets
Try to prove that on the true residual branch, every positive pocket has too little mass:
\[
\boxed{
M_j\le D_j \quad\text{for every positive pocket }(a_j,b_j).
}
\]

That would reduce the whole lane to the unique concave witness case.

### Target B: kill the unique concave witness
Assume \(\Upsilon\le 0\) on all of \(\mathcal I\), so \(\Delta\) has a unique global maximum witness, and show that this profile is impossible for the true residual branch.

A very concrete theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the true residual branch cannot support either a resurrection pocket }M_j>D_j\text{ or the resulting unique-concave-witness profile.}
}
\]

That is where I would keep pushing now.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:58:05)

_Thought for 56s_

I found a useful new reduction on the corrected **strict outer-overlap** lane.

It sharpens the earlier “pocket mass” picture into a **finite witness-count theorem**.

The key point is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{if }\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))=\Delta''(q)
\text{ has }m\text{ positive pockets on the overlap interval, then }\Delta'(q)
\text{ has at most }2m+1\text{ zeros.}
}
\]

Equivalently, the number of same-value / same-slope witnesses is controlled purely by the number of positive transport pockets.

That is a real combinatorial compression of the live overlap problem.

---

## 1. Setup

On the corrected live regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
the outer windows overlap on
\[
\mathcal I=(q_-,q_+)=(B_1-B_2,\ B_0).
\]

Let
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:\mathcal I\to R_0,R_2
\]
be the outer inverse branches, and define
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\qquad
\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))=\Delta''(q).
\]

As already established,
\[
\Delta'(q)\to +\infty \quad (q\downarrow q_-),
\qquad
\Delta'(q)\to -\infty \quad (q\uparrow q_+),
\]
and
\[
\Upsilon(q)\to -\infty
\quad\text{at both endpoints }q_\pm.
\]

Let the positive set of \(\Upsilon\) be
\[
P:=\{q\in\mathcal I:\Upsilon(q)>0\}
=
(a_1,b_1)\sqcup\cdots\sqcup(a_m,b_m),
\]
a finite disjoint union of bounded open intervals, ordered left to right.

These are the positive transport pockets.

---

## 2. Monotonicity decomposition of \(\Delta'\)

Because
\[
\Delta''=\Upsilon,
\]
we have:

- on each pocket \((a_j,b_j)\), \(\Delta'\) is **strictly increasing**;
- on each complementary component of
  \[
  \mathcal I\setminus \bigcup_{j=1}^m (a_j,b_j),
  \]
  the function \(\Delta'\) is **nonincreasing**.

So the overlap interval is decomposed into alternating regions where \(\Delta'\) is monotone one way or the other:

- decrease / flat,
- increase,
- decrease / flat,
- increase,
- …,
- decrease / flat.

This yields a very strong counting consequence.

---

## 3. Witness-count theorem

### Theorem
If \(\Upsilon\) has exactly \(m\) positive pockets on \(\mathcal I\), then the derivative \(\Delta'\) has at most
\[
\boxed{2m+1}
\]
zeros on \(\mathcal I\).

Equivalently, the number of same-value / same-slope witnesses is at most \(2m+1\).

### Proof
Break \(\mathcal I\) into the \(m\) positive pockets and the \(m+1\) complementary regions where \(\Upsilon\le 0\).

- On each complementary region, \(\Delta'\) is nonincreasing, so it can have **at most one** zero there.
- On each positive pocket, \(\Delta'\) is strictly increasing, so it can also have **at most one** zero there.

Therefore the total number of zeros is at most
\[
m+(m+1)=2m+1.
\]

Because \(\Delta'\to+\infty\) on the left and \(\Delta'\to-\infty\) on the right, the total number of zeros is automatically odd. ∎

This is the main new theorem.

---

## 4. Corollaries

### Corollary 1: no positive pockets \(\Rightarrow\) unique witness
If \(m=0\), i.e.
\[
\Upsilon\le 0 \quad\text{on all of }\mathcal I,
\]
then \(\Delta'\) has at most one zero. Since it must have at least one, it has exactly one zero.

So the earlier concave-witness regime is recovered as:
\[
\boxed{
\Upsilon\le 0 \Longrightarrow \text{exactly one same-value/same-slope witness.}
}
\]

### Corollary 2: one positive pocket \(\Rightarrow\) at most three witnesses
If \(m=1\), then
\[
\boxed{
\#\{\Delta'=0\}\le 3.
}
\]

So the first genuinely nonconcave overlap pattern is a **tri-witness** configuration.

### Corollary 3: \(m\) pockets \(\Rightarrow\) odd witness count in \(\{1,3,\dots,2m+1\}\)
Because the total number of zeros is odd,
\[
\boxed{
\#\{\Delta'=0\}\in \{1,3,5,\dots,2m+1\}.
}
\]

So each positive pocket can contribute at most one extra *pair* of witnesses beyond the first one.

---

## 5. Exact role of successful pockets

This refines the earlier pocket-mass criterion.

Recall that for a pocket \((a_j,b_j)\), its mass is
\[
M_j:=\int_{a_j}^{b_j}\Upsilon(s)\,ds>0,
\]
and the incoming negative deficit is
\[
D_j:=-\Delta'(a_j)\ge 0.
\]

Then:
- if \(M_j\le D_j\), the pocket is **unsuccessful** and creates no upward crossing;
- if \(M_j>D_j\), the pocket is **successful** and resurrects \(\Delta'\) to positive values.

So the actual witness count can be written more sharply as
\[
\boxed{
1+2N_{\mathrm{res}},
}
\]
where \(N_{\mathrm{res}}\le m\) is the number of successful resurrection pockets.

This is the cleanest discrete formula so far.

---

## 6. Why this matters

This is useful because it converts the live overlap problem into a finite discrete classification problem.

Before, the pocket picture was only qualitative:
- either global concavity,
- or some positive pockets.

Now we know much more:

- no pockets \(\Rightarrow\) exactly one witness;
- one pocket \(\Rightarrow\) at most three witnesses;
- \(m\) pockets \(\Rightarrow\) at most \(2m+1\) witnesses;
- each extra witness pair must come from a successful resurrection pocket.

So the strict-overlap transport problem is now reduced to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{control the number and success of positive \(\Upsilon\)-pockets.}
}
\]

That is much sharper than “control \(\Upsilon\) somehow.”

---

## 7. Minimal genuinely difficult case

The first genuinely nontrivial overlap configuration is now completely clear:

- exactly one positive pocket,
- and it is successful:
  \[
  M>D.
  \]

Then there are exactly three same-value/same-slope witnesses in the generic case.

So if the unique-witness concave case can be killed, the next live subcase is not arbitrary complexity — it is precisely the **one-pocket tri-witness** branch.

That is a very manageable next frontier.

---

## 8. Honest boundary

This still does **not** close the lane.

What remains unproved is any RH-specific statement that says:

- there are no positive pockets at all;
- or every positive pocket is unsuccessful;
- or the unique concave witness profile is impossible.

So the remaining work is still on the sign/mass behavior of \(\Upsilon\), but now in a very finite combinatorial way.

---

## 9. Best next attack

The next attack should be one of these.

### Attack A: exclude any successful pocket
Try to prove
\[
\boxed{
M_j\le D_j\quad\text{for every positive pocket }(a_j,b_j).
}
\]

That would force the unique-witness concave-type behavior even if \(\Upsilon\) becomes positive somewhere.

### Attack B: kill the one-pocket tri-witness case
Assume there is exactly one positive pocket and it is successful, producing three witnesses, and try to show this is incompatible with the true residual branch.

A sharp theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the true residual branch cannot support a successful positive \(\Upsilon\)-pocket.}
}
\]

If that falls, the whole strict-overlap lane collapses to the unique-witness case.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:59:52)

_Thought for 23s_

I found a real sharpening of the strict-overlap picture.

The useful new point is that the witness set is not just bounded in number by the pocket count. It has a forced **alternating geometry**.

More precisely:

\[
\boxed{
\text{every successful positive \(\Upsilon\)-pocket contributes exactly one local minimum witness of }\Delta,
}
\]
and between any two such minima there is exactly one local maximum witness. So, in the nondegenerate case, the witness pattern is

\[
\text{max},\ \text{min},\ \text{max},\ \dots,\ \text{min},\ \text{max}.
\]

That is a stronger and more geometric reduction than the previous \(2m+1\) upper bound.

---

## 1. Setup

On the corrected live regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
the outer windows overlap on
\[
\mathcal I=(q_-,q_+)=(B_1-B_2,\ B_0).
\]

Let
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:\mathcal I\to R_0,R_2
\]
be the outer inverse branches and define
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\qquad
\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))=\Delta''(q).
\]

As already established:

- \(\Delta'(q)\to+\infty\) as \(q\downarrow q_-\),
- \(\Delta'(q)\to-\infty\) as \(q\uparrow q_+\),
- \(\Upsilon(q)\to-\infty\) at both ends of \(\mathcal I\).

Let the positive set of \(\Upsilon\) be
\[
P=(a_1,b_1)\sqcup\cdots\sqcup(a_m,b_m),
\]
the finite disjoint union of positive pockets.

---

## 2. Zeros of \(\Delta'\) split into maxima and minima by sign of \(\Upsilon\)

This is the first new theorem.

### Theorem
Let \(q_*\in\mathcal I\) satisfy
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0.
\]

Then:

- if \(\Upsilon(q_*)<0\), \(q_*\) is a strict local **maximum** of \(\Delta\);
- if \(\Upsilon(q_*)>0\), \(q_*\) is a strict local **minimum** of \(\Delta\).

More generally, if the first nonzero derivative of \(\Delta\) at \(q_*\) after \(\Delta'\) is of even order and negative, then \(q_*\) is a local maximum; if positive, a local minimum.

### Proof
Since
\[
\Delta''=\Upsilon,
\]
the sign of \(\Upsilon(q_*)\) gives the usual second-derivative test whenever nonzero. The higher-order statement is just the standard analytic extremum criterion. ∎

So the sign of the mismatch \(\Upsilon\) at a witness tells you whether it is a max- or min-type same-value/same-slope coincidence.

That already fits the corrected sign picture from the previous push.

---

## 3. Where maxima and minima can occur

Because \(\Upsilon>0\) exactly on the positive pockets and \(\Upsilon\le 0\) on the complementary regions, we get:

\[
\boxed{
\text{local minima of }\Delta\text{ can occur only inside positive pockets,}
}
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
\text{local maxima of }\Delta\text{ can occur only in the complementary regions where }\Upsilon\le 0.
}
\]

So the pockets are exactly the places where \(\Delta\) is allowed to bend upward enough to create a minimum witness.

This is the right geometric interpretation of the pockets.

---

## 4. Successful pockets create exactly one minimum witness

Now assume a pocket \((a_j,b_j)\) is **successful**, in the sense that its mass exceeds the incoming deficit:
\[
M_j:=\int_{a_j}^{b_j}\Upsilon(s)\,ds > D_j:=-\Delta'(a_j).
\]

Then:

- \(\Delta'(a_j)<0\),
- \(\Delta'(b_j)=M_j-D_j>0\),
- and \(\Delta'\) is strictly increasing on \((a_j,b_j)\) because \(\Upsilon>0\) there.

Therefore \(\Delta'\) crosses zero **exactly once** on that pocket.

Since \(\Upsilon>0\) there, that zero is a strict local minimum of \(\Delta\).

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{each successful pocket contains exactly one minimum witness of }\Delta.
}
\]

This is a strong sharpening of the earlier statement that a successful pocket contributes “an extra pair of witnesses.” It tells you exactly what happens inside the pocket.

---

## 5. Between successive successful pockets there is exactly one maximum witness

Now take two successive successful pockets
\[
(a_j,b_j),\qquad (a_k,b_k),
\qquad b_j<a_k,
\]
with no other successful pocket between them.

By the previous theorem, each pocket contains exactly one local minimum witness of \(\Delta\).

After the first successful pocket, \(\Delta'(b_j)>0\). Before the next successful pocket begins, the incoming deficit condition means \(\Delta'(a_k)<0\). On the intervening complementary region, \(\Upsilon\le 0\), so \(\Delta'\) is nonincreasing.

Hence \(\Delta'\) crosses zero exactly once on that complementary interval, from positive to negative. That zero is a local maximum witness of \(\Delta\).

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{between any two successive successful pockets there is exactly one maximum witness.}
}
\]

Likewise:

- before the first successful pocket, since \(\Delta'(q)\to+\infty\) at the left end and \(\Delta'(a_1)<0\), there is exactly one maximum witness;
- after the last successful pocket, since \(\Delta'(b_m)>0\) and \(\Delta'(q)\to-\infty\) at the right end, there is exactly one maximum witness.

So the entire witness pattern is determined.

---

## 6. Exact witness-count theorem

Putting the previous points together gives the real count.

### Theorem
Assume all witness crossings are nondegenerate. Let \(N_{\mathrm{res}}\) be the number of successful positive pockets. Then:

- \(\Delta\) has exactly \(N_{\mathrm{res}}\) local minima;
- \(\Delta\) has exactly \(N_{\mathrm{res}}+1\) local maxima;
- therefore the total number of same-value / same-slope witnesses is exactly
  \[
  \boxed{
  2N_{\mathrm{res}}+1.
  }
  \]

And their order from left to right is
\[
\boxed{
\text{max},\ \text{min},\ \text{max},\ \dots,\ \text{min},\ \text{max}.
}
\]

This is the main new theorem.

It is stronger than the earlier upper bound \(2m+1\), because it counts witnesses by **successful** pockets, not merely by all positive pockets.

---

## 7. Minimal nonconcave case is now completely rigid

If there is exactly one successful pocket, then:

- there is one local minimum inside that pocket;
- there is one local maximum before it;
- there is one local maximum after it.

So the minimal nonconcave case is not just “at most three” witnesses. It is exactly:

\[
\boxed{
\text{max} \;-\; \text{min} \;-\; \text{max}.
}
\]

That is a very rigid tri-witness picture.

So if the unique concave-witness case is excluded, the first remaining overlap geometry is not arbitrary complexity. It is this one-pocket max–min–max pattern.

---

## 8. Why this matters

This is useful because it turns the live strict-overlap lane into a finite dynamical pattern problem.

We no longer just know that:

- no pockets \(\Rightarrow\) one witness,
- successful pockets can add witness pairs.

We now know the exact *type* of each witness and how they alternate.

So the strict-overlap transport problem is reduced to a sequence of local extrema of \(\Delta\) generated by successful pockets, with all maxima lying in \(\Upsilon\le 0\) zones and all minima lying in \(\Upsilon>0\) pockets.

That is a much more geometric target for contradiction.

---

## 9. Honest boundary

This still does not close the lane.

What remains unproved is any RH-specific statement excluding:

- the unique global maximum case when there are no successful pockets,
- or the max–min–max tri-witness case when there is one successful pocket,
- or more generally the alternating witness pattern for several successful pockets.

So the reduction is now very sharp, but still conditional on understanding the sign/mass behavior of \(\Upsilon\).

---

## 10. Best next attack

The next attack should now focus on the first genuinely difficult nonconcave case:

\[
\boxed{
\text{one successful positive pocket, hence a max–min–max witness pattern.}
}
\]

There are two clean targets.

### Target A
Prove that a successful pocket is impossible:
\[
\boxed{
M_j\le D_j \quad\text{for every positive pocket}.
}
\]

Then the whole lane collapses to the unique-maximum case.

### Target B
Assume the minimal max–min–max pattern and show it is incompatible with the true residual branch.

A very concrete theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the true residual branch cannot support a one-pocket max–min–max witness pattern on the strict overlap interval.}
}
\]

That is where I would keep pushing now.

I’m attacking the narrowed strict-overlap lane directly at the first genuinely difficult case: one successful positive pocket, i.e. the max–min–max pattern. The useful next gain would be to show that the middle minimum forces an impossible return of \(\Delta\) to the same endpoint asymptotics, or else that the pocket mass can’t actually exceed the incoming deficit on a true residual branch.

_Thought for 1m 47s_

I found a real sharpening of the strict-overlap transport picture.

The new point is that the witness set and the positive \(\Upsilon\)-pockets do not just control each other numerically. They **interlace exactly**.

So the live overlap geometry is now a very rigid alternating configuration, not just “some pockets and some witnesses.”

---

## 1. Setup

On the corrected live regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
let
\[
\mathcal I=(q_-,q_+)=(B_1-B_2,\ B_0)
\]
be the common outer-value interval.

Let
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:\mathcal I\to R_0,R_2
\]
be the outer inverse branches, and define
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\qquad
\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))=\Delta''(q).
\]

As already established,

- \(\Delta'(q)\to+\infty\) as \(q\downarrow q_-\),
- \(\Delta'(q)\to-\infty\) as \(q\uparrow q_+\),
- \(\Upsilon(q)\to-\infty\) at both ends.

Let the positive set of \(\Upsilon\) be
\[
P=(a_1,b_1)\sqcup\cdots\sqcup(a_m,b_m),
\qquad
q_-<a_1<b_1<\cdots<a_m<b_m<q_+,
\]
the disjoint union of positive pockets.

Call a pocket \((a_j,b_j)\) **successful** if it resurrects \(\Delta'\) from negative to positive, equivalently if
\[
M_j:=\int_{a_j}^{b_j}\Upsilon(s)\,ds > D_j:=-\Delta'(a_j).
\]

---

## 2. Interlacing theorem

### Theorem (witness–pocket interlacing)
Assume all witness zeros of \(\Delta'\) are nondegenerate. Let
\[
(a_{j_1},b_{j_1}),\dots,(a_{j_N},b_{j_N})
\]
be the successful positive pockets, listed from left to right.

Then \(\Delta'\) has exactly \(2N+1\) zeros
\[
z_0<z_1<\cdots<z_{2N},
\]
and they interlace with the successful pockets as follows:

\[
\boxed{
z_0<a_{j_1}<z_1<b_{j_1}<z_2<a_{j_2}<z_3<b_{j_2}<\cdots<z_{2N-2}<a_{j_N}<z_{2N-1}<b_{j_N}<z_{2N}.
}
\]

Moreover:

- the even-indexed zeros
  \[
  z_0,z_2,\dots,z_{2N}
  \]
  are strict local **maxima** of \(\Delta\);

- the odd-indexed zeros
  \[
  z_1,z_3,\dots,z_{2N-1}
  \]
  are strict local **minima** of \(\Delta\);

- each successful pocket contains exactly one minimum witness, and every maximum witness lies in a complementary region where \(\Upsilon\le 0\).

This is the main new theorem.

---

## 3. Proof sketch

The proof is clean once the earlier reductions are in place.

On each pocket \((a_j,b_j)\), \(\Upsilon>0\), so \(\Delta'\) is strictly increasing. Hence a successful pocket can contain at most one zero, and if it is successful it contains exactly one zero, necessarily a local minimum of \(\Delta\).

On each complementary region of
\[
\mathcal I\setminus \bigcup_j(a_j,b_j),
\]
one has \(\Upsilon\le 0\), so \(\Delta'\) is nonincreasing. Hence each complementary region contains at most one zero, and any such zero is a local maximum of \(\Delta\).

Now start from the left endpoint. Since \(\Delta'(q)\to+\infty\) as \(q\downarrow q_-\), the first zero must occur in the first complementary region before the first successful pocket. That gives \(z_0<a_{j_1}\), and \(z_0\) is a maximum.

Because \((a_{j_1},b_{j_1})\) is successful, \(\Delta'(a_{j_1})<0<\Delta'(b_{j_1})\). Since \(\Delta'\) is strictly increasing there, it has exactly one zero \(z_1\in(a_{j_1},b_{j_1})\), a minimum.

Immediately after \(b_{j_1}\), \(\Delta'>0\). On the next complementary interval, \(\Delta'\) is nonincreasing, and before the next successful pocket it must become negative again. So there is exactly one zero \(z_2\) in that complementary region, hence
\[
b_{j_1}<z_2<a_{j_2}.
\]

Iterating gives the full interlacing pattern. The final zero \(z_{2N}\) lies after the last successful pocket because \(\Delta'(q)\to-\infty\) at the right endpoint. ∎

---

## 4. Consequences

### (a) Exact count from successful pockets
This recovers the earlier count in a stronger form:
\[
\boxed{
\#\{\Delta'=0\}=2N+1,
}
\]
where \(N\) is the number of successful pockets.

So it is not just an upper bound. In the nondegenerate case, the successful pockets determine the witness count exactly.

### (b) Successful pockets are isolated by maxima
Every successful pocket is bracketed by two maxima of \(\Delta\), one on each side, except that the leftmost and rightmost maxima may use the endpoint tails of the overlap interval. So a successful pocket is never “merged” with another witness mechanism.

### (c) The first nonconcave case is completely rigid
If there is exactly one successful pocket, then the whole witness pattern is
\[
\boxed{
\text{max}\;<\;a\;<\;\text{min}\;<\;b\;<\;\text{max}.
}
\]

So the first genuinely nonconcave case is no longer vaguely “three witnesses.” It is an exactly interlaced
\[
\text{max–pocket–min–pocket–max}
\]
configuration.

That is a very rigid subcase.

---

## 5. Why this matters

This sharpens the live overlap problem significantly.

Before, the best reduction was:

- no successful pockets \(\Rightarrow\) unique witness;
- each successful pocket adds a pair of witnesses.

Now we know much more:

- each successful pocket contributes exactly one minimum witness;
- all minima lie inside successful pockets;
- all maxima lie outside them;
- and the whole witness set interlaces rigidly with the successful pockets.

So the strict-overlap lane has been reduced to a very concrete alternating pattern problem, not just a sign/mass problem.

That is the main gain.

---

## 6. The next genuinely difficult case

This makes the first hard case extremely precise.

The first nontrivial nonconcave branch is:

- one successful positive pocket \((a,b)\),
- one maximum \(z_L<a\),
- one minimum \(z_M\in(a,b)\),
- one maximum \(z_R>b\).

So if the unique-witness concave case is excluded, the next live target is exactly this rigid one-pocket interlaced configuration.

That is much more manageable than arbitrary multi-pocket behavior.

---

## 7. Honest boundary

This still does **not** close the lane.

What remains unproved is any RH-specific statement excluding:

- the unique concave-witness profile (\(N=0\));
- or the one-pocket interlaced max–min–max profile (\(N=1\)).

But there is now no ambiguity about the geometry of those cases.

---

The next best attack is now very focused:

Exclude successful pockets entirely:
\[
\boxed{
M_j\le D_j\quad\text{for every positive pocket}.
}
\]

Then only the unique-witness concave case remains.

### Attack B
Assume one successful pocket and attack the rigid interlaced configuration
\[
z_L<a<z_M<b<z_R.
\]

A concrete theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the true residual branch cannot support a one-pocket interlaced max–min–max configuration on the strict overlap interval.}
}
\]

That is where I would keep pushing now.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 02:04:59)

I found a cleaner reduction of the first genuinely hard strict-overlap case.
